The Red Page
Anzacian Verse
KILLED, IN ACTION.
Where the ranges throw their shadows long
before the day’s surrender,
Down a valley ivhere a river used to
tumble to the sea,
On a rising patch of level rest the men who
dared to tender
lAfe and all its sweetness for their love of
liberty.
In a thousand miles of ugly scrubby waste
and desolation, t
Just that little space of level showing open
to the seaj
Nothing there to lend it grandeur (sure, it
needs no decoration)
Save those rows of wooden crosses keep-
ing silent custody.
There’s a band of quiet workers, artless lads
who joked and chatted
Just this morning; note they’re sullen and
they keep their eyes away
From the blanket-hidden body, coat and
shirt all blood-bespattered,
Lying motionless and waiting by the new-
turned heap of clay.
There are records in the office —date of
death and facts pertaining,
Showing name and rank and number and
disposal of the kit—
More or less a business matter, dnd we have
no time for feigning
More than momentary pity for the men
who have been hit.
There's a patient mother gazing on her hopes
so surely shattered
(Hopes and prayers she cherished bravely,
seeking strength to hide her fear),
Boyhood’s dreams and idle memories—
things that never really mattered —-
Lying buried ivhere he’s buried ’heath the
stars all shining clear.
There’s d young wife sorrow-stricken in her
bitter first conception
Of that brief conclusive message, harsh
fulfilment of her dread;
There are tiny lips repeating, with their
childish imperception ,
Simple words that bring her memories
from the boundaries of the dead.
Could the Turk have seen this picture when
his trigger-finger rounded.
Would his sights have blurred a little had
he heard that mother’s prayer?
Could he know some things that she knew,
might his hate have been confounded?
But he only saw his duty, and he did it,
fighting fair.
Just a barren little surface where the grave
mounds rise ungainly,
Monuments and tributes to tlie men who’ve
done their share.
Pain and death, the fruits of battle, and the
crosses tell it plainly,
Short and quick and silent suffering; ivould
to Clod it ended there!
HARRY McCANN.
MARCHING SONG.
Boots , belt, rifle, and pack —
All you’ll need till you come back;
All you’ll doff when you lie down to sleep;
All they’ll take off when they bury you deep.
Boots, belt, rifle, and pack.
Boots that went light down the Suffolk lane
Will shuffle and drag ere they tread it again.
'Nails that rang gay on the cobbled street
Will have pierced through the sock into some-
body’s feet.
Boots, belt, rifle, and pack.
Belt —for water-bottle and sword;
One to save life; the other —O Lord!
’Fore you’ve finished with them, you bet,
One will be dry and the other wet.
Boots, belt, rifle, and pack.
Rifle —the soldier’s only friend —
True, if you treat her well, to the end:
Feed her with five, and the tune she’ll play
Will reach the heart of a Turkish Bey.
Boots, belt, rifle, and pack.
Pack —that holds u-hat a man most wants:
A shirt, an overcoat, socks and pants,
A Bible, a photo-of heart’s desire;
But you’ll throio it away when you charge
—or retire.
Boots, belt, rifle, and pack.
Leather and canvas, steel and wood,
They’ll stand by yon if you’re good;
Keep them oiled and keep them dry,
They’ll see you home safely—by and by.
Boots, belt, rifle, and pack.
C. J.N., from “The Anzac Book.”
Love Letters of an Anzac, by Oliver
Hogue (“Trooper Bluegmn”)—Melrose, 3s.
6d.—is somewhat interesting, since the
author describes the real thing with con-
siderable journalistic facility, though without
much distinction. His letters have not the
realism, or perhaps one should now call it,
the dinkumism, to be found in the letters of
any non-literary Anzac to his cobber or his
tart or his ma; for “Trooper Bluegum”
seems to think it necessary to inform his
girl of military matters in which no nice
Jean, even though she allowed her boy to
call her “Honeybunch.” would have been the
least interested. Either this Anzac had
an inkling that Jean would ultimately pub-
lish his letters, and therefore he made them
as historically complete as possible, or his
“Honeybunch” was a Sydney newspaper.
The alleged love-interest, consisting chiefly
of endearments and “Light of my Life,” could
so easily have been put in afterwards: at
least, these are so unconvincing that they
have the air of sub-editorial afterthoughts.
Purged of this make-believe sentimentality,
the book would pass as war-correspondence
of about f.a.q.

J*
1. Thou shalt not think thy god the only
god: 'worship thine own, and tolerate
them all.
2. Thou shalt, not look upon thy backyard
as the whole of Australia: for thy
land is a. fair land, and much in need
of great thoughts and great deeds.
3. Thou shalt not take the name of lord, or
duke, or sir; but strive earnestly to
be worthy of thine own.
b- Remember the Sabbath Day, to keep it
cheerful.
5. Honor thy father and thy motherj and
don’t forget thy children, that they
may get a fair and square start in
life, and so be brought to honor thee.
6. Thou shalt not forget to order thy next
suit at the Victoria Barracks, where
fit and style are guaranteed.
7. Pitch not a tale of passion to any other
than the woman of thy choice, that
thou mayst escape the brick, of shame
and the knitting-needle of revenge.
S. Thou shalt not strike in time of war.
9. Thou shalt, not corner food-stuffs in time
of war: for the food exploiter is hate-
ful in the eyes of the peoplej and the
Lord, or the law, or someone will visit
■ him with ten thousand blasting, brim-
stone curses, so that he will sit up
and wonder what struck him.
10. Thou shalt not buy imported patent,
medicines, or smoking cures, or hair
restorers: for the quack of thine own
land has a claim before his brother in
Ecuador.
VICTORIA.
J. D. C.
The War
The Story of a Prisoner of War , by Pri-
vate Arthur Green (Ghatto and Windus;
Is.), is the real thing at last. Green can-
not write : he cannot even spell; but he can
tell the truth, the blankv truth, and nothing
but, the blanky truth. He was wounded and
taken prisoner in the retreat from Mons,
was sent to various Hun prisons, and finally
to the Wittenberg Hell, whence he was. at
last, exchanged as an incapacitated prisoner.
“I got mine in the thigh. I thought I’d
been kicked by an elephant. It hit me
sickey, I can tell. I riggled my straps off,
drank all my water, tried to get up, thinking
I could run ; but I found my leg would not
let me go.” In the first Hun prison the
men soon “got. as lousy as cuckoos.” The
rest of the story is chieflv lice, starvation,
longing for a smoke, and Hun brutality.
Once “they lashed out and gave us a
saveloy, that was the only piece of meat I
had while I was there.”
At Wittenberg, Green notes that one of
the officers was a pig. “He got his cabbage
cooked in the end: but it took nine months
to do it.” “We were not many days there
before we were lousy. I felt ashamed of
myself; but, there! I could not help it.
There was a canteen at this time. The men
had to get in line for this. Then up would
come a German, spot an Englishman, 'out
with his whip, and chase him. Oh! they did
get a time.”
The prisoners had a cheerful Christmas
Day. “Had our ration of bread, 10 to 12
ozs., played a couple of games of crib (cards
made out of backs of cigarett packets),
three-quarters a pint of pea-water, black as
soot, with about 12 peas in it; then off with
our shirts, having a srood hunt for lice. I
was out of the running, only catching about
40. Then a read of the Testament for an
hour, another came of donkey, then a talk
of home and the old times we had had, and
what we should have eaten and drank if we
were there, wishing everyone luck, especially
the boys in the trenches. Then 5 o’clock
1 pt. of flour water. Then we kept up
Christmas by singing a few carols and
songs; but the thing we missed most was a
smoke. We never had a smoke all day.
Then we made our beds down and was in
it, and I suppose most all was asleep by
6.30. And there was no fat heads next day
or bad insides. That was the most terrible
Xmas you could have.”
Contracting “hague,” and with a “temper-
ture,” Green had to go into hospital. “I
was the only Britisher in there. The place
was filthy, the lice were in millions; you
could see them climb up the walls. There
was a Russian carried out, like a bag of
bones. His blanket, what was over him,
looked like frost on it; but I could see it
was lice in millions. I expect he soon died,
or they done him in. That was the first of
the typhus.” Two men died, and “that left
us 18 men. We used to work in their
two bowls for extra dinner, then take it in
turns at having it. We done it for two
months before we were found out.”
P*
The gentle German deserted the camp
when the typhus broke out. There were
15.000 Russians, over 2000 French, 850 Eng-
lish, and about 40 Belgium “civies”; and
the Huns would stand about nine feet from
the wires and speak to the prisoners. The
food was sent down a shoot. There were
about a score of doctors prisoners in the
camp: but they had no means to fight the
fever, And the lice were so bad that at
night Green could not sleep. Ultimately six
English doctors came to the camp, having
volunteered to fight the disease. At this time
the prisoners were dying at an average of
30 a day. “They were burying them 2 in
one box —take them straight down and lay
them along side one another.” The losses,
according to Green were 1500 Russians,
over 300 French, and 87 English, all from
typhus.
“It was during the month of March and
April that we felt the starvation most.
Every one was like wolves. We were also
all affected with itch, which made us nearly
crazy. Then a smoke use to worry us. We
could not get a fag nohow, so we use to cut
up straw out of our beds, coffee grouts,
cut up cigarett holders, and scrape the oil
out of the wood.” And in March they got
their first bath. “Well, it seemed a treat to
miss the lice, for a few hours; but we were
just as bad in a few days.” Green, dying
for a smoke, but having nothing to sell,
took off his under flannel, which he had
been wearing for four months, and gave it
to his chum. It took him two hours to get
some of the grease off; then he dried it and
tramped all over the camp to sell it; but all
he could get for it was a penny. Two hours
later he managed to acquire another half-
penny ; and at 9 o’clock he brought back “a
fag and a bit.” “Then we had a good smoke,
putting a bit away for the morning, and we
thought we were going to enjoy it ourselves.
But first one popped his head out, then
another; so I gave them the hit to go on
with. It was at them times just like giving
them | sovereign each.”
At last permission was given to the Eng-
lish to write home. And a parcel reached
Green’s mate. The good food made him
bad for three or four days; but that night
“we had the pleasure of smoking ourselves
dry. Darkey [his mate] is a great fag-
smoker. lie was out of his mattrass evgry
half-hour lighting up. I shall never forget
the 18th of May, 1915, when we had the
Duff.”
Conditions gradually improved, not because
the Hun repented, but because he was forced
to pretend to a little decency by the insist-
ence of the United States Ambassador. Of
course, the Huns knew the time of that per-
son’s visit, and readied things nicely for his
inspection. But the English gave the game
away to him, and. a surprise visit subse-
quently put the Iluns on their best behavior,
though, as Green indicates, they did not
become angels. And when, finally, Green
was exchanged and reached Holland, he
says, “It seemed, so nice to have kind people
to talk to you.” It must have !
This book would make pleasant reading
for our own interned Huns. It should be
distributed in every camp by our military
authorities.
Drama
Paul Claudel, one of France’s new writers,
had worked for 20 years in an obscurity com-
plete except for the personal talk of
him by certain discerning critics. Claudel
is a Catholic, and a public official, Consul
in various towns in Asia ; so, afraid of dam-
aging his career, he published anonymously.
Suddenly the war allowed him to break his
silence, and now the young critics rank him
as a dramatist with yEschylus and Shalc-
speare. Claudel is primarily a poet, though
his speech is neither prose nor poetry, but a
curious, though dramatically effective, form
of vers libre. He has brought the whole of
his dramatic work together under the general
title of L’Arbre. A French critic claims
that Claudel, is a great religious poet. “His
work, it has been said, is a long pilgrimage
toward God.... The present fashion for
Roman Catholicism (in France) counts for
something in Claudel’s sudden vogue, just as
Claudel has done something to bring about
the fashiop.”
The Tidings Brought to Mary (Chatto
and Windus, 65.) is described by Claudel as
“a mystery” : “the scenes of the drama take
place at the close of the Middle Ages, seen
conventionally, as medieval poets might have
imagined classic antiquity.” It is an inter-
esting drama, but one that bears translation
badly. The form and the content and the
view-point are strange to the Australian
reader. The story starts with a kiss that
a young girl had, in all innocence, given to
a man who loved her. That man, Pierre de
Craon, a master-builder of churches, was a
leper. Volaine’s innocent kiss was seen by
her sister, Mara, who desired to marry
Jacques, to whom Volaine is betrothed. But
Volaine contracts the disease, and discloses
the fact to Jacques. “She cuts the linen of
her gown, upon the side upon the heart,
under the left breast, and leaning towards
him she opens the slit with her hands and
shows him the flesh where the first spot of
leprosy has appeared.” And Mara, who has
already poisoned Jacques’ mind with her
story of Volaine’s kiss, marries Jacques,
while Volaine goes away to the leper’s cave:
In a grim and remarkable scene years later
Mara brings her newly-dead baby to
Volaine’s cave. There the leper-woman tells
Mara that her kiss was innocent, and that
she let Jacques believe her guilty, so that
he would not regret leaving her. The grief-
distraught Mara places the corpse of her
baby in Volaine’s arms, and tensely asks
Volaine to bring the child to life. For the
leper-woman lias assumed in the eyes ot her
sister the character of a saint. The time,
with an obvious symbolism, is that of
Christmas Eve. Wolaine hears the
Christmas Bells, the bells announcing the
midnight Mass. “O Mara,” she cries, “a
little child is born to us!” And Mara re-
plies, “Then give me back mine.” Then in
a mystical scene, in which Mara reads “the
Christmas Service, the First Lesson of each
of the three Nocturnes,” and the voices of
angels are heard chanting in Latin, the
miracle occurs. All the while Yolaine has
held the dead baby beneath her cloak, and
no\v her cloak moves. “The little bare foot
of a baby, moving lazily, appears in the
opening of the cloak.” The child lives!
But the mother looks at it closely as she
takes it.
Volaine!
What does this mean? Its eyes icere
black.
And now they are blue like yours.
Ah!
And what is this drop of milk I see on its
lips?
The mystery ends with the recovery from
leprosy of Pierre de Craon, the cathedral-
builder, and Volaine’s reconciliation with
Jacques, and her death. The final scene is
suffused with the spirit of renunciation and
peace, with the Angelus sounding from
churches far and near —from the churches
that Pierre de Craon has built to the glory
of God.
Paul Claudel has marvellously reproduced
the strange blending of realism with the
poetry of the Middle Ages. His characters
are real people; but they move in an atmo-
sphere of poetry. And the language has the
same vague, medieval beauty, especially in
the speeches of Pierre de Craon, builder of
churches. He speaks of churches “so deli-
cately put together, adjusted with such art.
that they seem as if they would ring like a
bell under a finger-tap.” He defines a
■ cathedral as “Darkness, with God.” And
his apprentice dilates upon his master’s
methods, lie sent an apprentice who stayed
all big time in his corner, drawing, up on
the scaffolding to serve the masons all day.
Saying that by the end of the day he would
know two things better than- he could
learn them by rule and design: the
weight a man can carry, and the height
of Ins body....
Do you think Father Noah was indifferent to
the body when he built the ark? And
are these things of no account? —
The number of paces from the door to the
altar, and the height the eye may be
lifted to, and the number of souls the
two sides of the church may hold all at
the same time?
For the heathen artist made everything from
the outside, but toe make all from within
like the bees,
And as the soul does for the body: nothing is
lifeless, everything lives,
Everything gives thanks in action.
d*
In 1913 Winthrop Ames offered a prize of
lO.OOOdols. for the best play submitted
anonymously by a TJ.S. author. Nearly 1700
MSS. were received, and Alice Brown re-
ceived the cash with Children of Earth, Sub-
sequently produced by Mr. Ames in /New
York, without, apparently, making any per-
manent success, though the critics praised it
highly. It is now published in book form/by
Macmillan, N.Y. (7s. fid. out here). T*ihe
action takes place in a village in New Eng-
land ; and there is much rustic humor and]
joviality, and dancing round the apple-tree,\
and baiting of the love-crazed village idiot by ’ *
the children, and generally a flavor of old
clothes, blossoming orchards, old pewter,
meanness, avarice, suppressed love affairs,
shiftlessness, and middle-aged romance. The
plot hardly matters: it is the subtle charac-
terisation that carries the reader’s interest.
The types are evidently true to life; but the
amount of life that survives in rustic New
England is almost negligible. It is a quiet
little play, lifted into action only once, when
the married middle-aged Peter Hale and a
sexually starved middle-aged spinster decide
to elope. It is the accidental sight of Peter.
Hale’s wife—most awkwardly contrived by
the playwright—about to commit suicide, and
then recalled to her duty of “gifting the
breakfast,” that upsets the romantic plans of
these middle-aged lovers. They decide that
since even the wronged wife can bustle about
and get the breakfast, it is up to them to
stay and face the inevitable scandal. In
the final act the wife makes another and
more difficult sacrifice to shield Aunt Mary
Ellen, by falsely accusing herself of drunken-
ness, her old failing. There is scarcely a
character in the play that does not sacrifice
him or herself. And the play closes with
the two women, Mrs. Peter Hale and Aunt
Mary Ellen deciding to live in the old house
with Peter Hale—a happy family of three.
As Mary Ellen says: “She [Mrs. Hale]
wants to go right on, same’s we’ve been
goin’. An’ she seems to know we can go on.
You an’ I know it—but she knows it, too.”
For all Mrs. Hale wants is .“to have you two
back ag’in. You stay right here in this
house, an’ let me stay with you. And we’ll
work. An’ you’ll keep the devil out o’ me.
An’ Peter Hale’ll come her an’ eat. An’ I’ll
see to the house down there. An’ that’s all.”
If Children of Earth is the best that the
U.S. can do for 10,000d015., here we have the
explanation of the sort of plays that are
imported to Australia. They are the best
available.

ache, rheumatism and gout, gravel, and the
burning sensation of inflammation of the
bladder, etc.

strengthen the kidneys and drive the

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Moonsong.
The stars are winking overhead
Demurely in their impish glee;
They wink because the lazy, red,
Bold moon leaps up to kiss the sea.
The naughty moon has kissed the sea
Nightly for all a million years;
The talk of her inconstancy
Is wicked slander, it appears.
The meteors fall like golden tears,
And in the depths of ocean glow
As cold, soft pearls for Mabel’s m rs,
Or, strung, upon her breasts or snow
Coo into ecstasies. I know
How very happy they must be—
Pearls war* against her heart, although
Her heart is ice to Love and me.
The moon leaps up to kiss the sea,
A moon impatient, flushing red;
And at the sight with impish glee
The stars are winking overhead.
N.S.W.
SEEWYN RIDER.
Friendship.
There are no arts (nor all the books that
tell of them
Tell of no artifices that replace them)
To guide frail men concerning who speaks
well of them,
Nor who would honor them, nor who
debase them.
I want to know for sure who is a friend of
mine,
And who would stand by me, and who
would scout me;
What and to whom I ought to give or lend
of mine,
And, in the outcome, who would pay, who
flout me.
I seek a friend that I may freely give to
him
Aught that I value much, as friendship’s
token ;
My life were little, but as I may live to
him
Like to a link, save which fife’s chain
were broken.
I seek in vain among all modern sciences,
For standards, measures, touchstones that
may tell me
Where I may make reliable alliances
With such as, on occasion, will not sell
me.
This may appear a trifle misanthropical—
It is not really, for I am not made so.
I look, benevolently microscopical,
For what is not —at least, I am afraid so.
I hope—no mortal ever hoped more fer-
vently—
To find the things I search for so in-
tently,
Patiently watching, and, not unobservantly,
Seeing who passes, closely, not ungently.
Something too much of this, 'I miss the
touch of it—
Asking the world for something that’s
beside it.
It is but idleness to ask so much of it,
And it is churlish, when it fails, to chide
it.
If social science, like specific gravity,
Could be reduced to a defined equation ;
If we could weigh out virtue and depravity
In grocers’ scales', what need of. this
oration ?
I find I have been making, most informally,
In my mixed manner of oblique allusion,
Two statements, not antagonistic normally,
Yet apt to lead us on to some confusion.
First, that the art is more than hypotheti-
cal
That can distinguish flatterer from lover;
Next, that I languish, in the mode poetical,
For what I fear I never shall discover.
Neither or both be true—but, incidentally,
I’ve hinted that the thing has no exist-
ence,
And all the while declared that, senti-
mentally,
I seek it with illogical persistence. 4
Come to the old trite tag: what’s in the
heart of us
Is all our world, the warrant of our
being;
Friendship and friend are equally a part of
us,
Only the spirit needs the art of seeing.
SPAIN.
WAIF.
Heaven anti Hell.
I seek no vague bliss in a dim hereafter
By walking gloomy paths in pain and tears;
Right well I know that Heav’n is made of
laughter,
And Hell of morbid thoughts and gloom and
tears.
The earth itself builds not its joys in matter,
But by our pleasure everything is fair;
We reap the fruitage of the joy we scatter.
So, if there’s Heaven, we shall harvest there.
Here we already walk in Hell or Heaven
Assailed, it may be, by some earthly pains;
The happy heart supplies the priceless leaven
By which the bread of life its worth attains.
We train our souls to give and garner
laughter,
To think the best, to dream the noblest
dreams,
And Heaven waits not for a vague hereafter,
But through earth’s clouds in present com-
fort gleams.
N.S.W.
HAMER.

A joyous life
From worry freed.
On hill and curve
They've steady nerve,
And all the time
The sights observe.
Though fast their flight
They take a bite.
Wares branded “REX”
Are their delight.

The Bulletin
Kegistered at the General Post Office, Sydney, N.S.W., Australia, for Transmission by Post as a Newspaper.
Vol. 37.—N0. 1899.
THURSDAY, JULY 6, 1916.
Price 6d.
THE NEW PRESIDENT TAKES OFFICE.
Shanghai, Wednesday.—Admiral Liming-hsen, Commander-in-Chief, has demanded that Li Yuan Hung, who assumed the office of President upon the death
of Yuan-Shih-Kai, shall restore the Provisional Convention of the Old Parliament (which the late President dissolved) and appoint a new Cabinet. Otherwise,
Admiral Liming-hsen says, the navy will declare its independence.

The National Australian Newspaper.
“Australia for the White Man.”

Musgrave and McLeod Streets, Mosman, for The
Bulletin Newspaper Co., Ltd., at the Office of the

THURSDAY, JULY 6, li)16.
INDUSTRIAL UNTIDINESS AND
COAL DUST.
When it was announced the other day that
the Commonwealth Ministry and the Sydney
Coal-lumpers’ Union had arranged for the
formation of a naval transport coal-
ing battalion, it appeared appropriate to
various newspapers that they should offer
some more or less profound remarks about
“regimentation” and its dangers; also about
strikes and patriotism and the propriety of
adjourning industrial squabbles until the war
peters out. The point of most of the solemn
observations was that to hold up a transport
by a strike is an act of disloyalty, and that
the first consideration of every citizen should
be to keep the preparations for war going
without interruption. If this represented all
there was to say about the battalion of
coalies, the mobilisation of these toilers
would be merely an incident of temporary
significance. But the battalion is some-
thing more than that, and its formation sug-
gests ideas extending a long way beyond the
mere hurried flinging of coal into a steamer’s
bunkers. Under the agreement entered into,
the Lumpers’ Union binds itself that there
shall be no interruption of the work of get-
ting the transports away. Now that repre-
sents a step forward in organisation, the
introduction of reason into an atmosphere of
chaos; a recognition under the stress of war
that there is something more for men to toil
for than the day’s pay. So long as men
imagine there is not they are little better off
than bullocks beneath the yoke. When
society can' see the truth lurking behind
Arbitration Acts and coal lumpers’ battalions
■ —behind even the frantic howls we hear
regarding “class warfare” —then it will begin
to get a reasonable hang of the Capital and
Labor problem. Incidentally, it will then
commence to understand what citizenship
implies. It will acquire, too, a grasp of what
loyalty means —instead of imagining it to be
indissolubly mixed up with Gor’save and
a Cocked Hat. The great truth struggling
to be seen and heard is that work is social
service; that the primary duty of men and
women is to labor for the common good. Of
course it is a fine, large, plastic truth capable
of infinite . distortion. The form in which
it has received acceptance so far from society
makes the world look like a pack of dingoes
at work among a mob of sheep. And it is
often mighty hard to tell which is which.
* * * *
When coal-lumpers go on strike and stick
up a transport, while agitated officials rush
round struggling to tidy up the mess, what
they mainly succeed in doing is to illustrate
the muddled condition of society. When
they agree not to strike when loading trans-
ports, they concede the existence of a social
claim upon the individual; further, they make
it plain enough that social and industrial
muddle mostly exists elsewhere because there
is very little genuine attempt to remove it. It
also exists because there are very powerful
vested interests in the perpetuation of muddle.
If the same weight was given to social con-
siderations when dealing with industrial and
economic questions in time of peace as is
given to at least some matters of public con-
cern in time of war, there would be a fairly
decent chance for society to get its head up.
Every sane citizen sees a social disgrace in
allowing these vessels to be the sport
of warring individual interests. Why?
Because war has touched the civic spirit.
Because, in the face of war, considerations
that had been submerged in the greedy
struggles of peace have forced their way to
the top. And because in time of war men’s
minds are purged, in part at least, of the
baser anti-social impulses, and notions of
communistic obligations find expression. But
the ethics of these are, not limited to moments
of what are called national crises, nor to
men who perform humble tasks with dirty,
sweating bodies and heavy shovels. Every
hour in the life of a nation is a critical hour.
Every citizen is part" of a whole, with his
rights and privileges as a citizen, but with
no rights or privileges that are inconsistent
with the public welfare. There is no essen-
tial difference between a strike of profes-
sional coal-shifters alongside an Australian
transport and a strike of Melbourne bakers
and mill-hands. Botli are socially disgrace-
ful. The existence of the causes which lead
to these disturbances is more scandalous. If
the lumper and baker be blafnable, as they
surely are, the greater blame rests on. society,
and it is society which pays the price.
* * * *
Heaven alone knows what the human
family is blundering along to, but it is cer-
tain that the work of the world cannot
best be done if left to haphazard perform-
ance. There were some glimmerings of this
in Australia in the long ago when the idea
of industrial arbitration took root. The
history of that experiment has to some
extent been a disappointment. But it is
not the idea that is wrong. That still
stands as the foundation on which a better
State will be built. If it did not thus stand
the outlook for society would be towards
anarchy; for unless there be social respon-
sibilities, civilisation is merely a jocular
interlude between our descent from the trees
and our retirement into the jungle. But
even though the arbitration system has
failed to yield anything like perfection it
has still carried Australia nearer to a reali-
sation of the truths of citizenship than is
implied by the frequent recurrence of strikes.
So far from strikes proving or even suggest-
ing the hopelessness of social organisation
they do exactly the reverse. They flourish
because there is no organisation, and taken
in the main can only be regarded as vague,
clamorous protests against a social order in
which evasion of duty is rampant —in which
existence is to the mass of men and women
merely a disordered scramble after a share in
the national earnings, with society busy loot-
ing itself, and in which civic ideals ordinarily
carry less weight than do speculations on the
price of a leg of mutton. Arbitration has
sagged for a variety of reasons, mainly be-
cause it was asked to carry too big a weight,
and because of a delusion that it represented
the top of the ladder instead of a far-down
rung. There are cocksure individuals with
their mouths full of copious explanations
about how to correct these mistakes and
ready to fix society up in a new suit and get
it shaved and shampooed and equipped with
gold-filled teeth all in five minutes. There
are any number of other persons waving leg-
irons in the breeze and explaining that these
would put things right in five minutes. Such
quacks are no use. The road ahead has not been
paved and swept and dusted in advance. It
is full of holes and unexpected ditches and
strewn with sharp stones and spiky vege-
tables. Nevertheless, The Bulletin has an
unbreakable faith that the track ahead is
neither so rough nor so long as the one be-
hind. It was a queer-looking rabble that
started out on the path of progress away
back at the beginning of things, and even
if it still be unkempt and shabby, it has a
bath occasionally and there are enough boots
to go round, and it sees the stars. And
every sign there is that the civic ideal is
ripening, that the organisation of society for
the good of society is going forward, is -an
indication that we- are nearer a plane on
which men will use their brains and hands for
something better than the enrichment of
their own plutocracy. There are many such
signs, and this coal-shifters’ battalion, with
its black face and its toil-worn hands and
its dinner in its pocket, is one of them. So
this paper, in the midst of a great untidi-
ness, with much more to arrive later on,
gives these coalies three cheers, and, if an
intelligent Censorship will let it, inquires
whether the shipowners have been doing any-
thing which looks quite like the same thing.
THE SERF WITH THE HOE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Someone remarks that the Clerks’ Union
can’t get its alleged grievances redressed
because its members are too poor in spirit
to down tools and go out on strike. The
same, and a lot more, may be said of the
small farmers, those joyless toilers who mix
it with dairying, vegetable growing and
other small industries that keep their backs
bent for 14 hours 'a day.
One never hears the old gag of the “in-
creased cost of living owing to the war”
raised on the farmer’s behalf: yet everything
he uses in his business —agricultural imple-
merits, seeds, manure, spraying material, gal-
vanised iron, bags, sewing twine, rabbit-
proof netting, fencing wire, harness, horse-
shoes, etc. —has been bumped from 50
to 300 per cent, higher than the
prices ruling before Bill of Berlin went
fanti. In addition to that disaster, the
prices of the stuff he produces have fallen
so low that he can’t earn a living
wage, let alone interest on the capital in-
vested in land and stock. Even a farmer
has to exist somehow. True, in N. S. Wales
the Holman crowd doesn't admit the neces-
sity-. Hence whenever anything the farmer
produces appears in danger of returning him
a small profit for his labor, it is promptly sat
upon by some fool commission, in the inter-
ests of well-paid city workers, who in the
intervals between strikes growl at having to
work eight hours a day. At the same time,
they want the farmer to supply them with
cheap food by toiling 14 hours a day.
The alleged aim of the Trades Hall-ridden
Government is to prevent the producers tak-
ing advantage of war conditions to raise
the price of their products. Yet the city
wage earner, by combining to strike for
higher pay, can just as truly be accused of
extorting “war profits” from his country’s
misfortune as any highwayman in York-
street. And note how his friends in power
assist him to do it. He is given the biggest
say in fixing the price of the labor he has to
sell; but the cocky farmer has no say at all
in fixing the price of his produce. And
while the arrogant wharf-laborer sees
to it that his minimum salary is fixed,
the serf on the land has to suffer the rank
injustice of having a maximum price fixed
upon the products of his labor by a Commis-
sion upon which he is not represented. When
chaff -took a rise last year, owing to the dry
seasons, Holman accused the cockies of
being rapacious sharks who were holding
their stacks back for still higher prices. As
a matter of fact, most of the poor devils had
such miserable crops that the little they had
•to sell wouldn’t have paid them at £2O per
ton. The State then jumped into the busi-
ness—having nothing of its own invested —
and imported shiploads of rubbish that cost
nearly twice as much as our own growers
were compelled by law to accept, being thus
robbed to enrich a few Yankees. Now, if
it was true last year that the cockies could
and would have combined to force up the
price of chaff if the Government hadn’t
imported alleged chaff and sold it at a ruin-
ous loss, why is .chaff now being sold in the
Sydney market at £4, when the price still
fixed by law is £7 10s.? Why isn’t tlie
rapacious cocky combining to get the price
the ass-made law allows him to get—lf he
can? One reason is that he has no friends
who will fix a minimum wage for his labor,
in return for giving them his political sup-
port.
Turn now to another affluent monopo-
list—the cocky who supplies the suffer-
ing city of Sydney with vegetables.
The growing of cabbages is always
mentioned as a gilt-edged industry by
the experts who cultivate them in the daily
newspaper offices; but I, who live among
them, haven’t so far met a grower who is
getting fat on “war profits”—-or any
other profit. Cabbages are almost as ex-
pensive to raise as mortgages —a sister in-
dustry. Costly manure is required (many
growers use as much as a ton to the acre),
and seed costs 225. Gd. per lb. The labor of
planting, watering and hoeing between the
rows is a back-breaking and (this season) a
heart-breaking job. After months of tedious
toil the grower starts to market his goods.
Watch him cutting them by the light of a
lantern in the drizzling rain, then humping
them to the waggon waiting on the road.
There he counts them and stacks them, and
while the city toil-dodger is still aDed dream-
ing of less work with more pay, he is trudg-
ing the weary miles to the station. He
remembers that his grandfather died still
speculating hopefully upon the promised rail-
way. and, as he sploshes through the mud, he
ruminates upon the fact that a Government,
too virtuous to legalise the tote, intends to
spend £ll,OOO upon a hop, step and jump
line to a Northern racecourse, so that its
friend Ikey Noses may open his bag with
more speed and comfort. So much money is
spent upon the luxuries of the consumers, so
little upon the necessities of the producers.
The producer realises this in a dull sort of
way when he receives the account sales of his
load of cabbages, accompanied by a request
from the agent to send along a few shillings
to balance his account for freight and com-
mission. What would a wharf-laborer say
if his employer asked him to part up a few
bob for the privilege of stacking wheat?
It is useless to quote the law of supply
and demand to this farmer. That law, he
has found out, only works when there is a
glut. When there is only half a crop and
the “inexorable law” starts to send prices
up, the wharfies and other city workers com-
mence to cry about the increased cost of liv-
ing. and the Government at once flies to
their rescue. No chance of the cocky mak-
ing up his losses!
Take the much-abused cow-cocky, for
instance. In the winter time he has to hand-
feed his cows—all the time, lately. Butter
is scarce in the winter and, naturally, should
be dearer; but the political servants of the
city laborer very thoughtfully provide against
that by compelling the cow-cockies to lodge
30 per cent, of their butter in cold storage, as
a reserve to be drawn upon in winter. Did
anyone ever hear of importers being com-
pelled to store wheat-bags when they are
cheap, so that they couldn’t squeeze an exor-
bitant price from the wheat cocky at harvest
time? Don’t all speak at once! Here is an
extract from the market report of the Daily
Telegraph (16/6/T6) :—-
Stocks of carbide on spot are now concentrated,
and the market is in the hands of two or three
houses. Some holders asked £4O per ton.
It meant £lOO fine for the cocky to hold back
his chaff last year, and his haystacks, re-
served for a dry time, were liable to be seized
by a Government that is always abusing lnm
for not making any provision against drought.
Why doesn’t the Government inquire into
the prohibitive prices demanded by im-
porters for fencing wire, bags, and a lot or
other things that are at least as necessaij
to farmers as beer and plum puddings an
to the city electors?
If something isn’t done soon to ease the
strain, the man with the hoe may get really
annoyed and follow the advice of that Tam-
worth farmer who publicly advocated that
10 men from every district in the State
should march to Macquarie-street and argue
the point. Perhaps, when a large body of
returned soldiers have settled on the land
and discovered the sort of job they are up
against, the hint of the indigant Tam worth
cocky may be taken seriously.
CRIPPS CLARK.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Hughes’s Shipping Deal.
\V. Hughes has bought 15 steamers and
handed Australia the bill. It is a
horribly big bill —for about two millions.
It represents some of the price Australia has
to pay for war; and so far as the Labor
party is concerned it is the consequence of
leaping out of bed when the house is afire to
attend to something which should have
been done long before. There is an _ old
plank in the Labor platform labelled “Freight
and Passenger Steamers.” This deal is that
plank hurriedly dragged out. The pur-
chase may, nevertheless, prove a good one
for Australia, especially if it means an
increase of tonnage for the transfer of the
nation’s products. The mere purchase of
the steamers is, however, no guarantee
that tonnage now utilised by Australia will
not be diverted to fill the hole made by the
removal of the purchased ships. If the
result of the transaction is not to more
rapidly move the harvest there will be very
little to crow about. Still, in view of the
prevailing confusion and the abnormal de-
mand for shipping and the plentiful stacks
of wheat, what Hughes has done was in all
probability the only alternative to sitting
down and waiting for whatever might hap-
pen next. So far as price is concerned it
is easy criticism to say that we paid too
much. Whether we did or not depends
almost wholly on the probable duration of the
war, and on that point Hughes ought now
to be better informed than any of his critics
in Australia. Obviously if the vessels had
to work on a pre-war rate at least half the
two millions of value would suddenly vanish.
But there has been great destruction of ship-
ping during the war. The war is still on. Its
cessation must, for a time at any rate, lead
to great activity in shipping. And it is fair
to assume that Hughes weighed the risks,
and took advantage of his position to esti-
mate the duration of the war and the move-
ments of shipping and freight charges. On
this assumption, it may be reasonably con-
cluded that in Hughes’s judgment either
the war and a high rate of freight, or both,
will last sufficiently long to clear the
steamers even at the terrific price Australia
must pay for them.

The Foolish Habit of Barking at
Shadows.
The circular issued by Federal Treasurer
Higgs suggesting that wage-earners might
subscribe to the war loan by purchasing
£lO bonds with weekly payments through
their employers has been met by a howl of
protest from the Nepean (N.S.W.) Labor
Council. It seems about the silliest yelp of
the season. The justification for it is stated
to be (1) the grave danger of employers
putting the screw on workers and compel-
ling them to subscribe; (2) the fact that
married men on meagre wages could not
possibly pay ss. weekly to acquire a bond.
The obvious answer is that the likelihood of
there being an Australian employer mad
enough to try to exercise compulsion in this
matter over an Australian workman is as
remote as the Day of Judgment, and that
even if there are some men who cannot save
there are without a doubt many who can
and do. Common knowledge of the relation-
ship of workmen and employers in Aus-
tralia is sufficient to guarantee the first
part of this answer. The figures relating to
Savings Bank transactions prove the second
part. Knibbs estimates that there are
610,558 persons in Australia whose earnings
run from £2 to £4 weekly, and it is a fair
assumption, borne out by the information
relating to the number of married and
unmarried people in the Commonwealth,
that' a very large number of this total are
men and women without any other
“encumbrance” than themselves. These are
the people who in the main are savings-bank
depositors. AVhat is wrong with them lend-
ing money to Australia for war purposes t
In what way is it more “dangerous’ for
them to do tiiis than to lend it, as they do,
to wild-cat State Governments/ The
Nepean Labor Council is looking at things
the wrong end up. Participation in this
war lorn is a patriotic duty, and while it is
a pity that some Australian men and women
will be compelled to say they cannot assist,
it is almost as great a calamity that Miere
should be others who encourage those who
could assist tv say “I will not.” It is ;
Forward!

Making Martyrs by Bad Judgment.
Roger Casement’s trial has not brought out
any facts which make him appear heroic. Even
from the point of view of the most uncom-
promising Irish patriot it was bad business to
seek aid from the German. In the old days
Wolfe Tone could go with perfect dignity
to French republicans and ask them to send
over an army to help in establishing an Irish
republic. It might not have been the wisest
course; but the innate sympathy between
Frenchman and Irishman, which was racial
as well as political, made the proceeding
fitting enough. Caseaient’s mission was of
a different kind. He had spent his life m
the direct service of Britain, and when the
country he had served and which had given
him cash and honor was in the worst straits
he went over to the enemy of all free peoples,
and pleaded for help in such a way as to
bitterly prejudice the best interests of Ire-
land. Neither racially nor politically could
Germany have any sympathy for Casement’s
country, though she might be induced to use
it as a tool. But if Casement did nothing
heroic himself, the Government, by its folly
in taking his trial out of Ireland, gave him
the status which he sought. He was abso-
lutely correct in his claim that he should
have been tried by a jury of his own country-
men ; and the fact that he did not get such
a trial indicates a strange lack of courage
on the part of British Ministers. The whole
trouble with Ireland, as Asquith knows,
has been the unwillingness of Irishmen to
admit the judgment of England on matters
affecting their country; yet in this matter
of a trial for High Treason they, were still
prepared to follow the old irritating course,
and take the big risk of inflaming rebellion,
rather than the minor risk that an Irish jury
might bring in a verdict against the weight of
evidence. Casement, condemned by his own
country, would have been forgotten, in an
hour; Casement, condemned by Englishmen,
at once takes his. place among the notable
sufferers in the national cause.

Waiting for Our Great Offensive.
W. M. Hughes has done some things that
stand quite alone in the record, of Australian
talk in England. Other politicians have
gone forth on special missions, and some of
them have been successful and some have not.
Hughes stands among those who have not.
He failed all along the line in doing those
things which he avowedly set but to do; but
there never was a representative of this
continent who said more about matters on
which he had no instructions to speak at all,
and about which he had no means of know-
ing the will of the people of the country he
serves. And therein lies Hughes’s
magnificence. Others paused, remained
silent, or spoke the simple and easily-
mouthed platitude, because they were
burdened by the sense of responsi-
bility. Not so he. Opportunities to talk
presented themselves; ancient Tory reaction-
aries, with a noisy ha’penny press behind
them, ached to hear their opinions voiced.
The man from Australia stepped in and
obliged. He took England by storm —the
ha’penny press saw to that —and, throwing
all sense of respoilsibility to the winds, he
filled British ears with torrents of brave
Avoids. Now, with his 15 grain ships to cover
the fact that he failed to obtain the freights
he went to seek, his University diplomas to
hide the truth that the other matters in his
charge went wrong, and his freedom of
cities to disguise the British Labor party’s
coldness, he returns to Australia to—do
what? Already anti-conscriptionists are
waitinsr foi him with an axe, and other
weapons are being furbished up by various
sections and parties to greet him at his
landing. Will Hughes yield tamely and
fade away into impotent nothingness in a
maze of explanation? Not if we have the
true measure of the man! He. will gird
himself to this new task, with facile jaw and
untiring throat, until the mere multitude of
his words becomes the pride of an astonished
continent. On a moderate calculation,
Hughes will have to speak 10 times as much
as he did in England to produce the neces-
sary confusion —and that will represent an
offensive of the tongue beside which all pre-
vious Australian effort must sound like the
chirping of young sparrows in a windy tree.
Why Not a Citizen Fleet?
Australia has the beginnings of a citizen
army and there are flickering hopes that the
thing may soon take stronger shape and
form, the ranks being filled with grown men
whose training will be sufficient for some-
thing like adequate defence. Now the pur-
chase of an Australian merchant fleet opens
up the possibility of a citizen navy.
Australia is an island; and the endur-
ing advantage of an island is that,
within certain limits, a fleet can defend
it. Moreover, Australia is a very big
island, with a very long coastline and very
little in the way of railway connection
between point and point, and a fleet is obvi-
ously its surest if not its only protection
against invasion. Why, then, not make the
new mercantile squadron a means of training
for the crews of our future warships? Naval
construction is a pretty slow business at
best, but the training of men to handle war-
ships is still slower. Even the wheat ships
themselves would on the outbreak of any
trouble have some value as auxiliary cruisers.
They certainly couldn’t fight regular craft of
war, but they could be used as eyes and ears,
and" be a menace to transports approaching
our coast. Our citizen army will provide us
with a great fighting force of soldiers who
are earning their living, and incidentally the
living of the country, while still in training
and ready for the field. Our citizen navy of
the reserve might be in exactly the same
position if the notion of blending merchant
service with naval training were taken up
heartily. Incidentally, our merchant sailors
wouldn’t lose anything by the smartening of
naval drill and discipline.
Ten weeks ago the German losses before
Verdun were joyfully estimated (by our
side) at anything between 150,000 and
250,000. Then they mounted by easy stages
to 300, 400, and 500.000. All this while
the vastness of the Crown Prince’s sacri-
fices was instanced as evidence of the
enemy’s desperate plight. Newspaper theories
were built on the comforting assumption
that the Huns were paying for the Verdun
position about 300,000 more casualties than
it could ever be worth. Just a week ago,
however, the cable gravely informed us as
follows
According - to a message from Berne, in Switzerland,
a high German diplomatist is reported to have esti-
mated the German losses at Verdun at 170,000.
Which brings us up against a disturbing
conflict of suppositions. Either the German
losses are merely in keeping with the orig-
inal announcement that the Kaiser was pre-
pared to lose 200,000 troops in capturing
Verdun, or . the “high German diplomatist,”
if he really exists, was telling a wilful
diplomatic lie. We pay our penny and we
take our choice. In any case, we are left
with the unpleasant reflection that we still
tend to magnify our own gains and the
enemy’s losses. Isn’t it time this silly
policy was changed? Every month or two
some responsible Minister comes out with
the announcement that it is imperative
that we make this or the other great effort;
then the newspapers, controlled by a Cen-
sorship which from the outset has been a
good friend to Germany, announce tin
effect: “But why do anything more? We
are winning, anyhow!”
According to Tirpitz, in some remarks
made in 1914, the Kaiser’s navy could afford
to remain inert for two years, by which
time Germany would have won the war on
land and would be able to command peace
with her Dreadnoughts still unscarred. To
those unthinking optimists who regarded tin;
statement as a lying excuse for a fleet of
shirkers, the recent doings in the North Sea
were a warning lesson. What we now know
for certain is that the Kaiser has tried to
do everything but one which he threatened
to accomplish. Thus far he has failed to
capture Paris, Calais, the remaining little
bit of Belgium, or Dvinsk in Russia. But
his obedient minions have not ceased to try
to capture the places he hoped to arrive at.
The one thing that remains an empty Ilun
boast is the invasion of England. Is the
war likely to end ere that one crowning
design of German ambition has been actu-
ally attempted? We have to beware of
possibilities. Our worst friends are the
vainglorious asses who mistake prudence for
pessimism, and dope themselves with
optimism till 'their intelligence is dazed.
Our motto in this vast conflict must be
“Hope for the best and prepare for the
worst.”
Shackleton’s expedition to the South Pole
has been a series of disasters from the first.
One ship, the Aurora, had to be patched up
in Sydney before it went any further. Subse-
quently the old tub blew back without a
rudder or something to Maoriland, after hav-
ing mislaid nine of the crew with a couple of
sandwiches. Now the Aurora is being hur-
riedly mended again (and once more at pub-
lic expense) to go back and see what
happened to the missing men. If they im-
pounded a sufficient number of penguins
before the birds migrated, and if they sur-
vived 745 assorted perils in addition to indi-
gestion, they will be all right. Shackleton’s
other ship, the Endurance, was burst into
fragments in an icefield, and Shackleton
and three or four companions reached a
whaling station after a score of their mates
had taken refuge in an icehole at Elephant
Island with a tin of sardines. The last
heard of this lot is that a relieving vessel
which set out from the Falkland Islands was
the wrong sort of vessel, and was driven
back by ice. What has been transpiring
meanwhile in the icehole is less pleasant to
think about than roast turkey. Even at its
present stage the record is a ghastly re-
minder that somebody’s boot should have
come down on the whole foolish business long
ago. These expeditions are utterly futile —
a waste of time, energy, men and cash. That
they should involve Australia in expendi-
ture is a scandal.

It is no wonder that Auditor-General
Israel groans about the Federal finances.
It took him nine months’ harrying and
worrying to get necessary documents and
vouchers from various offices, especially
from the military section of the Defence
Department; and even then he didn’t get.
and never will get, all the papers he needed.
The irregularities move him to anguish. The
stock sheets of the Small Arms factory are
“utterly unreliable,” and “there appear to be
a very large number of important defici-
encies.” The Sydney ordnance stores stock-
sheets also show very many discrepancies;
and a visit to the Sydney paymaster’s office
gave the A.-G. the jumps, “the accounts
being in an unsatisfactory uosition, the cash
book kept anyhow and not checked, while
£IO,OOO worth of unpaid cheques and cash
due to soldiers were kept in an office safe.”
If they were always kept in the office safe
it wouldn’t be so bad; a worse trouble is
that now and then a chenue has mysteri-
ously disappeared. The whole office is in
an appalling muddle; and Pearce ought to
invite the assistance of a small commission
of business-men to straighten things out.
They could be easilv got—the very best men
in Sydney or Melbourne —and it might stop
the present sheer waste of tens of thousands
of pounds.
It is stated that the N. S. Wales Parlia-
mentary Labor Caucus, when discussing the
question of robbing the nub-closi g poll of
some of its terrors by permitting the sale
of light wines with meals up till S p.m., took
the view that —•
It would be unfair to allow a person rich enough
to patronise fashionable restaurants to have wine
with his dinner, while denying the working- man the
privilege of washing his meal down with a mug of
beer.
All of which makes a handsome bouquet of
thistles. In the first place, it is not the
habit of working men to sluice their food
down with beer; in the second place it is
not suggested that the sale of wine should
be restricted to fashionable restaurants and
expensive pubs. What is suggested is that,
while the doors are slammed against the sale
of the more potent liquors, reasonable oppor-
tunity might be given to the wine-making
industry, under conditions from which the
elements that end in drunkenness would be
removed. The consumption of light Austra-
lian wine by people who want it would
surely inflict no hardship on people who do
not. And wherein lies the Labor objection
to allowing the working man to get aus-
tralian wine if he happens to arrive too late
for the beer? This foolish parade of timidity
is merely another indication of how hope-
lessly antiquated the whole business of
drink-distribution has become.

According to a recent letter, nothing had
been done by the Defence Department to end
that snobbery which in Egypt directs Bill-
jui that he may ride only third-class. Or
if anything had been done it had not been
effective. If it is proposed that. Billjim
shall continue to suffer this indignity, let us
at least make his training complete, and pro-
vide a sufficient number of third-class and
suitably-dirty carriages for his accommoda-
tion at home. If we are not game to do
this at home, then it is grossly unfair that we
should call upon him to submit to it once we
have got him abroad. Ours is a citizen army,
the military chiefs might remember —not an
army of derelicts who have taken to the
colors as a last desperate resort to raise a
bob.

A crowd of jewellers went to Tudor, Min-
ister for Customs, the other day and asked
him to lift the war order prohibiting the
importation of gold ornaments. It told
about the poorest sort of yarn, though the
Chamber of Commerce backed it, and
Tudor went away to consult with the
Treasurer. That consultation ought to be
brief and to the point. Gold jewellery is
no sort of necessity; and the only excuse
the barbaric business can give for its exist-
ence in a country like this is that it pro-
vides 1600 people with a job.. The deputa-
tion to Tudor asks for a juicier job for the
middleman dealer in the stuff by turning
him into an importer; but as all evidence
shows that the juicier jobs of this sort pro-
vided from time to time have been a bitter
loss to the country, Tudor ought to come
back from his talk with the Treasurer with
derision on his lips and tell those jewellers
plainly to get work.
The Argus is torn by a conflict in her ample
bosom. She runs a “rural interests” depart-
ment which for some time has strongly- sup-
ported the Victorian Farmers’ Union, while
cheery reports on the formation of additional
branches have been given prominence in
other parts of the paper. But when secre-
tary Isaac Hart declared the other day that
the policy of the Y.F.U. is “to run its own
Parliamentary candidates,” the old lady
plunged her pen into her tear-pot and sobbed
thus: “It would greatly imperil the cause of
good government to have a third political
party spring out of the Victorian Farmers’
Union.” “Good government,” you see,
consists of supporting one of two
parties, whether . you . believe in what
either of them is doing or not. The
Argus is horrified when the Labor
Caucus cracks the whip and brings recalci-
trants to heel. That is tyranny. But it is
“good government” when two parties crack
two whips and bring everybody in the State
to heel.
As far as they go, the Eveleigli (N.S.W.)
workshops are still hammering the importers’
joss. Engines built there hold the world’s
record for mileage done without cleaning;
and the new locomotives for the express , to
the Queensland border —a long trip with
much hill-climbing—are admitted cham-
pions. Yet only a decade or so back it was
thought necessary to send orders to the
U.S. for engines of any old sort. Now 28
of the imported coal-eaters are being re-
formed by Australian workmen, who promise
to. make a saving of between 25 and 35 per
cent, on the consumption of coal and water.
The unsatisfactory part of the business is
that more than half of the material used in
these performances is still imported, and
appalling delays are suffered in consequence.

“Quidam” : One of the most aggravating
features of the Slaughter in Europe is the
impossibility of sizing up any of the great
men concerned and deciding whether they
really are great men or not. Owing to offi-
cial lies and suppressions, absence of un-
fettered critics and various other factors,
the atmosphere of the battlefields has been
reduced to one of heavy fog in which occa-
sional rifts allow of such glimpses that only
misleading inferences are to be drawn.
Even the Kaiser, principal hero or direst
villain, is obscured, and opinions as to his
greatness or otherwise are as divided as
before the disturbance began. Is he a very
great man. the motive power and main-
spring, or only an energetic mediocrity, the
polished brass nut on the spindle? It is
not really necessary to know, but mankind
has got "into the habit of wanting to be
informed of many unnecessary things, such
as the distance of the furthest star and
the temperature of the South Pole. It is
the same with most of the other actors in
the drama —everybody who is doing any-
thing. The spruikers have the best of it.
Asquith and Lloyd George, Billy
Hughes also, have been able to convey
pretty definite impressions of themselves to
the public. But as for the real toilers, no
one knows whether the world will ever be
able to size them up correctly. The chances.
are that they will be simply overwhelmed
by the champion fluters. It seems part of
the price we have to pay for the system
under which the men who talk have to
pretend that they govern even if they don’t,
while those who listen have to pretend to be
their bosses, even though they are not.
“Spiro” : As an instance of the devitalising
influence of syphilis, Dr. Gamble, of the
Kew (Vic.) Asylum, has stated that two-
thirds of the idiot and imbecile children ex-
amined at that institution a few years ago
were the offspring of syphilitic parents, as
shown by the Wasserman blood test. Illus-
trating inherited syphilis as the cause of pri-
mary dementia, he cited the case of a
syphilitic man married to an apparently
healthy woman. They had nine children.
The five eldest were more or less insane. A
sister and a brother are inmates of the Kew
Asvlum; another sister is in Yarra Bend,
whilst, at home, the parents are managing
as best they can a son and a daughter, both
distinctly mental deficients. Gonorrhoea, so
lightly and jauntily regarded by some fools,
is, as regards women, as serious as, if not
Lip Service.
Randolph Bedford discusses a Censor and
Gomorrah :■ —-
Tom Buchanan, of Coalinga (Gal.), leaves Aus-
tralia for home very soon. Tom was for a long
time in Columbus, Ohio, and when I told him that
the State of Ohio has elaborated its censorship of
films to the criminal extent of limiting the kiss
of the movie hero and heroine to 10 feet of film, ’he
became deeply emotional, and said that he would
avoid the State of Ohio as a pestilence. For it is
certain that the righteous men of Ohio or Gomorrah,
who thus put a throttle-valve on photographic
passion, will lose little time in limiting" the dura-
tion of kisses of real men and women who kiss not
for money but for fun or keeps.
The war, by giving woman the man’s job, which
her own vanity will refuse to relinquish after the
war, is certain to intensify the real war that has
always been and never knows peace—the war of
sex. Its nearest approach to peace is but an
armistice so that a new child may be begotten.
And then there come these scurvy knaves, the cen-
sors, further depreciating the chances of population
by making laws to govern equally Brother Anjemia
and Hercules— to apply equally to Mrs. Skin-&-
Grief (poor soul, it is not her fault!) and Venus
of the maturescent breast; to Master Thxnbeard
and Colossus who measures 48 inches around the
chest. Then will they carry their madness further,
these haters of life and sunlight. Censorship of
the film-kiss will pall; therefore will they with
mouths leaking pleasantly follow maid and man to
Lovers’ Lane, spying upon the kiss of bride and
groom, stop-watch in hand, counting the legal
number of seconds, and then crying, “Break, or I
bring the police!”
These considerations, emphasised by me with
cruel gladness to the man who lived in Ohio, have
made Tom Buchanan decide that he will fly across
the dreadful State, if needs be he must go near it at
all; and, further, that if the Fates permit, he will
stay in California —warm, fecund California with
the old brown Missions and the old Spanish love of
living; California and its serried rows of the wine
grape; with the pleasant city where the 1200 saloons
threw away their bar-door keys the day they got
their licenses by San Francisco Bay.
To kiss is so natural that, when a man is asked
for a definition of the act, he stammers and balks
his words, and at last goes hotfoot to the “Encyclo-
pedia Britannica.” And this is all that portentous
and in many ways foolish oracle has to say on the
King of indoor games and the chiefest pleasure of
the world. Find it on page 837, Yol. 15, “Ita to
Kys”:—
“KISS: The act of pressing or touching with the
lips, cheek, hands or lips of another as a sign or
expression of love, affection, reverence or greeting.
Skeat (Etym. Diet. 1898) connects the Teutonic
base Kussa with the Latin Gustos, taste, and with
the Gothic, liustus, test, from Kinsan, to choose;
and takes ‘kiss’ as ultimately a doublet of choice.”
The kiss of peace doesn’t count, humanly speak-
ing, because it is now down to the mere kissing of
stoles and oblations and Eucharistic services; but
all the rest of life is based and crowned by the
half-dozen lines of the inhuman “Encyclopaedia
Britannica,” which is more interested in the deriva-
tion of the word than in the tremendous meaning
and consequence of the act. I do not know what
the kiss of reverence may be, except it be the
kissing of the dead; and herein there is less of
reverence than of love or heartbreak. The kiss of
greeting is probably that frosty peck that women
give each other, without affection or emotion; and
the foolish old Encyclopaedia mentions never the kiss
of duty, which is the kiss of habit of the man or
of the woman who wishes to feel martyrised and
misunderstood. Nor does it speak of the kiss of
Judas —a very much and unjustly maligned man,
bv the way; for he repented, and where there is
shame there is virtue. It fails to tell of the kiss
of the sister of Judas, which is the kiss of the
interested courtesan, of the woman who married for
money, and of the adultery that wipeth its mouth
and sayeth it hath done no sin. These are the
dirty kisses the goodly will neither censor nor ban.
Like their product, syphilis, they are too filthy for
holy mouths; therefore shall we legislate only
against the natural and the clean.
And the real kisses of affection do not come in
here because they are the kisses of home; mostly
the kisses of those delicious things, children —the
best time-payment furniture any married pair can
buy. The mere fact that, just as the last instalment
of the purchase is paid on them, they are discov-
ered to be children no longer, and prepared to
belong to some perfect stranger, does not matter.
The man whom a child’s kiss cannot lead is a bad
man, or at least a careless man, passing the real
gold of life for the lead of it. But what shall be
said of him who shall propose to limit children and
their kisses by limiting the kiss of tender love —even
the kiss of clean passion? To perdition with the
film censor who would limit even a film kiss to
10ft. 1
Ten feet seems a lot; but it isn’t. To say that a
girl’s mouth is two inches wide, and that in kissing
it 10ft. you kiss it 60 times, is merely Dutch calcu-
lation. Quantity of kisses is not so much as quality.
Tennyson, who was a warm old gentleman before
he wrote the foolishness of Victoria being “pre-
eminent in grief,” or such banality as “The village
ne’er had seen a costlier funeral,” wrote of the kiss
splendid—the kiss that drew the girl’s soul through
her lips, while the active partner was as-one drink-
ing dew. This kiss (and I speak from research
made merely for the purposes of this article) cannot
be done in less than lmin. ssec. And the Elders
and the Pharisees would limit us to 10ft. !
Love cannot be measured with thimbles. Ten
feet? Ten feet measured by film-time is ridiculous,
llorghly, one can say that a motion-picture runs
17min. to the thousand feet; a little less than a
foot to the second. So these fools of Ohio think a
man is to be put off with a lOsec. kiss —a mere
peck for a curate. The kiss that means business
must run at least 50. I have heard of one of 2min. ;
but the watch was said to be not certainly reliable.
This is a subject of tremendous importance affect-
ing the welfare of the race ; affecting, by the gods,
the continuity of it. And if I were to approach it
without due reverence I would be in danger of a
beating from women; and would deserve it.

An Expedition to the Hun’s Vitals.
The newspapers went to press this week
with headlines which at last meant some-
thing. The British and French armies had
moved towards the enemy over a front of
25 miles in Northern France. The long-
looked-for offensive had commenced, and
artillery was turning the line thereabouts into
a hell. What this offensive will lead to is not to
be decided in a day or so. The great issue
to be determined is not whether the Allied
troops will push the German bag and bag-
gage out of Belgium before Sunday morning,
but whether control of the military situation
is definitely passing into the hands of the
Allies. Along part of the Russian. front
it seems to be certain that the Bear is get-
ting the upper hand. The Italian is hitting
back hard and effectively in his section of the
disturbance. At Verdun the French are
holding the enemy at bay. . If they
all continue to do these things, and
if the British attack achieves solid
strategical advantages, then, for . the
first time since the war began, will it be
possible to say with truth that a hole is
being bored into the Hun’s armor. But it
would be unwise to expect spectacular vic-
tories or to count upon progress being any-
thing but slow ; for the German has still a
powerful punch and the job of him
down is one of tremendous difficulty.. lliat
he is sorry he ever started the gigantic
struggle, tjiat what are vaguely de-
scribed as “things” are in a bad way
behind the Rhine, and that the Prussian
dream of world-wide Imperial glory lias
changed into a nightmare of ash-heaps and
cemeteries —all this is probably true. That
the German populace has had to take up
more than one hole in its belt and is growing
restive is also very likely. But mere contem-
plation of these circumstances is not going to
bring that crushing defeat of. the German
arms which alone would achieve a peace
worth having. The Hun can be relied on to
fight hard, for a long time yet, and the Allies
to encounter desperate resistance. Another
thing that can be relied upon is that when
the Hun is rooted out of Belgium and France
and Poland he will be suffering . from a
broken back, for he has dug himself in deeply
there, and the last thing he wants on earth
is to fight on German soil. The battles now
raging have a deep interest for Australia, as
Bieljim is somewhere handy, and the people
at this end of the world are occasionally able
to get some incredibly pulling accounts of
how he is getting on. The encouragement
given to recruiting by these fatuously-inad-
equate scraps of news is indescribable.
Corporal Robert Bowling, the fifth son of
ex-Premier Wade’s turbulent old friend
Peter to go to the Front,, was farewelled
last week.
Half a dozen early inhabitants of Victoria
have just died within a few days of one
another. They were William Anderson (73),
pioneer of the meat-canning industry and
founder of the Flemington Meat Preserving
Co.; J. S. Peterson (82), brother of the late
Franklin Peterson, who came here in the
goldfield days, started as an importer, and
was later a manufacturer and one of the
founders of the Australian Club; Auguste
Fisher, artist in stained glass; G. L. Mit-
chell, a pioneer of the ironmongery trade; B.
Richards, who came to the goldfields 65 years
ago, and later owned the biggest dairy in the
State; and John Inglis (89), part-owner of
the first line of sailing vessels to run
between Melbourne and the Gippsland Lakes.
South Aus. thew out a surprisingly
strong batch of 70-year-old public servants
on July 1. The foremost is A. B. Moncrieff,
Railways Commissioner, who did his best
work long ago as Engineer-in-Chief. Another
is Sholl, chief secretary to 14 different Chief
Secretaries. Harry Blinman, the old crick-
eter, is his direct successor, though Sincock, of
the Government reporting staff, becomes
secretary to a new-formed Premier’s depart-
ment. Exit also “Captain” Matthews, who
has had a quiet time as Inspector of Mines,
after a lively one in Westralia as the first
manager of Bayley’s Reward ; and Raymond,
Commissioner of Police (succeeded by In-
spector Edwards), with G. L. Reed, his secre-
tary, and Burchell and Bennett, veteran in-
spectors ; and Stanton, secretary to the Edu-
cation Department; and Iloltze, head of the
Botanic Gardens, whose place nobody seems
to know how to fill, so that he has stayed on
in it till he is close on 77. Reckoning in the
recent vacancies on the judicial Bench, no
S.A. Government ever had so many promo-
tions to hand about in so short a time as
Crawford Vaughan’s.

Melbourne, which was lately said by its
own immortal Melba to be “musically
starved,” is still being served with a good
square meal by Marlow at the Princess’s.
Last week the Gonsalez Co. added
two more productions to the menu.
The more charming of them was the
less rushed —for strictly unmusical reasons.
“TJn Ballo in Maschera,” sweetest ofVerdi’s
operas, had never been popularised in Aus-
tralia by any previous company or particular
aria. When Simonsen’s Co. played it long,
long ago the tenor was not first-class, and
when the Williamson Italian crowd tackled
it in 1901 the baritone (Cattadori) was no
great shakes. Now, the two principal men
are of first importance in “Un Ballo.” The
tenor is an amiable Duke, who makes
warm platonic affection to the baritone’s
wife, the man with the heavier voice being
the Duke’s faithful Prime Minister. In the
first place the loyal baritone stands between
his liege lord and the male chorus of
murderous conspirators, but when he finds
that his wife has been wandering down the
mountain side with his royal master, and the
conspiring chorus chaffs him concerning his
misplaced confidence in the fond couple,
Prime Minister Renato joins the conspiracy
and assassinates the Duke. After which he
discovers that his worst suspicions were ill-
founded.
* * * X-
“Un Ballo” proved to be the most satis-
fying of the Gonsalez Co.’s efforts up to
date. True, the chorus was comparatively
thin, and the chorus’s courtly costumes sug-
gested a draper’s bargain-counter; also the
voice of Miss Russ, who played a lurid clair-
voyant, w y as as nondescript as usual. But
Scamuzzi, in the baritone part, which has
the one famous aria in the opera, sang as
forcibly as he acted, whilst Capelli, always
richly melodious and sincere, made an unim-
peachable Duke. Cacialli, as the chief
plotter in black velvet, sang well enough to
atone for the foggy utterances of the other
basso. Mrs. De Revers was intensely emo-
tional and fairly sopranoic as the Wife

chief colleagues, was quite beautiful.
In “Rigoletto” the honors were with Miss
Gonsalez as Gilda and Filippini as a rather
light and waspish kind of jester. He did
very well, though there was not much weight
in his wrath and despair, and the music of
the name part isn’t as grateful to a sugary
baritone as that of the sympathetic papa
in “Traviata.” Still he and the prima donna,
whose warble (except that it was occasion-
ally “off” in intonation) was as sound as a
bell, won an encore for the quartet in the
last act, tenor Lois and Miss Russ seeming
of less importance to the combination. Lois
was just a nice young duke and a pleasant
vocalist. Cacialli presented a smooth,
matter-of-fact Sparafucile, the professional
cut-throat, who delivers corpses at 15s. a
head whilst you wait, and charges twopence
extra for the sack. “Rigoletto” is far from
being the best of the co.’s performances.
“Tosca” is the only novelty promised for
this week, revivals of previous operas being
in constant demand.

A welcome relief from the American farce
is promised for next Saturday at Sydney
Criterion, when a U.S. light comedy, with
the Australian title of “The Boomerang,”
will afford Myrtle Tannehill, Hale Ham-

At Sydney Adelphi Wish Wynne con-
tinues to charm with her exquisite little
sketches of English life. The rest of the
Ben Fuller show comprises an assortment
of mostly-acceptable vaudeville items.
The Follies have gone from Sydney Tivoli
to Maoriland, and the theatre is now yawn-
ing for wizard Horace Goldin, who is due
on Saturday at the matinee with his
masses of gear and his 35 assistants. Faith
moves mountains, and the illusionist who
shakes Faith has to move mountains of
props.
Paul Dufault, now singing triumphantly
through a return season at Sydney Town
Hall, has two more concerts to give—to-
night (Thursday) and Saturday. If the
writer had, instead of a press ticket, a mean
employer called Peter, he would cheerfully
rob him to go and hear this supremely
artistic Paul. And there is a Pauline, too
—Miss Bindley, of Bendigo, who has im-
proved so much that she is nearly bright
enough now to be a star.
At Melbourne Royal they were to revive
“Madame X” on Wednesday and present
Madge Fabian as that disreputable divorcee
for three nights only. On Saturday, for
the first time in Australia, the actress with
the Roman name plays the heroine in
“Romance,” said heroine being “an Italian
prima donna with the hot blood of the
Tuscan in her veins.” Melbourne is looking
for that sort of girl. Too many of them,
alas! have only the cold straw of the Tuscan
in their hats.

*❖ * *
At Melbourne Auditorium, where Evelyn
Scotney has been trilling her lay with cap-
ital results, artistic and financial, the two
Russian musicians, Mirovitch and Piastro,
will give further recitals to-night (Thurs-
day) and Saturday. Mirovitch is a pianist
of the poetic order who can be brilliant
when a Liszt rhapsody gets him going, and
Michael Piastro is somewhat of a wizard
on the violin. Given a little more personal
magnetism and unctuousness of expression
he would make an audience jump, for be is
a master craftsman and his tone is always
sweet. The violinist plays from memory,
accompanied by his partner. The combina-
tion started last Saturday night with the
Kreutzer Sonata, and in this the two young
men were individually and jointly delightful.
Also the new Chappell instrument that
Mirovitch stroked and tickled gave emphasis
to the fact that until such time as Aus-
tralia ceases to import pianos at all she
will not need to get another box of music
from Germany.

Mankin, a weird contortionist; Maximo,

Bijou. Laurence Johnstone, the ventrilo-
