A brief trampoline act by the Bouncing
Brooklyns is the only new feature of im-
portance at Melbourne Tivoli. The rest of
the programme is supplied by Norton and
Earl, the three Kundles, Baslo and Yivi,
Gardner and Revere, Mary Dorr, Jimmy
Fletcher and two or three others. The
Kundles, the tight-wire trio, and Fletcher,
the contortionist, are about, the best, while
Gardner and Revere, described as the
“comedy couple who score bushels of
laughter,” seem least entitled to reap the
harvest.
Of an art show:—
The art of Frederick McCubbin and son is getting
another advt. at Melbourne Athenaeum. The old man
has most to say in the show, son Louis being nudged
into a corner with instructions not to interrupt. As
a matter of fact, sonny’s haystacks and mine-views
make a meek appeal beside dad’s acres of radiant
scenery. McCubbin, senior, is ever a spring and
summer fancier, and his landscapes, ranging in size
from the huge “Old Fence” to moderate canvases like
“The Creek” and “Bush Hut,” nearly all record gala
days in the brightest part of the calendar. It is a
handsome exhibition of greenery and sunlight, but it
seems to demand a stronger human accompaniment.
Mac. puts figures into his larger efforts without in-
creasing their interest or variety, blit omits popula-
tion from his small flowered spaces, though they
look as if they were made for picnic parties and
awful orgies of sandwich and tea. In the meagre
non-landscape section of the show the painter’s cold
decorum in portraits Nos. 1 and 6 is much to be
preferred to the genre jauntiness he displays in the
mistakes labelled “Interior” and “Shelling Peak”

Preached the last sermon at Armidale
(N.S.W.) on July 2, Bishop Coopei’, who
had worn the Anglican gaiters there since
1901. Cooper was born in England in 1849,
and after climbing up to an M.A. degree at
Trinity College, Dublin, came to Australia
as a young curate in 1874 with Dr. Thorn-
ton, who was imported in that year to bish
in the then newly-formed diocese of Ballarat.
Thornton, by the way, is still alive in Eng-
land, aged nearly 90, and married a second
wife a couple of years ago. Coopei], who
never married, stayed in Western Vic. for
27 years, becoming Archdeacon and subse-
quently Bishop-Coadjutor of Ballarat. _ On
Thornton’s departure, Ballarat Anglicans
chose Green, who then bished at Armidale.
to succeed him, and Cooper went to fill
Green’s gaiters. Business ability was his
strong suit as a bishop, and for many years
he had been chairman of committees in
the Anglican General Synod, of which he
had been a member since its birth.
The Defence Department was probably
right in deciding that Australians who have
enlisted in England should not be per-
mitted to exchange to the Australian forces;
but in defending that stand it was quite
unnecessary to reflect on the British army:
In connection with the British army, the same
rigid standard in the examination of officers was not
observed as that which obtained in the forces of the
Commonwealth. In Australia, commissions were only
granted after a strict examination: but in Great
Britain commissions in some cases had been granted
without examination.
It may be true that commissions are not
granted in Australia without examination;
but it was to. say the least of it unfortunate
that, simultaneously, the Soldier, the offi-
cial voice of the powerful Returned Sol-
diers’ Association, should declare it about
time commissions went by merit, and not
as the result of seeing So-and-so.
E’rom the Office Curate :—-
The Synod committee offered the job of Bishop of
Ballarat to Maxwell Homfray Gumbleton, formerly
Smith, who is vicar of Chippenham and rector of
Ilardenbuish, in Wiltshire (Eng.) ; and as the net
returns from his two collection' plates, put together,
only amount to £435 a year, while the Ballarat
gaiters are worth £lOOO, of course he heard the call.
Born in 1872 in Jamaica, where his father, an English
barrister, had a Judgeship, he was schooled in Scot-
land; struggled up to a bare pass degree at Cam-
bridge, without any honors or prizes; served his
“LUCIA DI LAUMERMOOR” AT
Belloni.
MELBOURNE
PRINCESS’S.
Cacialli.
time as curate, meanwhile marrying Ella Maria, his
rector’s daughter; and first got a parish of his own
18 years ago. There are three sons —the eldest
now in Flanders —and one daughter. You can almost
hear him saying to Ella Maria last winter, “Really,
my dear, what with the children growing up and
this dreadful war-time taxation, I’m afraid we can’t
go on like this: I shall be compelled to take some-
thing in the Colohies !” The Melbourne papers call him
“Canon,” but his canonry is only an honorary affair,
quite worthless as a distinction and not carrying
the title, except by courtesy. He was once chaplain
to (hats off!) “the high sheriff of Wiltshire,” and is
still chaplain of the local workhouse; and when
such “distinctions” are aU that can be said for a
man, he is pretty obscure.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“T. 0.1.” : W. H. E’ord, the reputed “Father
of the Stage,” died last week at
(Vic.), ip his SGth year, lie had been an
invalid for many years. Born in London, of
theati’ical parents, in 1830, he was on the
stage at Astley’s at seven years of age. In
MICHAEL PIASTRO, Violinist, and
ALFRED MIROVITCH, Pianist.
the late ’forties he came to Sydney and
applied for employment at the Victoria, then
open only three days a week. The caretaker
advised him to look for some other job, as
the stage meant starvation in Sydney. So
he went on a station in what is now Queens-
land ; and at Christmas he converted the
woolshed into the Theatre Royal, Tarampa,
for the amusement of the Cameron family
and visitors. Several entertainments were
given, and two cattle-buyers from Brisbane
advised him to start shows in that township.
On this suggestion he built the first theatre
in Brisbane, and later another in Ipswich.
Thus he drifted back to Poverty Point. When
Johnson built the old Scandinavian Hall,
Gastlereagh-street (the Sydney Tivoli is now
on the site), W. H. Ford became the mana-
ger. Prior to that he had been associated
with G. V. Brooke, and later with Barry
Sullivan, as costumier. He tried Maoriland
with Clarence Holt in the early ’sixties, but
the climate crippled him. He had numerous
children well known in the profession, among
them George, Jack and Cyril Ford, Mrs.
G. P. Carey, Mrs. G. Lister and Mrs. Allan
Hamilton.

Tom Pollard, who nowadays x-uns a M.L.
West Coast pictui’e-show, is taking a holi-
day, and is spending it in rehearsing a
Christchurch crowd in “H.M.S. Pinafoi-e.”
It is just over 35 years since the first Pol-
lard Lilliputian Opera Cos. struck Maori-
land. Arriving at the Bluff, two days later
the co. opened with “H.M.S. Pinafoi*e” at
Sloan’s Theatre, Invercargill. Tom Pollai’d
was stage manager and Fred Duval repre-
sented the old firm of Dunning and
Reynolds, who in those days had a fixun grip
of the leases of the principal M.L. theatres.
The following was the cast: Sir Joseph
Porter, Maud Pollard; Captain Coi’coran,
Herbert Salinger; Ralph Rackstraw, Con-
nie Osmond ; Dick Deadeye, Dick Osmond;
Boatswain, Joe Sheehan; Boatswain’s Mate,
Albert Goodwin; Buttercup, Olive Pollard;
Cousin Hebe, Lena Salinger; and Josephine,
May Pollard. The musical conductor was
J. J. Pollard, jun.; the leader of the
orchestra, C. A. Pollard; the pianist, Alice
Pollard; the organist, Nellie Pollard; the
flautist, Fred Pollard. The list only wants
somebody called Bran to make it read like a
market report.
“Stargazer”: She had made quite a com-
motion in the vaudeville world at the outset,
and she went on occasioning considerable
noise. Then because it was always the same
noise people got tired, and began to betray
a willingness to let her go off when she was
through with her programmed items. There
came a time when an infinitely better artist
who created no particular atmospheric dis-
turbance took well and gave pleasure to all.
The new one became a subject of general cop-
versation, and the house was loaded nightly;
but the old one still acted as if confident
that she was the responsible person, making
little glad speeches thanking everyone for
coming to see her. “This crowded house
speaks for itself,” said she. Eventually the
house did speak for itself. “There are
others!” it said. She was equal to the occa-
sion. “But they can’t keep you away!” she
cried. At this stage the management inter-
vened and barred speeches.
“Glen Huntly”: The noisiest perform-
ances of Italian opera I have heard are
l ' those now being given at Mel-
bourne Princess’s, not because of
what is done on the stage, but
because of what happens off. At
“Gavalleria Rustic-ana” I was
forced to the conclusion that the
Gonsalez Mutual Improvement
Society was holding a Mock Par-
liament behind the scenes. Some-
times a gentle buzz like “the
murmur of lonian bees” was
wafted from the wings, but now
and then the sound would swell
to something like “the hoarse
magnificence of rushing stones,”
punctuated by barks and yelps.
Most of the chorus, too, seem
lately to have had their boots
half-soled and heeled, it being a
long time since we’ve seen any-
body who could get off the stage
with more noise and remain off
less silently. If the row went
on at some shows one could name
it would not matter ; but just at
present nearly all the people at the Princess’s
happen to be worth listening to.
“Stargazer” : “Quinney’s,” produced at the
Repertory Co.’s new Playhouse (Melb.),
may be quoted with several other McMahon
productions as an example *f the danger of
the Repertory drift towards puerility in its
quest of something entirely different. The
aim of the Repertory Cos. is to be different;
but if the difference entails a conspicuous
inferiority, there is no occasion for this new
movement to assert itself. There was noth-
ing in “Quinney’s” but the fair acting of
Arthur St.van as J. Quinney, and the com-
mercial theatre has given us many oppor-
tunities of seeing the good acting of Arthur
Sty an. “Quinney’s” is a thin comedy—at
any rate, it appeared thin to attenuation as
presented-—with a vagueness in the char-
acter-drawing that even an experienced pro.
like Styan could not quite cover up. If.
after all, there is so little meaty material
for a repertory theatre to operate upon that
recourse to rather mild dramatic renderings
of small family grievances like “Quinney’s”
is necessary, the movement is not going to
contribute to our happiness and uplift as
liberally as I once fondly imagined.
An Australian’s letter from London :—-
One day who - should come along hut Corporal
Hareourt Beatty, home from France on seven days’
leave from St. Eloi! He looked a picture of health,
and younger by years, and he’s 51. Before he
joined he was doing special-constable work for
months—after the show at night, from 12 till
four. That’s what I call a man. I hope to Heaven
he comes back safely at the finish. Another Aus-
tralian player who joined here is Cyril Bell, who
married Mrs. Robert Brough. Bell is now a sub-
lieut. on H.M.S. Hermione. Before getting his
naval appointment he served with Kitchener’s Army
in the north of France, all through the Armen tiers,
Ypres, La Bassee and Loos affairs. His division
was in the thick of it at Loos. While out there
he got his call from the Admiralty. One English
mummer who came tearing home from the States
to get into it was Pope Stamper, a great favorite in
musical comedy in U.S'.A. His wife is a niece of
Emma Temple, Bessie Major and Mrs. Brough.
Stephen Phillips, poet and playwright, of
Britain, dead a while back, left a fortune of
£5, and no will to dispose of it.
“Lantern”: As I turn the handle, it seems
to me that Chaplin is at last tiring of his
library of monkey-tricks. I saw him last
night in one disconnected chapter of a stupid
and confused serial which ended with him
hosing an entire company. It wasn’t funny;
it was merely pathetic. Perhaps the poor
scenario had depressed the modern Grim-
aldi ; or maybe it is the languor of riches
that is slowing him down. If the new him
firm which bought him at boom rates can t
get better stuff out of him, I don’t like the
promoters’ chances of making a fortune.
Anyhow, the world deserves a change from
Charley.

Adelaide had four inches of rain in the
last week of June, which made it the wettest
week for 40 years; and Sin. for the month,
which made it the moistest on the entire
local record.
These are cold days for dance-giving
bachelors of military age. At Lithgow
(N.S.W.), when the hosts of a “bachelors
ball” in the town hall arrived at the scene
of the intended shivoo, they found the hall
tastefully decorated with recruiting posters,
kindly contributed by their lady guests as a
relief from the monotony of white feathers.
The orchestra expressed its feelings in the
matter by playing “Your King and Country
Need You” and “Boys of the Dardanelles,
instead of more hackneyed waltz tunes.
Altogether, the young men had . such a
pleasant evening.
The Hobart Bumbles intend to stick to
their war-time economy of saving £4OO per
year by removing garbage only once a week.
They are fortified by their health officer,
who says it really doesn’t matter whether
they gather up the muck once a week or
once a month as long as they follow the
present practice of dumping it all down in
a heap along the water-front of the town.
He has, on his own confession, been advocat-
ing a destructor for 18 years, but the same
old stinksome method is going strong, and
is good apparently for another 50 years.
A month ago this paper’s “Woman’s
Letter” mentioned the departure of the lady
who was formerly Gertrude Tait to join her
husband in London. Captain Lewis, who
set out for the war soon after it started,
had never seen his firstborn; and, as he
had arranged for furlough in July, Mrs.
Lewis went off to show him the family
treasure. How horrible war is! Mrs.
Lewis’s last weeks in Sydney were clouded
by the death of her brother, Lieut. Tait;
on her arrival in England by the Osterley
this week she will learn that her husband
was killed in action in France on June IS.
Ir; years past she was matron of the Thurs-
day Island and Darwin hospitals and was
superintendent nurse in Papua; and a mul-
titude of Northern men who have admired
her skill and courage will be full of sym-
pathy for her. Before leaving for the Front
Capt. Lewis was head of the Cable Guard at
Port Darwin.
“Croson”: Next to making me a bishop
(that’s a nice, clean, well-paid trade) I
always regret that my parents didn’t turn
me into a military officer, preferably in the
commissariat department. Then, in addi-
tion to receiving a good wage, I should
have the pleasure of spending huge sums of
money for which no one would ever call me
to account. Had such been my luck I would
have tried to emulate those who paid £214
for the hire of a horse, cart and man for
10 months, to now and then hump a few
rations to South Head fort, Port Jackson.
Of course four or five turnouts could have
been bought for the money, but then if you
buy a turnout you’ve got to feed the horse
and keep the cart in repair and mend the
harness. Besides, the horse might die, and
then you’d have to chase after another, with
a fair certainty of being taken in. It is
much easier to let somebody else worry—
especially about the bill.
After the German “victory” off Jutland,
the Imperial liar asked the small neutrals
to be glad that “the freedom of the seas”
had been restored. Precisely what the small
neutrals are to understand by “tlie freedom
of the seas” Dernberg, the German Secre-
tary for the Colonies, announced in the
Reichstag some years ago when Burns, Philp
and Cos. were struggling for the Pacific
trade:—
Australian competition in the South Seas is verj'
keen, and this competition will have to be driven
off the field. The N.D.L. has succeeded in driving
from the field very keen rivals for the +rade between
New Guinea and Australia—rivals who made it very
difficult for Germans to place their wares, and who
gave preference to English and Australian goods.
Dernberg asked for the usual subsidy and
got it. Also, by the way, the patriotic
shippers of Australia handed their usual
subsidy in the same direction, so that Ger-
man shipping in and out of Commonwealth
ports increased from 845,000 tons in 1909 to
1,211,404 tons in 1913.
Maoriland’s proposal to impose an addi-
tional rate on incomes exceeding £3OO found
Fat unperturbed. He put it thus to the
newspaper man: —
As to the burden of the taxation, I do not think
that the large companies will find it more than they
can bear. They have the old refuge—they can pass
it on to the public.
True patriots all, for be it understood we
theft our country for our company’s good.
AN UNPOETIC DREAM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rev. Canon Maxwell Ilomfray Gumbleton, selected
for the Bishonric of Ballarat, was formerly known
as Canon Smith, but changed his surname some time
ago in accordance with the provisions of a legacy.
The bishop erst was Canon Smith ;
But one among his kin and kith,
Who deemed his name of humble ton,
Induced him with a golden bribe
To drop the badge of all his tribe,
And dub himself a Gumbleton.
The name that I subscribe herewith
Has more renown, just now, than Smith,
Which is the least I’d say for it;
Yet, heedless of my noble blood,
I’d change my famous name to Mud
Did gold but pave the way for it.
By any other name this rose
Would bloom as fair in verse or prose—
A name's a paltry, penny thing.
If some bad uncle, deaf and dear,
Left me a thousand pounds a year,
The Court might call me Anything.
VI.CTpKIA.
EDMUND FISHER.

SOCIETYPENEOLDS
WINES
Canon Maxwell Gumbleton, viee.v of
Chippenham (Eng.), is to be the new Bishop
of Ballarat, succeeding Dr. Green, who
retired last year. A Board to make such
selections as this is kept alive in each dio-
cese, members being elected from time to
time by the local synod. But in the final
issue such Boards of course receive guidance
from Above. According to an interesting
romance in a Melbourne daily as to the
Board proceedings at Ballarat, no less than
12 names w T ere “submitted” for the con-
sideration of the selectors, eight of them
Australian and four English. Presently this
list was allegedly reduced to three—“a
bishop in Australia, a priest in Victoria, and
Canon Gumbleton.” Then the final choice
was made. As a matter of fact, no Aus-
tralian was ever formally considered for
the position. It was a very simple affair.
After the matter had remained open for a
time and been discussed openly and secretly
and in the street and in the vestry and over
the washtub and the walnuts, Gumbleton
was favorably mentioned to the Board by a
certain party. Whereupon everybody on
the Board said with one voice: “Why, cer-
tainly, if you say so!” —and Gumbleton it
was.
TIT FOR TAT.
“Come on now, Mac. Sing ‘A Man’s a Man
before you go.”
“ It’s gey late and it’s a noisy sang—what
wull the neighbors say?”
“Oh, blow the neighbors! They poisoned
my cat yesterday.”
Out of a population of 1000 or so King
Island, the lonely speck in the middle of
Bass Straits, has sent 237 men to join the
khakis. It expects to send a few more, but
there are not many left now.
Hats off to the memory of a brave Aus-
tralian girl! Mabel Rimington, of Casino
(N.S.W ), aged IS, took little brothers Eric
(aged three) and Leslie (10) for a w r alk
along the river bank one evening last Octo-
ber. Eric fell in. Mabel jumped in after
him, and struggled with him to land, pushing
him far enough up the steep bank for Leslie
to clutch him and haul him up to safety.
Then Leslie held a stick to help her clamber
out; but knowing that her weight would
only pull him in, too, she said: “No, you
aren’t strong enough. Run home and get
help.” But by the time help came Mabel
had slipped back and was drowned. At a
public meeting in the Casino Town Hall
the other afternoon Bob Bell, of the North
Coast Shipping Co., and Charley Metcalfe,
a well-known Sydney citizen, acting on
behalf of the Humane Society, handed
Mabel’s family the Society’s certificate for
her bravery.
* * * *
The latest casual Australian to be men-
tioned takes life easy in South Melbourne.
His name is Sharpe. On a recent evening
his wife told him she was going to a picture-
show. He made no objection, and went out
for a stroll. On returning he found her
lying on the bed, fully dressed, apparently
asleep. With gentle regard for her feelings
Sharpe retired to rest without disturbing
her. On waking next morning he found her
still in the same position. Unperturbed he
left for his work, and on coming back at
midday, although there was no meal pre-
pared and his wife had not yet altered her
position on the bed, he did not worry—lie
just went back to liis job. Coming home in
the evening, however, and finding things
exactly as he had left them, lip reckoned the
missus’s siesta was a bit too prolonged. So
he told her it was about a fair thing—
and discovered that she was dead. Since
then the female friend who was accompany-
ing Mrs. Sharpe to the theatre has informed
the bereaved husband that on the way to
the city his wife fell off a tram, she
brought her home and left her. Which
seems to have been a trifle casual, too.
* * * *
“Quidani” : A word that is putting in a
lot of overtime lately is “traditions.” Even
Jellicoe remarks that “the glorious traditions
of the British Navy were most worthily up-
held.” Every day it is nointed out that
though something or other didn’t quite come
off, somebody or other upheld the glorious
. traditions handed down
by some dead person.
The glorious traditions of
the past may be right
enough, but such a lot
of inglorious traditions
handed down from the
«same dim period have
caused so much trouble
that nobody wants to lie
awake at night to do rev-
erence to the word. And
a whole lot of the most
inglorious were reckoned
glorious till they pulled
us right into the dirt.
There were, for instance,
those glorious traditions
about one volunteer being
equal to 10 pressed men,
and conscription being
necessarily inferior to the
British voluntary system
because it was adopted
by Continental Powers —
these and others have
served to humbug a na-
tion into allowing its
leaders to collect much
credit by doing little but
talk. Now that the Rus-
sians are doing some-
thing definite in the way
of advances, it is inter-
esting to note that the
cables don’t wax eloquent
explaining how the Bear
is upholding the glorious
traditions of the past.
The Bear’s traditions are
left alone, but nice, reas-
suring, solid facts are set
forth showing that he is
raising Cain in the way
he is wanted to. Until
business on other fronts
proceeds on the same
lines, no one wants to
have the fact that we haven’t advanced, or
have slightly advanced backwards, palliated
by reference to what our great-grandfathers
did.

“Petifi”: I notice that N.S.W. Justice
Harvey has been giving a Sydney girls’
school sartorial advice :
At the present time I see many women wearing
ham frills around their necks. I strongly advise you
not to wear them. A ham frill is only becoming to a
person with a face like a ham.
Would it be right to say, then, that no
person should wear a horse’s tail on his
head unless he has a head like a horse’s
hindquarters?

From Ted Colles :
His job was somewhere along a narrow, much-
used international waterway, and each morning the
patrols would report back to their headquarters,
which in turn would wire results to a greater
H.Q’s, which would consolidate the lot in one wire
to supreme H.Q’s —“Canal all clear,” or whatever
it might be. As often happened, there was some
delay the other morning in the arrival of the two
minor ll. Q’s reports; and while the sergeant
was roaring up the signal-office, Billjim, the new
office-orderly, was shuffling around with a home-
made birch broom. Came a sharp b-r-r-r! from the
’phone. Billjim woke up, dropped his broom and
grabbed the receiver. From the mightiest ll. Q’s
came a typical English staff-officer’s voice:
“Hi! Aaa-Aaa—aw —er — what 1 Is that So-and-
So head-quaata/is? Eh?”
“Yairs,” drawled Billj.
“Well, Ai saay, y’know! ” continued the voice
fiercely, “what th’ deuce d’you people mean! Thet
confounded canal report not in yet! And”
But Billj. wasn’t familiar with the canal-report
ceremonial, and said something to that effect into
the ’phone. There followed a passage Of vague and
unintelligible questions and answers, and the Voice
became more and more hysterical:
“Whai, demmitall, man ! Who are you ? D’you
know the taime? It’s after eight o’clock and you
haven’t got the confounded canal clear yet—-eh—
what t”
The dazed look on Bill’s face gave way to one of
desperate boredom.
“Gawstrewth! ” he drawled, “I got one scrag uv
a broom t’ clean this place with before eight
o’clock. Surely t’ cripes it ain’t my job t’ sweep
th’ blanky canal!”
A spluttering sound came over the wire—just as
the sergeant dashed in with a signal form in his
hand, and, shoving Billj. aside, grabbed the receiver
and saved the situation.
Next morning Billjim was requested into the
presence of G. 5.0.2, who held a memo, from supreme
H.Q’s in his hand, and there he received kindly
advice on the correct manner of conferring with
staff-officers in general, and Imperial staff-captains in
particular.

“Pat O’M.”: What sense is there in hav-
ing these public holidays in the mid-winter
months? Last week under a grey sky a
shivering few tried to feel glad while setting
forth on excursions to places where they
remembered the shine of the sun on happier
occasions. Even if the King did have the
doubtful joy of being born in the depth
of the Australian winter, why should loyalty
BLANCHE SWEET IN A COMING
LASKY FILM, “THE CASE OF BECKY."
A Photodrama of a dual personality.
Balzamo (Theodore Roberts) fiefs the ’fluenee
over Dorothy (Blanche Sweet).
to his office or person demand a stoppage
of work at a time when the odds are against
me holiday-makers having a good time?
Why not take all these winter holidays out
of the calendar and pool them into one good
carnival in the spring? A carnival extend-
ing over several days in the time when
wattles stir the poet’s soul to make “dawn”
rhyme with “morn” might come to be a
feature of Australian life. The old Romans
knew the value of such a time for letting off
steam, and their example has been followed
in many European towns and villages all
through the ages. To go reasonably mad
for one- week in the year would be about
the best safeguard against going stupidly
insane for the other 51. Anyhow, holidays,
to do any lasting good, should be consumed
in pints, not nobblers. Put into a big
measure and filled with sunshine and frivo-
lity, we could keep all the commemorations
out of the business, and go on the honest
basis of taking time off when we wanted
it and not when an accident of birth, death
or marriage gave us the arbitrary call to
break holts with toil.

The Y.M.C.A. is now spending nearly
£lOOO a week in providing free writing-
paper and envelopes for Australian soldiers
in various parts of the world. It is figures
like these which attract the wealthy old
fellow who ordinarily has no time for any-
thing with a “religious” label, but in whose
well of sentiment a few pints of human
feeling still remain. As a result much of
the Y.M.G.A.’s war-cash comes from men
who haven’t seen the inside of a Sabbath
collection-plate for years.
* * * *
“Truthful James”: Here’s another gritty
Anzac in the making. The other Saturday
night young Lovett, of Ardlethan (N.S.W.),
a boy of 18, tumbled off the back step of a
coach and broke his leg. The passengers
did not notice the accident, and the rattle
of an adjacent battery drowned his cry for
help. When he had steeled his nerves to
it. he crawled 300 yards over very rough
ground to the mine office and asked for
something to bind his leg together. The
sound leg was considerably worn during the
crawl, but the lad’s chief concern was the
expense his accident was thrusting on his
parents. A brother, by the way, went under
in Gallipoli, and his officer in a private
letter to the parents said their lad was one
of Australia’s gamest.

“Rifle Ranger” : On the march in France
the other day we passed through an Eng-
lish Tommies’ camp which was marked by
beautiful lawns, artistic arbors and rustic
seats. These lawns looked like corners of
some “swell” estate at ’Ome, and they cer-
tainly were a credit to Atkins. Then I
thought of the d —d bully beef and how
much better it would go with an onion or
a carrot grown on the spaces where
“fatigues” had been employed for months
raising grass and buttercups. I admit at
once that Major Fitzmontmorency is
entitled to his comforts, too —but bully beef
and onions! :

“Not-at-All” from the Bar table: —
The present congestion in the Ma State’s Supreme
Court, where the cause-list is never finished by the
end of the jury sittings and the Full Court finds the
greatest difficulty in disposing of appeal cases set
down for term, reminds ope of the remedy attempted
in England at the time of the 1831 Reform Bill.
About then the Chancery Court was thronged by
litigants seeking equitable remedies and seldom find-
ing them on this side of the grave. Lord Chancellor.
Harry Brougham made great efforts to clear away
some of the debris. lie sat all day and well into the
night doing his best to fulfil the promises he had
made as Radical leader advocating law reform. He
often sat on public holidays, and even dealt with
an important trust matter on one bright Good
Friday morning—which led an embittered critic to
remind him that he was the “first Judge in any coun-
try who has ever presided in his court on a Good
Friday since Pontius Pilate.”
Of the N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench, Pring,
J., is the only one at all likely to emulate the
keenness of Harry Brougham or Pontius Pilate.
Pring is as keen on his work to-day as a generation
ago when his zeal was the despair of the Junior Bar.
The wig least likely to do so is Justice Dick Sly,
whose successs at golf lends much lustre to the
Bench. Chief Justice Cullen does a big share of the
common-law work, and generally falls in for lengthy
resumption cases; but he never fails to cut out
Saturday-morning work. Gordon and Street, JJ.,
have the most varied duties, the former having re-
cently, within a week, sat in the Common Law,
Equity and Divorce jurisdictions; and the latter,
within 10 days, in Common Law, Equity, Bank-
i uptcy, Probate, and Admiralty. Ferguson does the
lion’s share of chamber work; and the two other

Equity Judges, Simpson and Harvey, have heavy

hung up by the numerous resumption cases on which

moves and gets his living. What old Harry
Brougham, L.C., forgot was that speedy settlement
of litigation mostly satisfies no one, and does in fact
not produce a real settlement at all. And the Ma
State’s Court makes up in sureness for what it lacks
in speed, and that is not a bad thing. The remedy
for congested lists is not speedier justice, but more
Judges.

“B.B.”: There’s too much brain-fag about
this war. Just when I was hopelessly
tangled Sydney Daily Telegraph
(29/6/T6) rushed to my aid with the expla-
nation that “everybody knew that both
armies” (French and British) “were acting
in complete accord.” Now I know how we
stand; all mists are cleared away and
doubt is dispelled by the searchlight of
fatuousness or some such illuminant.
“Devilfish”: After about seven months on
the job and a good look at the N. S. Wales
trawlers. Prof. Flynn, the
Speck’s Royal Commissioner on
Fisheries, has told all about it.
He advises the Government to
charter boats or make contracts
with fishermen for a supply of
fish, to put up cool stores to
keep them in, and to open shops
or subsidise hawkers to dis-
tribute them. This is for a
start till the State can get
trawlers of its own, and Flynn
reckons motor-boat or steam
trawlers costing from £.lOO to
£2OOO each would fill the bill.
Where you can get a £2OOO
steam trawler in these days he
doesn’t tell. He says that
nearly all the Tasmanian fish
are suitable for canning, and lie
can only suppose that either
want of capital or lack of tech-
nical knowledge, or both, have
been responsible for the failure
of‘past ventures. In the case
of the cannery, now deceased,
at St. Helens (whose obituary-
notice appeared in The Bulle
tin recently) the canned pro-
duct was well up to the stan-
dard of the imported Yankee
article, and was greatly in
demand. Flynn says the Gov-
ernment ought to encourage can-
neries and the like, but omits
to mention how; and the how is
the really important thing in
these matters. In fact, it is
ostensibly to learn how that
Governments appoint commis-
sions. The man at the corner could tell
them the rest. <
* * * *
From one of TirE Bulletin’s Billjims in
France:—•
Away in the north we hear a rumble; gradually it
grows louder until we see the flashes of guns and
distinguish the ripping of bursting shrapnel. The
order is given to “stand to.” Two minutes and the
message is ’phoned back to headquarters, “Q Com-
pany all correct.”
The din is now terrific. More of our guns have
opened and are being answered by those of the enemy.
Shells are bursting over and behind our parapet, and
‘No Man’s Land” is as bright as day with flares from
either side. Orders are given to prepare for a gias
attack. Every man puts on his helmet.
Our breastwork trembles. A terrific roar and cloud
of dust tells that something has got pretty close.
At times our parapet is like a boat in a swell, it
rocks so much. My good cobber takes it smiling,
cracks a joke or smokes a fag, and sympathises with
the “poor blankers” on our left who seem to be
getting it hotter than we.
A message comes to take all casualties to the dress-
ing station. Thank God, we can report: “None so
far.” But in the next platoon a shell has come
through, blowing one man to pieces and wounding
two others.
Whatever the peril, the stretcher-bearers do not
tarry. The wounded are quickly administered first-
aid and carried back nearly a mile, along a trade
where there is practically no cover, and over which
shrapnel falls like rain.
The noise dies down and in 15 minutes all is quiet.
Excepting that the parapet has been blown in,
strewing bags and loose dirt everywhere, and that
our officer gives us the good word and hands each man
a liberal rum, one would never know there had been
anything doing. But we have been under fire for
an hour and a half.
* * * *
The Dentists (Amendment) Act which
was due to come into force in N. »S. Wales
on July 1 is not very drastic legislation;
but it tightens up the law in regard to the
quack tootlismith. When the original Act
brought some kind of order into the unregu-
lated dental business, opportunity was given
to men who had been practising to become
registered; but it was found that so long
as the fang-pluggers did not claim to be
dentists, they could give the public H—l
and charge for it without registration.
Generally they ran their gilded Inquisitions
as “Dental Parlors” or “Dental Com-
panies” ; and the Law on occasional out-
breaks of activity used to send its emis-
saries to make sure the practitioners didn’t
want to call themselves dentists. If they
didn’t use the sacred word, they could do
what they liked with their customers’ teeth.
The new Act gives extra grace to the out-
siders : the man who has been practising for
five years may continue to do so for another
three, and then secure registration upon
passing some examination; but a fine of
from £2 to £2O will be waiting for any
other person who undertakes to repair the
public’s machinery.
“Enzedder”: Recently the Auckland
Star came out in a rash of headlines: “Had
to Surrender. Could Hold Out No Longer.”
Beneath there was an account of how an old
German resident in M.L. had been compelled
to apply to the police for assistance, owing
to his remittances from Germany having been
cut off since the war. As the old man is a
brother of Ballin, the Hun shipping chief,
the Germans will probably translate the
article, and point out that the impotent
English have been reduced to bragging about
such small beer.

“Bill Bedsox”: We had one of those
quiet but pretty weddings the other day. At
the time fixed for it neither the cake nor the
bride’s teeth had come to hand. However,
the absence of these odds and ends was not
permitted to interfere with the course of
events. Not having the teeth, the bride
didn’t want the cake, and she nodded and
clicked her tongue when asked if she would
have this man.
DRINKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The shortage of whisky is due to the fact that
the raw spirit is being utilised against the Allies’
enemies. It is being turned into a high explosive.
The whisky that I used to take
Now makes the guarded hillsides quake;
And spouting shellbursts on the lip
Of gunpits mark my morning nip.
The drink I used to take at lunch
Compels some frightened Hun to hunch
His shoulders now. I love to think
How little he enjoys his drink.
My “nightcap” ! Ah, they sleep too well
Who take that flaming draught from Hell!
As far guns roll across the sea,
I mutter: “Fritz! have one with me!”
x.s.w.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
Paris Nesbit, the brilliant Adelaide law-
yer*, is now much better in health, and it has
been represented to him that his reasons for
lately resigning his K.C.-ship were hardly
sufficient. So the S. Aus. Government has
formally rescinded its acceptance of his resig-
nation, and things are as they used to be.
Meanwhile his son and former partner, R. G.
Nesbit. has been appointed head of the new
Licensing Court.
SENATOR NEEDHAM (W.A.) at a
fancy-dress ball.
Maorilanders have been recalling a long-
ago tragedy in the Kitchener family. Lieut.-
Col. Henry Horatio Kitchener, father of K.
of K., owned a. property at Waihemo, Otago.
To manage this property his nephew, Captain
Henry Kitchener, who had sold his commis-
sion in the army, immigrated to M.L. with
his family. On July 1, 1882, while they
were asleep in their residence at Dunedin, it
was discovered by a belated citizen to be in
flames. Mrs. Kitchener and a baby were
saved by a policeman, and two of the boys
leaped to safety from a window ; but three
girls, Susan, Sydney and Edith, the oldest'
11, perished. Their father died two days
later.
BETTER THAN THE BEST.
FACETIOUS NON-BIDDER (to perspiring
auctioneer who can’t raise a bid for
dilapidated violin): “Is it a Strad?”
A UCTIONEER: “Oh, no, nothing like that!
It’s by a good maker.”
They are everywhere. A late mail
brought two letters from Sydney boys in
remote places—one from young Pursell, of
an old-established insurance-broking family ;
the other from Leonard Dodds’s son. The
former was with the Armored Car Section
which the British Navy has lent to Russia ;
the latter with a motor corps in East Africa
under Botha. Pursell had wintered in Lap-
land, the vessel in which he and his lot left
for Archangel having been caught in the
ice ; “Ted” Dodds wrote from the slopes of
Kilimanjaro, the snow-capped peak which
pushes itself up 19.000 ft. above the East
African plains. The usual heavy rains
had begun to fall in April, and the motor
corps was having a bad time in a country
described as very much like the N. S. Wales
North Coast, with the addition of monkeys,
humming birds, giraffes, various kinds of
buck, and flocks of canaries.
Jimmie Mirams, a remainder of the
Berry brigade who passed out in Melbourne
the other day, got off a fair retort now and
then. Just after Service bad been badly
dished, Mirams, on rising in the House, was
greeted with some jest about his lack of
inches. “Do not sympathise with me, gen-
tlemen,” lie said. “I am far from being as
small as my opponents feel at this moment.”
Visiting Ballarat as one of Graham Berry’s
bodyguard, be was the victim of various
practical jokes. On the first night some-
body placed an infant’s high chair on the
platform. conspicuously labelled. “For
Jimmie Mirams.” Jimmie, who had just
been made a father, gravely added the word
“Junior,” and took the chair away with him.

R. T. Vale, for many years a member of
the Vic. Leg. Assembly, died the other day,
aged 80. He was a brother of W. M. K. Vale,
who made more noise as member and Min-
ister. In fact, the noise was a riot in the
days of his beloved colleague, “Mint Sauce”
Jones.
A D.C.M. has been won by Sergeant
Charles Wheeler, 22nd Battalion Royal
Fusiliers, who 18 months ago laid down the
mahlstick for the rifle. Born in Dunedin,
Wheeler began to study at Melbourne
National Gallery nine years ago, and got
together enough money to take him to Lon-
don. He there displayed fine talent in draw-
ing and painting: one picture of his, “The
Poem,” was bought by the Felton Bequest.
His portrait of John Madden hangs m tne
Melbourne University.

has just died in an East Melbourne private
hospital. Like William de Morgan, the
novelist, Fisher, w r ho
came of an old French
family in Warwick-
shire, served articles
with one of the Cold
Land’s leading stained
glass firms, and
reached Australia 35
years ago. Literally
one of the illUminati,
he worked up in Vic-

lished himself in 1890,
a world-wide reputa-
tion. Ilis windows
adorn the Canton Club
and buildings in South
Africa and Maoriland,
and many a ray
serene that penetrates
Menzies, the Oriental
and Collins House
first passes through
Fisher’s glass.

. Among the first to wear the Home Ser-
vice Medal, issued' to those who have volun-
teered for active service but have not been
accepted because they are doing indispens-
able work in Australia, was Defence Min-
ister Pearce. He must have blushed with
conscious worth, in his prospective capacity
as a soldier, to be assured by himself, in his
official capacity as Minister, that his ser-
vices in this unconscribed Commonwealth
are indispensable.
Proof of the effect of the tropics on the
white man is supplied by the Crane family
of Singapore, to which belongs Captain
Joseph Crane, of the jailer William Mit-
chell, now at Hobart. His grandfather
went east as a young man and held out
against the climate till he was 98. But
the captain’s father was cut off when he
was 87, and the pernicious effect on the
third generation may be judged from the
fact that of a family of 14 only eight are
now alive, and the captain, who is next
to the youngest of them, is but 71.
When the late “Jimmy” Mirams passed
out, a Melbourne daily printed the state-
ment that the deceased was probably the
last of those who sat in the Vie. Parlia-
ment of 1874-1877. Forthwith a loud mur-
mur arose from a lot of old gentlemen who
objected to being treated as dead. Actually
six members at least of that Parliament are
on deck —John Gavan Duffy, Simon Fraser,
Thomas Hunt, Robert Murray Smith, Rob-
ert Walker and John Madden. The Chief
Justice, by the way, was well on towards
the front stalls by that time. Tn ” r -i bo
was Solicitor-General in the McCulloch
Ministry; and during ’76, when Janus Ser-
vice formed his second Ministry, the bright
young man was Minister for Justice.

William Longbottom, dead in Adelaide at
84, was one of the founders of the big firm
of Colton and Co.; his son. of the same
name, is still a director of Colton, Palmer,
Preston and Co. —the old house with a new
roof. Like so many other prominent South
Australians, he was the son of a parson,
the first of the William Longbottoms having
been shipwrecked on the coast of S. Aus. in
answer—so the Methodists of infant Adel-
aide declared—to their prayers for a
spiritual guide. What the shipwrecked
sailors thought about it is not recoided.
William D. Bear, imported from London
32 years ago to be supt. of the reorganised
lire brigades of Sydney, died last week,
aged 70. Of his first paid corps, formed
in July, 1884, there are four men still in
the service.
Dead on June 30, Russell Barton, at the
age of 85. At one time he represented the
men and sheep of the Bourke district in the
I.eg. Ass. He and his brother were pioneer
storekeepers at Cobar.
One Melbourne citizen who has been
watching the situation in the U.S. with
feverish interest is old Alexander McKinley,
of. Paunch. If the U.S. should be “dragged
into” the European disturbance on the side
of the Allies and the Americo-Mexican row
develops into a war, Mexico will be all
enemy country, or thereabouts. McKinley
is consul for Mexico and fond of appearing
in public in a lovely consular uniform of
black cloth covered with silver sequins and
braided as to the pants with broad silver
strips; and to throw this beautiful uniform
out of action would be nearly as bad as
death.
Died the other day, at Surrey Hills
(Vic.), Mrs. Bell, whose father, William
Harper, was a midshipman on the Bellero-
pbon when it took Napoleon across to Ply-
mouth before he was shipped to Saint
Helena. Harper came to Hobart in 1829,
and Mrs. Bell was born there. She was
taken to Victoria in 1838 and went to the
first school in Melbourne. Eighty-three.

£ s degree in the East Indies rubber business.
Apparently lie failed, for latterly be has

Grammar.
From an old political hack :—•
Irvine, K.C., never looked less like an iceberg than
he does to-day. Of late years he has taken on
a well-fed, mellow appearance which quite belies
his nickname. After dinner, gently flushed with
dry ginger-ale out of a champagne-glass, he would
pass for a prosperous suburban mayor in his
prime—-except for the whiskers. Irvine never
sported any kind of whisker. Long ago, before he
reached Parliament, he was a tall, pallid, liolioW-
cheeked person who seemed to have a secret sorrow
gnawing' at his breast-bone; and his clothes, collars
and conduct gave him the air of an undertaker’s
assistant at cut rates. But he has got over all that,
and now looks every inch the political boss.

HE: “Will you give me no hope?"
SHE: “None whatever — I’m thinking of

“Adel.”: A generation back there was
some talk in Adelaide over the three lawyer
brothers Downer conducting cases to be
decided by the fourth, who was S.M. and
Commissioner of Insolvency. A counsel son
has often “appeared” before a Judge father,
but it is doubtful if the position has ever
been reversed, as it is in Adelaide just now,
Paris Nesbit, K.C., being at the Bar while
his son, Reg., is Licensing Magistrate and
acting-S.M.
THREE GIRLS ON THE WHARF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s three girls waitin’ on the wharf.
Our ship is gratin’ in.
The other lads is trottin’ orf,
Each with his hero’s grin—
A snigger that is right and fit
On him that’s fairly done ’is bit.
I stand abaft the funnel here,
Accoutred like a torf;
There’s Mag and Nell and Annie dear
A-waitin’ on the wharf;
They’re gaily wavin’ somethin’ white—
Gorstruth, it is Der Tag all right!
That’s Gentle Annie by the stack....
’Ow do these things occur?
I swore if ever I come back
I’d sorter marry ’er.
A bloke don’t recollect too well—
Perhaps I said the same to Nell!
I met Ann at a ’op. We broke
A sprat to faithful be;
So ’elp me, never was a bloke
Romantical ez me.
And ’ere I’m creepin’ ’ome to-day...
Three on the wharf—oo-gaudy-ray !
There’s three girls waitin’ on the wharf —
It’s bunks for Privit Brown !
Tuck sudden with a ’orrid corf,
They'll wheel me into town!
I've parched, ’n’ bled, ’n’ froze, Ti' fought,
But ain’t the ’ero that I thought.
I gave Nell, with me fondest love,
Me photo, smilin’ bright;
She ’anded me a cotton glove
To be ’er shinin’ knight.
To Margrit, though, upon me life,
I promised nix. She’s just me wife.
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

IN JUDICIOUS SPENDING LIES THE
PRACTICE OF REAL ECONOMY.
THE present-day call for economy emphasises the inducement—a saving of 2/- in the £ which
ofleis in the reduction in price of all “Austral” Suits to Order during Sale Time, which
commenced on Wednesday, June 28, and continues till Saturday, July 22.
The actual money saved, however, would not represent judicious spending if the price reductions
merely meant a price reduction—but this Sale Time offer is not merely one of Tailoring at a lower
price, since it carries with it —not only a' plain promise—but an assured fulfilment in the truly-
enhanced values, quite irrespective of the price.
However much less might be paid during the Sale Time, the standard of “Austral”
Tailoring does not vary, so that the actual full measure of saving is at once shown
by a reference and comparison of the usual to the Sale price.
And because of this, those of our Customers who have asked for Suiting Patterns
prior to the Sale, do not need to send for a further range in order to take advantage
of the Sale prices which now prevail.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Last week General-manager Teece, of the
A.M.P. Society, completed 50 years of ser-
vice ; and the head-office staff and the dis-
trict secretaries met to tell him what they
thought of him, and in reply he told them
what he thought of them—though probably
on neither side was it quite what they have
sometimes told their wives. But, at any
rate, between them they have established a
brilliant record, making the A.M.P. the
greatest mutual office in the British Empire.
Fifty years ago the funds totalled £310,000;
to-day they are £35,000,000. Then there
were 0000 policies; now there are 450,000.
Meantime £20,000,000 has been distributed
in cash bonuses; that is to say, policy-hold-
ers have been enabled to buy £20,000,000
worth of insurance in addition to what the
premiums were calculated to return a tri-
bute alike to good management and the
healthiness of Australia.
The remarkable success of the A.M.P. is
all the more striking and instructive because
the society is a complete negation of most
of the principles upon which Australia has
in the past been governed. Until the last
few years the idea of public ownership has
had few influential friends. We have been
told that private enterprise and proprietorial
interest were the only things that could be
relied on to make a big commercial success
of anything. Yet the A.M.P. proudly adver-
tises 'the fact that it has no shareholders,
and upon this it relies largely for its custom.
The men who have made it a success have
got none of the profits of the business. They
have had their salaries and their pride m
the great institution —and no more. If there
ever was a shining example of the advan-
tages of co-operation it is here. Yet, curi-
ously enough, Australia is woefully bare of
co-onerative enterprises outside life insur-
ance ; with the result that, say, the farmers
of Australia, who can afford to waste money
less than almost any class, unnecessarily
pay away probably £750,000 a year. And
the people who talk most about the magnifi-
cence of the A.M.P.’s achievements don’t
raise a hand to help them.
And then there is the odious Freetrade
idea that the little local thing is not worth
making some small sacrifice for. Had Aus-
tralians been as loyal to Australian indus-
tries as they have been to Australian life
insurance there might easily have been to-
day a score of manufacturing concerns as
great as the A.M.P., and because of their
greatness working at so low a cost that they
could offer Australians equal advantages.
Further, there is absolutely no. reason why
Australia should not be also doing for itself
the whole of its fire insurance. Instead, foreign
offices have received wholesale support, and
until the last few years hardly anybody
turned into an Australian fire office because
it was Australian. In consequence there is
no huge Australian fire office; and because
they all have to struggle for business with 30
or 40 foreign competitors, costs are high and
rates are high. It is Australian property-
owners’ own fault that they are not enjoying
the same advantages that accrue to the life-
policy-holders, who, as mentioned above, have
received from this one A.M.P. office alone
£20,000,000 of cash bonuses in 50 years.
Finally there is the equally odious idea
that the foreigner can do the thing better.
Therefore we expect the Australian goods,
when we ask for them at all, to be cheaper
than the foreign stuff; and when we have a
big job to be done we import somebody to do
it—from the wearing of a bishop’s apron to
the construction or management of a rail-
way. Richard Teece, born in Maoriland,
was educated in Australia, and has spent
his whole working life here. The stair
which has helped him to achieve the A.M.P.’s
• great success is Australian, too. And nobody
doubts for a moment that when in April
next he retires, having reached the age of
70, an Australian will succeed him and set
about the great work of rounding up the
million and a quarter of Australasian people
who ought to be insured but still not.
From John I). Leckie: —
So that The Bulletin may add to its collection
of depreciated paper money I send one of the notes
issued by the tyrant Lopez during the Paraguayan
war in South America. It is interesting because the
case of Paraguay bears a curious parallel to that of
Germany. The note is for four dollars (about lt>
shillings at par), but before the close of the war it
was not worth more than the paper it was printed on.
And, as you will see, it wasn’t good paper.
The facts are as follows. In 1865 the South Ameri-
can republic of Paraguay became involved in war
with the combined forces of Argentina, Brazil and
Uruguay. Paraguay at that time was one of the
most prosperous and progressive republics in South
America. It had a larger population than Argen-
tina, and was ahead of that republic in railway con-
struction and internal development. Nevertheless
Lopez had to fight against great odds. But he had
been preparing for war for years, while the allied
"countries of Argentina, Brazil and Uruguay were
quite unprepared. On the outbreak of war a Para-
guayan flotilla invaded the Brazilian State of Matto
Grosso (nearly twice as large as N. S. Wales), and
occupied it with little resistance; and it was many
months before Brazil succeeded in regaining posses-
sion. To the southward Lopez himself, at the head
of 60,000 men, with 200 pieces of artillery, invaded
and over-ran the Argentine province of Corrientes;
then invaded Brazil (Rio Grande do Sul), where
later on one of his armies was defeated and compelled
to surrender.
Meanwhile the allies had been gradually collecting
troops, and Lopez, finding his communications threat-
ened, was compelled to retreat. For more than five
years Paraguay maintained the war against great
odds, more than once inflicting a crushing defeat on
the enemy; and the war was only ended by the
death of Lopez, who was surrounded and killed when
attempting to escape with the remnant of his army.
Paraguay’s war had points of resemblance to that
now being waged by Germany. Paraguay,likeGermanv,
was the aggressor; she also was surrounded and
blockaded ; she was at first successful in invading and
occupying enemy territory. Ultimately she was
forced to retreat, and was compelled to succumb to
superior forces, though only after putting up a
desperate fight. It was at this point that the papei
money, originally worth 16s. a sheet, fell to 2d. a
gallon.
There are many incidental points of resemblance.
For example, it is said that the Germans, owing to
shortness of metal, have melted down the church
bells for cannon. The same expedient was finally
adopted in Paraguay. On one occasion, when the
allied flotilla in the river thought itself secure, it
was surprised to find shells falling thick and fast.
A new heavy gun had arrived at the Paraguayan
front. This was known as “The Christian,” from
the fact that it was formed from old church bells.
So great was the scarcity of iron in the country that
the people, instead of hoes, were compelled to use
as a substitute the shoulder-blade of an ox tied to
the end of a stick. Soldiers were allowed extra
rations for any scraps of old metal found by them
.on the battlefield.
The translation of the inscription on the note
issued by Lopez is as follows:—“Forgers and their
accomplices will be prosecuted. The National
Treasury will pay to bearer the sum of four dollars,
value received.” But, though I relinquish to you all
my rights, I wouldn’t rely on the promise, if I
were you.

Sept.,’ls.. 15,628 1500 16 ~ ~ 16,232 2122 214,544
Mar.,’l6.. 33,505 1500 16 ~ „ 16,232 1632 228,685
Financially there is almost as much differ-
ence between this co.’s summer and winter
as there is between a farmer’s seed-time and
harvest; and it is rather a waste of money
to issue half-yearly accounts. Compared
with the previous six months the past six
seem to have been most fruitful; yet run
back over the table, and it will be seen
that no summer term of late has given such
poor net results. On the whole, though, it
is surprising that the co. has done_ so well.
Three things must have been cutting in to
the business: (1) Westralia’s dull times, the
result chiefly of drought in the farming dis-
tricts one year and too much rain the next;
(2) the early closing of pubs, ostensibly on
account of the war; (3) increased taxes.
The taxation is not. however, very heavy
yet, and the collector will probably take a
deeper cut by and bye. For the past six
months £3026 is charged to Profit and Loss
as against £2045 in the corresponding season
of 1914-15.
The investor wants to know (1) dividend
prospects and (2) the security for the prin-
cipal. The net profits for the past 12
months were £45,379, and the dividends took
all but £9915 of it. As an adequate amount
seems to have been first written off against
depreciation, things could get a bit worse
without imperilling the present dividend.
And, though general conditions in Westralia
may improve, it is likely that conditions
won’t improve for ‘‘the Trade.” Westralia
has, by the way, a problem the same as Ire-
land. ’ Part of it wants one thing and part
of it wants another; and Scaddan settled
the matter just as Asquith proposes to settle
Ireland’s trouble —by letting each have its
own way. The referendum is a democratic
instrument, as Happy Jack, being a Labor
Premier, knows; but as Happy Jack also
knew, the Goldfields weren’t going to put
up with it. democratic or not. if it meant
that'the city people and the farmers would
shut the miner’s pubs at 6 o’clock. This is
going to make people think. If the Perth
suburbs and the farming districts have no
right to dictate to the Goldfields as to what
their drinking habits shall be —or, if you
like, if the South of Ireland has no right to
dictate to the North of Ireland as to what
sort of Government it will have —what right
has Chatswood and Lindfield and Ivillara to
dictate to Broken Hill and Surry Ilills !
Nevertheless, right or wrong, the Trade,
taking it all round, is going to have a tougher
time than it has had hitherto. No doubt it
will be able to make a bit more here and
there; but it can’t afford to annoy many more
people, because, if it does, nationalisation
will arrive for certain. And that will make
shareholders look hard at the assets of
brewery cos. Suppose they had to go out,
what would shareholders recover V That has
become a more important question in con-
nection with brewery investments than ever
it was. In this case just half of the total
assets are hotel properties at cost —£276,627.
This paper hasn’t the least idea what that
lot would be worth if the licenses were lost.
Yet while there are these risks, there is a
bigger chance that breweries will go on mak-
ing fat profits. The stock is, however, no
longer one for the helpless maiden aunt to
put her little lot into.

Reserves are described as nominal because
the assets include goodwill, lumped in this
unusual entry : “By original purchase at cost
from the firm of Swallow and Ariell of free-
hold lands, buildings, machinery, plant, etc.,
including goodwill.” That item has stood like
that, with £148,500 against it, for 28 years.
It would be interesting to know what the
machinery, plant, etc., taken over from the
old firm 28 years ago is worth to-day. True,
the whole of the property, in a lump,
may be worth more than it was in 1888.
This paper doesn’t know. Plainly a balance-
sheet with an item showing a valuation in
1888 is of precious little value as a guide to
the value of the assets in 1916.
The column devoted to profits indicates
that one can’t take much notice of the net
return in any single year. The eo. is a good
deal like the Australian wheat farmer in
that respect; there are fat years and lean,
and the best one can do is to strike an aver-
age over a sufficiently long period. The
great variations in this co.’s profits are
mostly the result of great variations also in
the price of raw materials. As an average
the dividend for 10 years past has been a
shade over 6 p.c. But the question arises
whether it is a fair thing in this case to
base one’s calculations on an average,
because there had been a steady rise from
1905 until 1915, and that was probably due
to more or less permanently improved condi-
tions. Certainly the business seems to have
increased very much during this period:

The increase in book debts and stock sug-
gests that the turnover has grown very
much: and of course that ought to mean
more profits. Incidentally, liabilities have
grown, too, but not in the same proportion.
Ten years ago the co. owed £73,402, and its
assets (including the £148,500 before-men-
tioned) totalled £267,757. At the end of
April, 1916, liabilities were £94,142 more;
but assets were £224,680 more. That shows
an improvement of over £130,000 in the 10
years. But it wasn’t all accumulated pro-
fit : £49,880 of it was new capital. Still, that
left £80,658 of profit made in the business
and left there during the last 10 years; and
even if this is earning only 6 p.c. it makes
it as easy to pay p.c. now as it was to
pay 5 p.c. 10 years back.

Just over 10 years ago the capital was
increased out of accumulated profits from
£9OOO to £lO,OOO, and since then another
£4OOO has been paid up in the sanw way.
Now another shilling makes the 20.000 oOs.
shares paid up to 15s. This shilling is, of
course, additional to the usual 10 p.c. which
will be paid to shareholders in cash. Capital
and reserves —together £30,602 —are repre-
sented by premises (£12,823),
and advances (£9898), and public securi-
ties; and they ought to be all right- the
estates in hand are worth £1,756,191; five
years ago they were £1,617,302 —which
seems to suggest that people who have made
this eo. their executor are not as
agreeable as they might be. Still, what
little movement there is is the right way.
Indeed, it is hard to conceive of the business
of a well-managed trustee co. falling away.
In any case the principal is always there:
for the trustee co. is in the happy position
of not having to sink anything in immov-
able stock or plant. ( #
Australia’s biggest butter-maker, the North
Coast Co-operative Co. (N.S.W.), manufac-
turn! only 8031 tons last half-year (to April
30), as against 4560 tons in the correspond-
ing part of its previous year. The profit,,
after making ordinary allowances for depre-
ciation, was only £3076; £2OOO of this was
appropriated for special depreciation, and the
balance was carried forward. Capital is
now £33,429; reserves of one sort and
another, £19,871. Put in another way. the
eo.’s very extensive plant represents the co. s
entire capital and assets and about £7OOO
of borrowed money besides. Liabilities of all
kinds on April 30 were £83,869; and assets
of all kinds, exclusive of the plant, were
£76.497 —mostly sundry debtors and stock.
The co. could well do with another £lO,OOO of
reserves; and it can only get them by hang-
ing on to profit.
Hard-up days with Adelong Estates
(N.S.W.) brought Cohen on the scene; but
of £15,000 borrowed only £3OOO now re-
mains to he repaid. A similar amount has
just been paid, but it was not met so easily
as was forecasted earlier in the year. To
drop from Sfd. ground to dirt carrying only
sufficient gold to pay for drinks was a nasty
bump ; though it is not the first the show has
administered. Another was when No. 2
dredge got on to a bar as barren as South
Head, and calls had to be forced to put,
finances straight. Values since then have
been :

No wonder stuff of this grade not only shook
Cohen off the roof but put enough into the
purse to pay four Is. divs. and let the half-

bilities. Still a handful is wanted because
No. 1 dredge has to get on to a profit-earn-
ing run of dirt, and No. 2 plant has to bucket
its way out of a mat of timber it has worked
into.

The Sulphide Corporation, which some
years ago promoted the Central Zinc Co. in
London and guaranteed it 5 p.c. for a period
which has just expired, proposed recently to
buy out the whole undertaking for £150.000.
The works are at Seaton Carew, in Dur-
ham ; and when Britain at last woke up to
the fact that she had a huge war job on
hand, the State took control of the plant.
That upset the purchase scheme; but a com-
promise has been arrived at by which (1)
a substantial part of the war profits of the
concern, instead of goinsr into the Empire
Exchequer, are made available for extending
the plant; and (2) the Sulptyde Corpora-
tion takes over the business and works for
50.000 pref. shares, 50,000 ordinaries and
£7500 in cash, plus 5 p.c. interest for the
year ended March 31. The business hitherto
has not been profitable, though results have

ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.— Advt.

Efficient grinding is one of the many problems
that mine managers have to solve. The Ilardinge
Conical Mills have proved to the Calumet and Hecla
Mining Co. that grinding is not such a difficult
problem; they have installed 64 of them, and say
“The Ilardinge Conical Mill is the most efficient
grinding machine we have yet gotten hold of.” If
they say this of the mills, it should be worth the
while of mine managers here to inquire about them.
The Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
sole representatives for them in the Commonwealth,
and will be glad to forward particulars.— Advt. [1

Extract from The Citizen, London, 15th Septem-
ber, 1915: “Colonial Mutual Life Office.—lt is a
distinct pleasure to receive and review the annual
report and actuarial investigation of this excellently-
managed, world-wide mutual life office. Apart from
the purely technical aspect of its operations, there is
the admirable work which it is doing in knitting
together the English-speaking peoples in various
parts of the great British Empire. The Society is
mutual in the purest sense of the word, and the
manifest advantages of life assurance upon the
mutual principle are admirably portrayed in its
records. The Society has no shareholders, and all its
profits are divided amongst its members.” —Advt. [1

* * * *
Butler's tin, Torrington (N.S.W.), is in
luck, for the latest wire states that No. 1
dam is full and No. 2 is 'filling. Butler’s
expects to crush 350 tons per week, so a
return of 1 p.c. tin oxide will be profitable
if costs don’t exceed 14s. Still the market
for tin oxide is very uncertain. There are
50,000 shares.
# ♦ ♦
Tributors working at 31Sft. in the Central
Red, White and Blue have given Bendigo
a thrill. The general idea was that the
co. had cleaned up all the pay stone south
of the famous shoot. However, Tonald
Enever went for a 50ft. tribute, and in-
stead of merely a crust secured a nice patch
of quartz. This brought the old brigade
back on the scene, and work north and
south of the tribute block reveals possibili-
ties that have restored the punting gang to
life. The co. has a second chance in the
shaft, and so may again stir up the line.
As it is, a lot of nibbling in Victoria Con-
sols and Ironbark scrip has taken place,
though there is no direct connection between
developments in these neighboring mines.
Consols at G4oft. are punching into the
west leg of a saddle, out of the cap of
which tributors to the south are beating
pay dirt. The Ironbarks’ flutter is on the
possibility of the crosscut at 810 ft. bunting
into decent stone in the formation below
the Central Blue reef. With three mines
pegging away, it is quite likely that some
cheerful bulletins will reach Pall Mall.
Bendigonians are offering up thanksgivings
because outsiders are buying up forfeited
scrip in veteran shows like Suffolk United,
Clarence and Sheepshead. The pluck of
these people deserves reward, for it isn’t
often that Bendigo lets go any stock having
a decent prospect.
* * * *
J. Trevor’s costs sheet for the North
Nuggetty Ajax, Daylesford (Vic.), proves
that he does his bit to get the mine going
cheaply, but prospecting and development
alone have been gobbling up as much gold
as he is able to win. This means call-mak-
ing, with contracting to keep the pot boil-
ing. Deep sinking has not disclosed any-
thing fresh, and as the Central next door
is going ahead with that job for the general
benefit of the field, Trevor’s cash is being
used to crosscut east, so that rises may go
up there between the vertical reefs in
search of fresh flat makes. The best stone
being broken comes from above No. 6 level
and from the extension of the old shoot
worked there. That there isn’t much of it
is proved by the mine being put on a one-
shift basis.
* * * *
Mt. Lyell knew enough about the Her-
cules-Rosebery mines to exercise its option;
but it wants to know more and has put a
drill to work to see if it can dig into some-
thing beyond the 800,000 tons of ore that its
officials have already reported on.
* * * *
The boom gave New Rubv Flat (Tas.) a
profit last half-year of £lOBO from £2340
worth of tin. The shareholders got it all
in a couple of sixpenny divs. which (with
taxes, £00) called for £1279. On May 31
liquid assets were valued at £950, mostly
tin in the races, and the co. owed £326.
Water is available for sluicing three shifts
during this season; and there is enough
good ground ahead to make the little Mel-
bourne co. feel happy.
* * * *
The Gladstone (Tas.) tin district is
looking up. A lode has been located handy
to the township from which cne man. work-
insr singlehanded, is knocking out £23 per
week. Of course, he is only Specking it, but
it is said there is a big mass of. tin-bearing
rock, and the Pioneer Co. may take a bite to
see what it tastes like. Some of the alluvial
shows are doing well in a small way, and the
Government has been advised by the mining
officials to see what can be done to develop
the field.
There is some talk of a gold discovery in
the Port Cygnet (Tas.) district. Thirty
years or more ago a fair bit of alluvial
gold was got about Port Cygnet. Some
estimates put the quantity at 20,0000 z. or
more, but it probably grew in the telling;
the official record says the yield was 3000 oz.
or so. Though it has often been looked for
there is only one undoubted instance of
gold having been found in the bedrock.
The directors of the Magnet silver lead
mine in Tasmania have done the square
thing, and paid a bonus of £5 per man to
miners and mill hands who, accepted reduced
wages when the metal markets went to
pieces at the outbreak of war.
* * * *
There is a slight revival at Gympie,
because several mines on the Columbia line
show improved prospects. The S. Glanmire
and Monkland is also attracting attention
again, on the strength of a formation carry-
ing small leaders north of the Power reef.
No. 2 Columbia has been raising specimen
stone from below 14G8ft.; and the reef is
from Gin. to 9in. wide, carrying free gold.
No. 3 Columbia is raising payable stone,
but dividends are postponed while sinking
and other development work is progressing.
The North Smithfield and No. 2 N. Colum-
bia are popular because they promise a
few good crusliings.
The Oaks (N.Q.) is bucked up by the
discovery of a Gin. leader at 321 ft. from the
mouth of the tunnel. A crushing of 700
tons was cleaned up in June for 91 oz. gold.
Working costs were 6s. 4d. per ton, but
wages men have been out on strike, and the
tonnage handled is small if the Oaks is
aiming at a dividend from material that
is giving a mine profit of only 2s. to 3s.
per ton, out of which management, deprecia-
tion, etc., have to be provided for.
Blair Athol, near Clermont (Q.), raised
5956 tons of coal in May. The Newcastle
raised 2191 tons, and the Co-operative 2269
tons. Although the output from the three
mines is still small, the coal is better appre-
ciated every year, and the demand is steadily
increasing. Blair Athol paid a sixpenny
in June; its profit is a shade over 2s. per
ton.
* * * *
Lode Hill (N.Q.) is wearing a more
cheerful look than it has for some time.
Last clean up of 13 tons was finished in
the middle of May, and the ground is open-
ing out in width as Lode Hill proper is
approached. Two faces are now being
worked, and the next clean up, due about
the third week in July, should leave a sub-
stantial profit.
* * * *
“Platman” (from the neighborhood of
Cape York) : John Dickie, discoverer of the
Alice and Ebagoolah goldfields, is again
on the. Peninsula, the Government having
fitted him out with horses, etc. Word comes
that he has struck some likely-looking
country outside the Laura. The Peninsula
badly wants the. lift that a new discovery
would give, as it is rapidly becoming de-
populated .... The Palmer Gold Mines struck
trouble with the made-in-Germany engine
bought by the Government experts. Fuel
costs are ruinous. Oil costs £8 per ton
carriage from Laura to Maytown mines.
It would be interesting to know why the
State Mining Engineer and Inspector of
Mines were so keen on the oil-burning Ger-
man when there are plenty of good British-
built suction gas plants to be procured in
Australia.
“Copper-gouger”: The chairman of Mt.
Cuthbert (Q.) told his shareholders that
the co. produced 483 tons copper worth
£48,551, and that smelting, refining, etc.,
ran away with £31,746. Was anybody look-
ing when it went? It must have been the
most expensive bit of smelting this old globe
ever witnessed since the days of Tubal Cain.
Surely shareholders are entitled to know
how the £65-odd per ton is made up.
Just before the last mail left London Lake
View Oroyas hardened Gd. (to 10s.) and
London Australians 41d. (to Is. lOid.) on
the strength of a further rise in Burmas.
Lake View holds 100.000 of this stock, and
the London Australians £28,300 worth of
its debentures. London Australian also
holds, or had at the time its last list of
assets was published, 1800 shares in the Sons
of Gwalia, which were recently enhanced on
account of increased ore reserves. Speaking
at the Gwalia annual meeting in London
last month, chairman Barry said that for
the first time in the history of the mine the
ore in sight was fully four years ahead of
f.'f. Besides this they had between
6(X),000 and 700,000 tons of tailings which
were estimated to average Bs. a ton, and
yield a net profit of £50,000 to £60,000.
* * * *
Bullfinch_Prop. (W.A.) dug out £99,871
during 1910, and sundry small receipts
brought the total revenue for the year up
to £100,040. Two Gd. dividends were paid,
and after allowing for depreciation a shade
under £7484 was left to carry forward
Average working costs were 15s. 7d., or a
reduction of over Gfd. compared with the
1914 figures; and extraction was a little
over 90 as against 93f. Lower extraction
is attributed to an increasing quantity of
sulphide ore being milled. At the end of
1915 the co.. had cash £9157; bullion in
transit, not including reserve, £4816; and
stores, £7036; but against these was £8282
owed to sundry creditors. Ore reserves are
estimated at 97,334 tons, assaying 255. sd.
per ton. The two Doolettes and Dick
Hamilton, of Great Boulder, are still on the
Board.
* * * *
Leonora (W.A.) has livened up as a
result of the Gold Blocks and Chaffers
deals, and is now trying to dispose of the
Harbor Lights. The latter has a big lode
from which several thousand tons have been
taken and treated for a small profit by local
people, but its prospects are nothing to
blow about. Several option holders, includ-
ing the Associated Northern, have tested the
show from time to time, but without any
business resulting. Sons of Gwalia knows
ot' its existence, but prefers to send out pro-
specting parties to Laverton and beyond
rather than take it on. Attempts will pro-
bably be made to palm off several Leonora
shows in Adelaide, Melbourne and Sydney,
but long working options should be insisted
on and no deal completed without them.
The tombstone for Black Range, Sand-
stone (W.A.), is now being carved by the
faithful Couper. For 14 years the show fed
the squatters of the Vic. Western District,
who, led by Ilans Irvine, forked out the
£20,000 required to buy and equip the claim.
Altogether £240,000 of divs. were paid from
£905,000 gold won; £96,000 went in develop-
ment and £60,000 for plant. The other half-
million went to the worker and the tax-col-
lector. The mantle of the old show is to fall
on the Binnacles, where Couper lias been
knocking things into shape. From No. 2 and
No. 3 shafts he is testing the ground at
under 100 ft. Ilis tally at the first-named
shaft is 13dwt. ore, but at No. 2 only poor
dirt is in the face. As he has the 100 ft. level
opened up for 700 ft. he has plenty of room
to draw on any special lens if he' wants to
average up the grade of ore.
* * * *
The profit of Edna May for the half-year
was cut by taxation and depreciation to
£38,360, while divs. paid were £38,565. This
looks bad ; but comfort can be got from the
belief that capital expenditure on plant is
now about over. Of course, the shaft lias to
go down; still, if the water has been drained
by the pumps at 300 ft., there won’t be any
awkward stop in development. By taking in
all the ore at that level the reserves show up
better in quantity than they did six months
ago:—-
t —Oct., 1915.—, —April,
