Manager Williams may find that there is
growling over the inclusion of the whole of
the 300 ft. ore, as he has not proved it on all
sides; but experience at the mine justifies it.
The point that the bears will hang on is the
pitch of the shoot of ore north-east, as dis-
closed in the plan, and the low results so far
reported from the stone to the south-west of
the fault. On the ore reserves, valued at
£204,219, and with costs at 235. per ton,
there is £150,000 profit in sight. Then
00,000 tons of slimes are valued at 255. 6d.,
out of which Williams reports he is getting
the good extraction of 92 p.c. On such work
the dump is worth, say, £36,000 to the co.
If costs of treatment be 55., the net value
would be £28,000, or a total profit in sight
of £178,000 —roughly, £4 per share. This
has to be added to the £4 10s. already paid
by this bear-blistering freak of a mine, which
85,720 tons has won gold worth
£360,882 and paid £188,540 in divs.
* * * *
From a Bulletin man in the West:—
Edna May stocks have got Westralian dealers in a
tangle. Deeps has no ore reserves other than what
may be classed as “probable,” and even this is
only “probable” to the extent of *what may reason-
ably be expected from the development disclosed by
one diamond-drill core. The main shaft is officially
expected to strike the main Edna May reef at about
490 ft., and it is already down 450 ft. However, the
reef is more likely to be struck nearer 540 ft. than
490 ft., and if the shaft exposes the reef at any depth
over 500 ft., the development will be a good one from
the point of view not only of Deeps but of Edna
May also. Deeps cash reserves are about equal to
its ore reserves at present, but local director Tom
Duff intends coming to the rescue with a loan of
£2OOO from his hard-earned cash.
The Edna May Central new reef, which recently
sent the stock to £6, is rapidly finding its level:
Bdwt. per ton is about the value of the monthly out-
put, and nothing better is in sight except, perhaps,
slightly-improved yields for two or three months
obtained by an extra ore-chasing effort. The 225 ft.
level crosscut recently passed through a vein of
quartz which looks like the continuation of the new
reef. However, values were not pasted on the
office door on this occasion, and when Central makes
no announcement as to values it can be depended
that they are fairly low. The crosscut is being
continued in the hope of finding something further
on. This is good business, as one never knows when
lOoz. values may be met with in the Central. But
lOoz. yields have not shown up so far. Possibly
the balance is in a large bullion reserve.
Bears, timid bulls and poor devils forced to realise
through not being in a position 'to provide extra
“cover” have brought Edna Mays to about the £5
mark.

Britain is now importing more than half
l t n„!£ pply of t'orcign lead from Australia-
tons were imported in May. of which
1 667 tons came from Australia. The exports
were 5834 tons, leaving only 7851 tons for
domestic consumption. Before the war
Spanish lead had the run of the English
market, while thousands of tons of Aus-
tralian lead concentrates were regularly
shipped to Belgian and German smelters.
* * * *
Last year the four Ivanhoe directors
divided £2210 in remuneration, and as some
of those out in the cold thought the Board
was collaring too much, at the annual meet-
ing chairman Govett proceeded to justify
the expenditure by stating that the job was
considered one of the few prizes of the
“profession.” The six or seven members of
the now defunct Oroya-Brown Hill Board
used to divide about £5OOO a year; but that
was in the days when the mine was blooming
and Kalgoorlie was at its zenith.

BOTTLED IN FRANCE.
A Good Corn
Remover.
Those who suffer from corns and hard skin
on the soles of the feet w‘ll find it a great
boon to apply a little Atla Resin to the corn,
which, when applied to the corn or hard skin,
and a little covering of cotton wool on the
the top to prevent it sticking to the stocking,
will remove the corn in a day or two. The
Atla Resin seems to act by getting thor-
oughly into complete contact with the corn,
and thus being able to penetrate it, and not
to act on the surface only. It is certainly
the latest cure for these troublesome and
painful structures, and. according to all re-
ports by those who have used it, one of the
best.* f-

A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, July 3, 1916.
My Dear Auini. —
Bearded, russet-cheeked men from the
hill-country and long, lean men from the
Western plains crowded around President
James Kidd at the Sheep Show opening.
Governor Strickland turned an attentive ear
to the president’s glad tidings of a big in-
crease in exhibits, then took the rostrum and
launched his reply. After which the woolly
dukes and duchesses were led forward, with
a loud accompaniment of “Baa-aa,” to have
their toilets finished. There was fierce
resistance on the part of the aristocrats,
who backed and butted before they were
hurled into the Presence. Pure merinos,
such as his Grace of Crumple Horns, were
then invested with the ribbons of their
order. Veteran Sam McCaughey. was the
winner of a couple of events in this section.
A few women picked their way round the
pens, and the knitters’ gleam was in their
eyes as they buried a glove in blanket-rolls
of fluff. Squatter Merriman, the tough old
Yass pioneer, was the proud owner of a
small herd of prize-winners. Frank Edols,
of Burrawang ; Fred McMaster, of Dalkeith ;
Bob Carter, of Narromine; and Pat Osborne
were keen competitors; and Clive Dangar,
now in France, was represented by a'
countess or two. And of course Anzac was
there —a gentleman-sheep with horns all
round his woolly whiskers. He came from
Gostwyck.
Button Day for the War Chest began in
the bleak wintry dawn. Lady scouts were
early on the path, and by nine o’clock Martin
Place was like a market garden, with a sale
of fancy goods to steady the rush for pump-
kins. Faces began to peer out of high Challis
House fastnesses, and John Lane Mullins
looked like a miniature of himself framed in
brown stone. As the sun made a wan ap-
pearance—later drowned by weepy skies- —
the Governor’s chaise drove up. An official
retinue then fell upon his Ex. and landed
him on a small dais, where a herd of con-
suls revolved round the Presence. 11. Y.
Braddon, veteran Tom Dibbs, Kelso King,
soldier Finn, E. P. Simpson and other War
Chest backers made an island com-
pletely surrounded by Boy Scouts. The But-
tons were declared well and truly open —this
by his Ex. after Mrs. Ted Antill (one of the
first women workers for the War Chest) had
pinned a special decoration —gold set with
pearls—in the vice-regal coat. The delegate
from the Railway and Tramway Association,
which had gallantly given £550 for a button,
then took delivery of the goods, bobbed his
head and withdrew.
The 7th Light Horse ladies had a dashing
style in aprons. Dark butcher’s blue, with
red slashings and the number 7 proudly
mounted on one side. Their stall, just under
the G.P.O. chimes, sold nearly everything
you can put a name to. The 13th Battalion
dames, with Mrs. Morrison (widow of the
major who fell in Gallipoli), Mrs. Louis
Henderson (mother of a 13th who’s now a
dashing captain in -, there you are), and
others who barrack for Colonel Burnaby’s
lot, took possession of a Moore-street stand.
The rain pelted down for a while at mid-
day. Some of the girls had come out in
dazzling white, and 2 o’clock saw them
limp. Still they stuck to the game. Late in
the afternoon every other male was smoking
a “two-fer” cigar, for which he had paid six-
pence. Through the smoke he loomed over a
ballast of buttons that sagged his coat lapels.
Eadith Walker sold her wares in Pitt-
street. The Old Gold and Silver dames —
safely housed in Her Majesty’s vestibule —-
got buyers for about £lOO worth of stuff.
Across the way a plump citizen auctioned
anything he could lay his hand on. A bird
o’ paradise—stiff in death —brought over £5
in a few minutes. Jim Osborne, one of the
comic reliefs of the day, helped to run up
the fancy price of a fat sheep. Light repartee
and cash floated around the Red Cross Book
Depot —till the beast was moved on.
A Button Day rout in the Australia Win-
ter Garden was one of the thrills of the
War Chest campaign. Soon after lunch a
well-dressed mob of women collected round
the doorway. Wedged into a dense mass in
the outer hall, the pressure of a few femi-
nine battering rams at the rear gradually
hurled weaker units right up against the
glass doors. Behind rattled the china
which vassals were setting for the cafe
chantant. A mild young man sitting at the
receipt of custom, and selling the 2s. 6d
tickets, was nearly wiped off the map. In-
stead of opening the doors, a loud voice
forced its way through the surging crowd
and bawled a command “to get back to the
vestibule.” Lovely woman indignantly
. refused to fight her way whence she had
come at so much damage to her appearance.
Deadlock lasted for a few palpitating
minutes. Then the glass gate swung open.
Had it done so half an hour before, a mass
of fashionable millinery would have been
much less bashed.
When the company recovered its breath
it sipped tea and munched forage while
Bantock auctioned socks knit by the fair
hands of Julius Knight. Amidst a wild
buzz of comment, I fancy they climbed to
£lO 10s. and were knocked down to Loud
Applause. Ethel Cadman, Minnie Love,
Derek Hudson and Bryce Carter obliged
with sundry items. Julius gave “The Por-
trait” —a telling recitation which was
Lewis Waller’s first, spoken word to a Syd-
ney audience. Staid women then refilled
their teacups, while sprightly damsels
added a whisky and soda to their prelimi-
nary cocktails.
Archgaiters Wright put on his best purple
velvet bonnet the other afternoon and
marched round the grounds of St. Andrew's
Cathedral. Choir boys, with that heavenly
expression which comes from a well-soaped
countenance and well-fed interior, swelled
the procession. Archdeacon Gunther, who
grows thinner (he has about 77 milestones
behind), joined the throng. The fuss was
the opening of “The Soldiers’ Welcome.”
Mrs. Wright was inspired with the idea
when she saw the Melbourne rest-house in
St. Paul’s grounds. C.J. Cullen wedged
his way through the crowd after the pre-
liminary grace before buns. The grace, by
the way, was too long, and included at least
one mile of hymn which even the choir boys
didn’t know.
Colonel Miller came from Liverpool to
lift up the latch. He did it with, a small
piece of gold and a speech of about 64 words.
Then the visitors surged into the building
and fell upon the viands. The haven is a
large, cheery place, with a debt of £750 and
fibro sides. As about £4OO is already in
hand, the clerics are not afraid of being
rushed to the bankruptcy court. Ex-head-
master Stacy AVaddy’s curly head, jambed
into the millinery of a chaplain A.1.F.,
was a half-inch taller than most of the
pates present.
National Council ladies, in sables, velvet
and motors, bowled down to Government
House to resume their discussion on Thrift
—for other people. Lady Edeline, with
Ruby Board at her elbow, sat at the small
judgment table. Ruth Beale got into the
running before the company began to yawn.
She considered the cash how it may grow
(if Billjim, the wretch, doesn’t blew it on
’baccy and long odds!) by the new six
o’clock pub-rule. This done, the stately
Mrs. Laura Luffman arose. After some
purring she gave Australian housewives a
hearty scratch for being “the most wasteful
I have ever seen, and I have seen many
countries.” After detailing her own grief
when a former minion threw away a liam-
bone (this must have been in the halcyon
days when Bogue gardened in
the good lady sat down. Other speakers
perambulated from loin chops to husbands.
When the end came, A.D.C. Firth, emerg-
ing from some secret hiding-place in the
lobby, handed round cakes to the Thrifty—
his last job with the Stricklands.
The girl under
the early-Victorian
bonnet is no
stranger to you.
She is Paul in e
Bindley, the
soprano who ap-
peared with Du-
fault on a previous
visit here. Pauline
is a Bendigo girl,
and her father
“found” Amy
Castles and taught
her. Her musical
Mecca after this
tour will be the
U.S.A., where she
hopes to get into
light opera.
Chief Justice Cullen is Acting-Governor
for the coming fortnight or so. There has
been a kind of bank-holiday feeling in the
Governors’ Union of late. So our Ex.
moves on to Broken Hill, with a mild spree
in S. Australia at the finish.
Premier Holman, a weary man after the
recent storms, takes three weeks off before
facing worse oue s. He starts for Fiji this
week and puts i n a short holiday as Gov-
ernor Sweet-Escott’s guest. Then home by
the end of j u i y to meet t he Caucus and soon
a f te rwards the country,

Sympathy from a wide circle of friends
S’ o / 38 out popular Mrs. I lomley, of Manly,
whose elder _ daughter, the wife of Dr.
Hatley, of Lithgow. passed away recently,
Heceased lady left two infant children—one
very young.
Artist Frances Hodgkins has been well
hung” in the Royal Academy and is otnei-
wise flourishing. But her art school a ► •
Ives, Cornwall, was broken up by
authorities’ verdict: “No sketching on
coast.” Frances hadn’t thought about 1
that way, and the dear old War Office
hadn’t thought about it either till
M.L. artist had been at work for some time.
So she packed up her color-box for an Eng-
lish interior —Eversham —after swallowing,
the broth and sympathy of her friends in a
bad bout of quinsy and rheumatism.
Another M.L. artist, who began here
under Dattilo Rubbo, is off to London. Sue
is the youthful Evelyn Chapman, who is
dragging her devoted parents to England
via Panama. They left Wellington by the
Remuera. Evelyn notifies that she is going
to divide her time between munition making
and art study. It sounds strenuous.
I send you a pic-
ture of a man who
seeks publicity just
as little as anyone
in Sydney. But I
believe he would
go through the city
in a Chidley cos-
tume if he knew it
would make the
Red Cross Informa-
tion Bureau, at
Dalton House, 115
Pitt-street, known
to everyone or in-
crease its effective-
ness. Just over 12
months ago, when
it became very diffi-
cult. sometimes for
mothers to get tid-
ings of tlieir boys, Longer Owen, Iv.C., sug-
gested to the Bar of Sydney that here was
work for them to do. Promptly an organisa-
tion was established, and it has been Danger
Owen’s special charge. The portrait is Ins.
The bureau is ‘‘for obtaining news of sick,
wounded and missing soldiers,” and its ser-
vices are gratuitous. All it asks, if the in-
quirer can pay for the cost of the cable, is
that he (or she) shall do that much.
The Alpine Club pulls on its skis this
week for a tussle at Kosciusko. Profits of
the outing go to one of the War Funds.
The hardy annuals barrister Pitt and his
mother and sister take in their party Bar-
bara Gordon. She’s the girl who often gets
there first when the lady ski-ers are tossing
the snow about. There will of course be
some khaki in the party this year. Colonel
Harrison and Captain Hopkinson, of Dun-
troon, will wear it.
Do you remember the fair-haired Ethel
Brookman, of Adelaide? She was here for
the polo a month or so before the Great
Blaze. Her wedding to Pat Kirkpatrick, of
the Indian Police, throws a light on the
duties of Gentleman Bobby in the Coral
Strand. Pat controls a district as large as
the English county of Surrey, with head-
quarters at Sukkur, and half a million in-
habitants. Six months’ crisp weather on
the hills braces up the European resident
and incites him to get out of Sukkur, which
is 126° in the shade, with a butterfly breeze
at night. Ethel has been touring with her
Bobby. They get about on horseback, with
camels grunting behind under the Adelaide
girl’s Saratogas.
The war god is a cruel jester. Last week
Justice Ferguson got a letter from his son,
Captain Arthur Gardere Ferguson, report-
ing “all well,” and his Honor started on his
job in the law-mill invigorated by the good
news. A few hours later came word that
the boy had been killed in action on .Tune
14. A couple of years ago the Judge, kind-
est of men and most devoted of fathers, lost
another son. He is left now with only the
youngest—and the deep sympathy of a circle
of friends who feel for him something more
than ordinary affection.
Of the Anson family, who struck their
roots in Maoriland for a time, Denis, you
remember, was drowned in a frolicsome
launch outing in the Upper Thames in 1914.
A gentleman named Count Benckendorff
was one of the party. Now Frances Anson,
a cousin of Denis, is engaged to the Rev.
William Temple, younger son of the late
Archbishop of Canterbury. He used to be
headmaster at Repton, but is now rector of
St. James’s, Piccadilly.
Manager Freddie Shipman’s smile was
about half a yard wide at the first Dufault
concert. The size of the audience did it. The
silvery tenor is the same great artist as of
old. with either a bigger volume of voice or
a better understanding of our Town Hall
acoustics. His first two numbers, a noble
rendering of Handel’s “Where’er You
Walk” and Sullivan’s setting of the Prodi-
gal Son’s “I Will Arise and Go to My
Father,” were geriis. Pauline Bindley, in a
frothy white muslin frock caught with satin
bows, is the surprise of the present season.
The Bendigo girl, whom we heard first in
the raw, so to speak, is improved almost
beyond recognition. The recent M.L. tour,
with Dufault as friendly censor, has put a
polish on the always-promising material. A
tendency to sing a shade flat on her top notes
is her chief menace. Florence Scapini, the
Italian-Maoriland girl, in azure-blue tucks, is
the youthful fiddler of the company. Harold
Whittle pegged away cheerfully as accom-
panist.
What’s all this? The ’Frisco Examiner
credits Melba with the following
It is the dream of my life to have a home here
(US) and a class of young women in San Fran-
cisco. I shall regard the girls as my precious
charges. I shall consider it a privilege to teach
them personally, and a sacred duty to advise them.
Our songbird —who is about to stay on the
shores of Double Bay with the Spencer
Bruntons —is then described as alluding in
perfervid terms to a “Melba Conservatory”
in Melbourne. The hothouse plants in her
native city, however, according to the ’Mur-
lean reporter, won’t have the gardener’s
chief attention. That, lie says, goes to the
Californian buds —possibly next year.
Meantime Melba has set to work to sing
and beg some motor-ambulances for Russia.
Surely Ivan has earned them.
The Piastro-Mirovitch musicians crossed
the Murray after a final matinee in the Town
Hall. Final, that is, till they return. In
reply to queries as to why these upstanding
Russians are not in the trenches, I’m told
they’ve been exempted by the Czar. I haven’t
yet been able to ask the Czar why he did it.
Mrs. Arthur Kelynack, after three months
of agonising illness, lias passed into peace.
She went through the long suffering in her
own home on Lane Cove River. A devoted
family hovered round till the end. She was
the wife of barrister Kelynack, the big,
jovial man who looks like a wool-king in a
prosperous season.
In getting married at St. Stephen’s kirk
Mary Chisholm Ross did just what mother
did. And, moreover, all the Chisholm Ross
bairns as they came along, were christened
in the same Scotclibyterian stronghold. Bride
Mary’s finery, the most striking thing in the
loveliest of the June orange-blossom fetes,
was made by clever mother and a sister. The
trousseau was a dream in dawn-pink crepe
de chine. Every stitch was a labor of love.
Frank Coffee leaves by the Makura this
week for Vancouver. As it is somewhere,
about his thirtieth trip across, lie ought to
be able to navigate the vessel.
Dorothy Tange, a daughter of lawyer
Charles Tange, was wed in St. Peter’s, Neu-
tral Bay, the other afternoon. The ’groom
is Alfred, son of Brisbane bank manager J.
M. Davidson. The affair was very quiet,
just relatives to see the cake cut at Auntie
Moir’s, close at hand. Sister Thelma walked
behind, with Dr. R idler second-best man.
Tom, a son of the late solicitor Charley
Bull, of Sydney, has been promised that he
may call for Rosalie Henderson, a Kogarali
girl, and take her away for life.
Parson Wentworth Shields tied the knot
when Margaret, the fair-haired daughter of
the Hector Ross home at Croydon, married
George E. Cole, a Strntlifield man. The
bride floated up the aisle in White plumage
decked with Brussels lace. Above the tulle-
swathed corsage gleamed a necklet of pearls,
the bridegroom’s gift to the Peg o’ his heart.
Elsie and Lalla Ross were bridesmaidens in
foamy white. Dr. Phil Daniels supported
the ’groom. Reception at the Carlton,
where a sit-down banquet warmed the
toasts.
The 30th Battalion dames have wiped away
their tears, and are now looking for a new
address. Their bales of comforts blazed
away in a big fire at Winn’s in Oxford-street.
Hard luck, and I doubt if there’s a pen’orth-
of insurance over the depot’s £2OO.
George Lambert shows a portrait of Mrs.
Will Dyson at this year’s International
Society’s muster. As you know, she’s
Ruby Lindsay, Norman’s artistic sister.
J. C. Ives, a tall young man who for-
merly looked after the Lone Hand’s busi-
ness side, and now sits on a Bulletin stool
pursuing a similar line of thought, was wed
at St. James’s the other day. His best girl
is Bertie Norman, of Neutral Bay. She is
related to the Berkley who owns Newcastle
Herald.
The winter is unusually furry. The big
musquash coat, with a lady inside, has been
a prevailing feature of the season’s after-
noon gatherings. Somebody has a _few
coppers left, for the pelt runs into £25 or
so.
The Arts and Grafters hold their shew in
Norwich Chambers from July 3 to 7.
The 2nd D.A.C. comforts fund commit-
tee sends up a cry for helpers. 117 Pitt-
street is the address.
For some time the women’s war-census
bureau has been housed in the old building
that shelters the Amelioration Committee.
Like Jo, it has now been asked to move on.
But that doesn’t matter. It has been a
frost.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, July 3, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
St. Vincent’s Hospital carnival, which
concluded with a pageant of “queens”
at Melbourne Exhibition on Saturday
night, was no great draw for the many-
lieaded general public. The management
struck bad weather; but in any case it
committed a grave error by disqualifying
the ready shilling at the turnstiles of a
building which has an unlimited holding
capacity. As it turned out, the show didn’t
deserve a big crowd. The stage-management
was extremely amateurish. The pageant
and the rest of the carnival should have
been presented on one stage; tlien a booked
seat would have provided a view of the
entire proceedings. Actually the show was
split into two sections —singing one end <>f
the building, pageant the other —and the
results were chaotic. Mrs. John Mills, the
Gippsland winner, polled nearly 300,000
votes, and beat Mrs. Healy, the bookies’
nomination, by £5O. Several queens were
scratched, and most of those who went to
the barrier looked unhappy. The right
regal touch was given only by dainty Irma
Caron, who bowed with educated grace,
looked pleased and greeted the audience with
benign smiles, just as though she were get-
ting paid for her trouble, like an ordinary
professional queen in unreal royal life.
Mrs. Jack Mills, “Queen” of Gippsland,
comes from the Maealister country, where
the cow is holy and money grows around
every bush. For public view the lady wears
a Marcel-waved Queen Alexandra bang, a
bold eyebrow, a hearty manner, ldoz. large
diamonds, 3doz. small do. and a bright and
shining smile. She is the type of matron
who can read the day’s book o’ th races
from cover to cover at one sitting without
getting bored.
The most gruesome part of the proceed-
ings was when the defeated Empresses were
rounded up by Chamberlain Bibron to kneel
before the throne and pay their moral rates
and taxes.
Dorothy Low, the Angel of the Corona-
tion, did not float down from the dome, as
was first intended. Brother Dave looked at
the celestial ropes and tackle, gazed up at
the dome, and decided to censor the flying
act. So Dorothy did her part with her heels
planted firmly on the red baize platform.
The battle between the St. _ Vincent’s
Queens was keen enough; but it was _ a
deaconesses’ tea-party by comparison with
the action between the A.D.C.’s. Each
queen had an attendant secretary to super-
vise the beating-up of votes, and the secre-
tary of the winning royalty was promised a
handsome bonus. The idea made business
lively—just as throwing one joint into a
den of hungry lionesses would wake up a
zoo.
John Madden had something to say to
daughter Lesley after the big Queen rally
at Cliveden. Some of the card maniacs
were so dead-sure that the Madden flat was
one of the havens for play that they tried
to raid the place after John had retired to
bed. If they had been unlucky enough to
succeed John would have committed them
all for contempt of court.
The Grand National at Flemington on
Saturday drew a huge crowd. Fine weather
had been ordered, so rain fell in icy tor-
rents. Balsillie, the cloud-coaxer, was
present, wrapped in a huge grey over-
coat, and walked the soppy lawn
wearing a mysterious smile. Women
were buried to their noses in furs.
The new coat collar, which turns up to the
ears, and the fashionable hat, which comes
down to the eyes, make a complete disguise;
so I am still wondering who was the ultra-
fashionable woman dressed in bright blue
tailorings, with a foot-wide border of the
sensational civet-cat fur, with a hat and
collar of the same sort.
Mrs. Harry Kirby showed a skirt hem of
cold-grey silk ruchings over pale-grey spats;
her coat was a real sable heavyweight. Mrs.
Machonochie, down from her northern sheep
corner, supported rare pink-brown foxskins.
Mrs. Thompson (Albury) got a lot of com-
fort out of the ermine swathed round her
comely shoulders. A gross or so of Manifolds
came along from Camperdown, and two of the
girls did a dress duet in blues. The
Madden-McEvoy sister looked comfy in squir-
rels. John Mills from Gippsland was there,
but his wife was at home getting ready to be
Queen in the evening. One of the Geelong
Bussells aired a new crinoline effect, with
a flare of bright chocolate-brown skirts
heavily braided. Mrs. Bogers Thompson
wore part of the grey wolf her husband shot.
And one young squattress simply had to wear
her new pink spring hat, in spite of the
dreadful weather. It looked as pathetic as
a cold kitten.
“Bigoletto” on Saturday night filled the
Princess’s, and the enthusiasts who hadn’t
booked in time had to stand. Mrs. Hugh
Ward, from Sydney, was in a box and some
noticeable clothes. Dolores, demure and
dimpled, partied in the dress-circle with the
Holroyds. Manager Gonsalez, all smiles,
bows and shirtfront, kept station as usual
just inside the stalls door. The whole com-
pany (including the dejected chorus) are
now wearing new clothes. It sort of lights
up the show.
Young tenor J.ois as the Duke in “Iligo-
letto” was too beautiful for the feminine
audience to merely listen to. lie wore
turquoise-blue velvet slashed with silver
and white, and his wonderful legs were
revealed in white fleshings. That was in
the Court scene. lie made love later iu
black velvet and gold. A still later love
affair was sung in purple silk, high white
riding boots with spurs and a dashingly
villainous cloak of dull black. Manager
Gonsalez’s daughter Gilda looked young and
fair in pink.
From our cousin in sodden Adelaide: —
Those of us who aren’t washed out of house and
home or reduced to a condition of sniffling misery by
colds derive what comfort we can from the fact that
we’ve established a new weather record. Ever stood
on a cold poultice under the shower-bath ? Tatter-
sail’s races were like that. The Railways Standing
Committee was submerged between Hergott Springs
and the Queensland border; it turned up at Lvnd-
hurst Siding five days after it was due at Leigh’s
Creek, having abandoned its personal luggage and
tents and walked miles. The Sturt Creek flooded
Morphettville, and various suburbs were more or less
submerged. And now councils are wondering where
the money is to come from to mend the roads. But
we shall be able to have a bath next summer.
CONDUCTOR ERNESTO CONSALEZ
Marking a Pause.
The G milestones are deserting us for N. S. Wales,
where they have taken a house at Gladesville. You
know them —the Canon used to cane our big brother
at St. Peter’s College.
Mrs. T. Hope Murray has had news of the death
of her son Kenneth, who had a commission in the
Seaforth Highlanders.
The Government has increased the grant for the
Glenelg breakwater from £32,000 to £llO,OOO -
three years to be allowed for the additional work.
The extended design will make the undertaking a
“reproductive work” by giving largely-increased
shelter for coastal boats. Meanwhile Glenelg remains
calm till it sees the finished article.
The Poona, with the Sturt statue on board, is due
here this month, and a tender for the erection of a
pedestal has been accepted. The P. and O. Co. has
announced its intention of sending in no bill for
freight.
Amongst the six “drunk and disorderlies” at the
police court after the week-end were two country
J’s.P., who were spared the “ignominy” of the dock
and given a private hearing in the magistrates’ room,
Premier Vaughan is going to ask why.
Bindley Webb, Associate to Judge Gordon, is to go
into camp shortly.
Amongst the last batch of wills lodged for pro-
bate was one bequeathing a pet dog to a. friend and
£2O for the animal’s upkeep. As a “stranger in
blood” the dog pays probate, and £2 of the £2O
goes' to the Treasury.
An amusement tax, on lines adopted in England,
shows its beak through the shell. A halfpenny on
each picture-show ticket —and thence upwards.
Mrs. W. D. Scott had her 91st birthday at the
beginning of the week. Her work as a flower-painter
is well known, and she still indulges her hobby.
The Military Command in South Aus. has been
bobbing about in a curious way. When the war
began Irving had it, but a year ago got his chance
to go to Egypt. Sandford, of Victoria, was sent
over, but in April he returned to his fortifications
business. Darvall, of the General Staff, became
Commandant for a few weeks, then made way for
Col. “Jim” Rowell, the only local man of the lot.
Now Irving, back from Egypt, takes charge again.
Rowell has had command of a transport, but is a
shade old for service in this war. His son was one
of the original batch of cadets at Duntroon, and his
nephew, Col. Frank Rowell, died at Gallipo .

The best hostesses, who have done little
since the war, think they see a good excuse

Mrs Ted Dyson, Mrs. Russell Jackson
and David Low’s sister Dorothea organised
a cabaret concert at the Austral Salon on
Friday evening in aid of St. Vincents Hos-
pital. It was a lively gathering. Defence
Minister Pearce and Navy Minister Jensen
were present and supplied moderately-con-
vincing impersonations of Great Statesmen
in their More Frivolous Moments. Sketches
by Low, Esther Paterson, Nuttall and others
were auctioned by Dug Hart, and the names
and intentions of the various performers
were announced in delicately-modulated
accents by Edward Dyson.
The busy Crevelli dame is to open a
■jumble fete at Mentone on July 8 to help
the French Red Cross. Three o’clock, at the
Recreation Hall.
The wretchedly-named Melbourne Ragged
Boys’ Homes are feeling more ragged than
usual, and a demonstration and concert
with a 17-inch collar is due in the Town
Hall on Thursday. Mabel Mattingley is in
charge of the music, and there will be
movies.
Bert Bailey came to town to see the new
Steele Rudd play launched, and then retired
again to the bush to think it over. bydne\,
however, will see him enwliiskered and in
action when the Rudd piece gets across
there.
From “Rosna” in London: —
Australia and the army continue to keep their
friends busy nibbling wedding-cake. One of the
pretty Wesley Hall girls, of Melbourne —her whole
name is Florence Mildred Georgina Canning has
announced that she will try to make a better man
of Capt. Charles Edward Dunscomb Bridge, of the
Artillery, lie is a son of a brigadier-general, who
dwells at Reading when the Hun is eating sausages
at home. The Wesley Halls, you remember, are
Mt. Morganaires.
The Kilmany Park Pearsons’ younger daughter
Laura was married at Holy Trinity, Brompton, to
Capt. Claude Stewart Jackson, of the Coldstream
Guards, who dashed over from France on purpose.
On this occasion, too, there was a brigadier-general
with a D.S.O. and half the alphabet to sign the
register. He was the benedict’s brother and best
man, while the other Pearson maid, Helen, upheld
her sister. Some of the Pearson money also came
out of a mine—at Walhalla.
I may further be permitted to mention that Capt.
L. C. Pitman, of the Royal Engineers, who is a son
of H. H. Pitman, of Christchurch, has an appoint-
ment at the church with Mrs. K. F. Meiklejohn, a
Scottish lassie and widow of a lieut. in the Queen’s
Own Cameron Highlanders. Maoriland transfers
already signed and sealed are those of Dr. Cyril J. A.
Griffin, who is with the Army Medical Corps, and is
a son of the clerical Griffins of Auckland, to Jessie
Elizabeth Pinn, a S’African damsel; and Archibald
Kerr Arthur-W orsop, of Wanganui, who is with the
M.L. Medical Corps, to Margaret Leigh-Lye, daughter
of a Somerset' parson.

The latest addition to the luxuries or
Coombe Cottage is a large swimming-bath,
built in the open air on Grecian lines. it is
of marble, and has a blue lining which
matches the Lilydale sky.
On the Russians’ first appearance at the
Auditorium on Saturday night Melbourne
saved its musical reputation again by turn-
ing up in tramloads —not a bad performance,
considering the other shows available. -
the Auditors took kindly to pianist Mirovitch,
but found Michael Paistro of the fiddle some-
what cold, though superbly correct.
It seems a sensible thing to forward Miss
Niau with the latest batch of Australian
nurses for the French lighting zone, fehe
teaches French in the Newcastle High
School, and will pour irregular verbs into
our Nightingales’ ears on the voyage. This
will avert the tragedy of our capable women
failing to understand the delightful compli-
ments of Francois. He, poor fellow, will
certainly do his best, even when bandaged
out of recognition.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Ses”: “Minding bandicoots” is one of
the most dismal tasks on earth, and shooting them on
a moonless night the most precarious, there being
seldom anything more tangible than the thuds of the
retreating animal to aim at. The long-snouted crea-
ture departs with such emphatic thumps as to give
the impression that it hops like a kangaroo, whereas
it runs, or trots. In scrub lands, where logs and
stumps at first preclude the use of the plough, the
farmer endeavors to dodge the mischievous marsupial
by covering the track of his hoe. A long, narrow-
bladed implement is used, which, after being driven
into the ground, is prized back a little to enable the
worker to drop in his seed. Then the soil is pressed
back into its place. But “Boo” has good eyes and a
keen sense of smell. He does not wander about
prospecting here and there, but follows the row and
empties the holes as they come. In ploughed ground,
however, he does a lot of mining for little pay, for
there is no “lead,” nothing to indicate where the
“pockets” are. Some planters tar the grain, which,
though not eaten, is still scratched up; others set
traps along the alignment. The trap is carefully
buried, and a few giains of corn are sprinkled over
the spot; or it is set as for rabbits and baited with
peas, apple or carrot.
“X.Y.”: “Spreader” was lately puzzled by
Johnson grass, which at some stage or other in its
growth he had found mysteriously poisonous.
Towards maturity the starch cells become converted
into sugar; but "before this stage is reached the
starch is readily broken up into carbonic-acid gas.
If eaten at this time the green feed undergoes an
abnormally rapid decomposition and the gas causes
pressure on the vital organs, especially on the heart
and lungs, causing death quickly. It has been
noticed that when horses ate the young shoots of
Johnson grass (it starts early in the spring) they
became salivated to an extent that was simply mar-
vellous, the water falling from their mouths in half-
pints. The sale of the seed of this pest should render
the trader liable to severe punishment, as its intro-
duction renders the best alluvial soil almost worth-
less for farming purposes. The only use I can think
of for Johnson is suggested by the Germans. It
has been discovered that they have been importing
hundreds of tons of heather from Scotland to be
nitrated and used as an absorbent in the manufacture
of high explosives,: and the writer, with considerable
experience in that line, is prepared to back Johnson
grass, or even broom millet, against heather for this
purpose, at any time.
* * * *
“Barcoo”: Re the Haunted Hills (Vic.),
where it was recently alleged that cattle would
always rush when camped at night. Years ago when
large mobs of bullocks walked from Queensland into
Victoria there was one “haunted” spot that banged
all others. This was on the Old Man Plain, midway
across it, between Hay and Deniliquin (N.S.W.). The
cattle would all be nicely resting, when a grey pony,
known as “The Trotting Cob,” would tear up to the
mob at a great bat and go right through them. Then
there would be a scatter and 50 or 60 missing. At
daylight would arrive a handy mail-change man. He
was a wizard at finding lost bullocks—at a price.
This grey pony was by many superstitious stockmen
looked on as supernatural, but on the last occasion
he performed it was against a saltbush Bill who was
short of supersitions. He had a Winchester, though,
and that settled it.
* * * *
“A.A.8.”: Paragraphs have appeared in
recent issues declaring that eagles had been shot,
trapped or otherwise secured which have measured
Bft., 9ft. and upwards from tip to tip of wings.
Hr. Leach, of the Education Department, Victoria,
an eminent authority, author of the “Bird Book of
Australia,” asserts that there is no authentic record
of an eagle having shown a larger spread than 7ft.
If any of your contributors can supply him with
undoubted evidence that an eagle with a wing-spread
of Bft. or , over has been shot or otherwise obtained
he will be glad to have it.
“Devilfish”: “Hay” (B. l/6/’l6) may be
right in saying that there is only one species of
emu in Australia now. The Tasmanian variety of the
bird certainly differed from that common on the
mainland, being amongst other things smaller and
davker. They were exterminated with great enthusi-
asm in the early days, and were wiped clean out in
about 50 years. It is said that the emus found on
King Island, in Bass Straits, and also long since
extinct, were a third variety, differing from both the
Tasmanian and the mainland species, but no one
seems to know anything very definite about them.
“SEEING LIFE."
“E.T.L.”: It is 114 miles from Tocumwal
to Narandera (N.S.W.) and the train takes eight
hours to do it. I was one of the passengers and
was wondering why the department had compiled its
timetable on such stage-coach lines, when the cara-
van pulled up with a sigh, and the entire train
dismounted with fire buckets and boxes to harvest a
crop of mushrooms someone had sighted. The train
got to the next station on time by crowding on all
sail and dashing along at the rate of 10 miles per
week.
WAR ECONOMY-BREAD AND CHEESE AND KISSES.
“Billy T.”: I have been looking over a
poultry farm conducted by a man who professes to
know hens “in and out and all over.” He claims
that his output per fowl is superior to that of any
chicken-fancier in the business, not numerically, but
considered in bulk. He piloted me to the laying-in
department, and showed me how. His nests were
neatly arranged, and right in front of every laying
hen, staring her in the face, was a large, artificial
egg, about the dimensions of the ordinary product of
the emu. “Force of example,” said Blind. “It also
inspires the sportin’ spirit in a hen. She mightn’t
be able to do as well as that, but she has a try for
it.” Of course I laughed. Blind was hurt. He
pointed out the case of a notorious Scriptural sheep-
breeder as a historical precedent, and I was silenced.
It appears the notion is not merely the outcome of
Blind’s weird fancy; it has the support of the
managers of numerous large egg plants in France
and Italy, and results strengthen the opinion that
every hen should have an ideal.
* * *
“Warre”: “M.J.’s” lambmarking record
(B. 8/6/’l6) is nothing to ring the church bells
about. Remember a competition on a river station
nearly half a century ago when two “cutters” set
one another. Five catchers were allotted to each
man, who had to do his own tailing and earmarking
—the latter operation was done in those days with
the cutting knife, the top of the ear being sliced
off. As a further handicap, the scrotum wasn’t
then slit as now, but the top was removed. After an
eight-hour run the winner had put through 5850
lambs and the other bloque was a close second with
5520. Reckon either of those with the help of
earmarkers and tailers could put through 12,000 a
day.

“Sarto”: What manner of man is “Nil”
to sneer at such names given to our wild
flowers as “cockatoo-claw,” “spiders,” “dog-
face,” “old-man’s-beard” (also called “billygoat’s
beard”), and the children who named them? Him
and his “day’s eye,” “heartease,” and “queen of the
meadow ’! The Australian “cockatoo-claw,” “spiders,”
“dogface,” “old-man’s beard” or “billygoat’s beard”
were so christened because they resemble those things.
The names show initiative and imagination, two
assets that go to make the best type of men and
women. On the other hand, whoever saw a flower
that looked like a “day’s eye” or a “heart’s ease’ ?
Nobody that wasn’t jimjammy. Such names give
one the impression that they are the result of a
halfpenny-paper competition. As for “Nil” being told
to mind his own business by the Minister for Educa-
tion to whom he suggested that committees should
be created to select names for Australian wild flowers,
I emphatically shout “Hooray.” I'll also shout for
the Minister if he can spare the time and the money.

RAFFERTY’S PRAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Poor Rafferty was dead at last;
His trembling widow wept aghast,
And little, white-faced children stole
In sombre garb of dreadful dole,
While men with grave and kindly eyes
Spoke words of comfort good and wise.
A man whose every day and night
Had been a never-ending fight
With circumstance, the cruel blow
Of dark mischance had laid him low.
And all the golden dreams of peace,
Where weary vigilance shall cease,
And men with energy outworn
At least can sleep, though tired and torn,
For Rafferty had been in vain ;
He lived in strife and died in pain.
His was the daily life through which
All men must blunder, poor and rich;
Oft overtaken unaware
By debt or sickness and despair;
Dragging along through aching hours
Dumbly his half-exhausted powers ;
Laid low, when things began to mend,
By fate, by folly or a friend—•
One of a navvies’ gang, he drew
His cheques on life as monarchs do;
For life is but a painful game
Where, high or low, men do the same:
Fight for their rights, avenge their wrongs,
Are whipped with sticks and bound with thongs,
And face their sorrows with their songs.
A priest of solemn mouth was nigh
To watch the wasted navvy die.
“Mother of God,” so said the priest,
“This soul, from human pangs released,
Now through Thine intercession may
Rise to Heaven’s immortal day.
Pray then,” he murmured to the wife,
“For Rafferty’s eternal life;
Through day and night and night and day
Go down upon your knees and pray.”
And Widow Rafferty made moan
Upon her knees to reach the Throne,
And all her children knelt to plead
That by God’s grace the soul be freed.
Then the great heavy hand of God,
Which sometimes lays on men a rod
Of such intolerable woe
That only men could suffer so,
Or lifts them up from dreary pain
Making their spirits bold again—
The Hand went out through endless night
To that black place beyond man’s sight
Where the dead souls for evermore
Lie heaped, and ever millions pour;
And on the soul of Rafferty
God’s finger touched unerringly;
A Voice of awful thunder spoke:
“Awake!” And Rafferty awoke.
The Soul looked out with fearful eyes
On the illimitable skies ;
Immortally awake, it saw
The terrors of eternal law,
The ceaseless doom of life renewed
To uttermost infinitude;
Life with its woes, its fret, its pang....
And through the dismal waste there rang
A shriek, that swelled from sky to sky.
And still the echo would not die
’J ill on the throne of God’s command,
Where Mary and the Saviour stand,
It struck—and God withdrew his hand.
What said the navvy? What prayed he?
“Dear Gocl, for Christ’s sake, let me he!”
N-S.W.
ADAM McCAY.

boot worn by members of the Expeditionary Forces. We are mak-
ing them in thousands to the order of the Government, who have
an inspector to examine and pass every pair before delivery is taken.
Some pairs, when finished, fail to pass him—not for any fault that
really affects their wear or comfort, but for such minute defects as
one boot being as little as one-eighth of an inch higher at the back
than the other; one slug being out of place; or the heel not being
sufficiently even to pass the spirit-level test.

“Daybreak”: “Phillip’-’ (B. 4/5/TG) is
wrong in several respects regarding the habits of
mutton-birds. F’rinstance, he says the parents after
having hatched the young ’uns clear out and leave
them to live on their own fat. Not so; the old
birds return every night, and their method of feed-
ing their progeny is this. Throughout the day the
old birds collect, and deposit in the stomach, fish,
carrion and any other suitable matter that is of a
fatty nature, and disgorge it down the necks of
the fledglings. Ma and the old man do not return at
the end of March to escort the young ones away ;
they are on the job the whole time, from spring to
autumn. The general mobilisation for departure
begins on April 27, when for three days there is a
strenuous time giving the youngsters preliminary
sprints on the wing preparatory to the final evacua-
tion on the 30th. On that day both old and young-
clear out, and the rookeries are deserted till the
spring comes round again. Where the birds go to
seems to be a mystery. No one knows for sure,
but some say Japan. “Phillip” wildly exaggerates
when he says the mutton-fowl's egg is. as big- as a
goose’s. ’Tis about the size of a duck’s, and some-
thing similar in taste. 1 know; I've sampled them.
The young birds when cooked unsalted make a very
tender and delicious bit of picking. While the catching
season is on men live on them alone for over six
weeks without a change of menu. Some doctors say
they are very beneficial as a diet for consumptives, and
the mutton-bird oil, which is better than the best
cod-liver juice, is prescribed for all ailments of the
lungs.

“Specco” : Skins may be tanned this way.
Cut off the useless parts; then soften the pelt ln-
soaking, so that all flesh and fat may be scraped
from the inside with a blunt knife. Soak in warm
water for an hour, and during that time mix equal
parts of borax, saltpetre and Glauber salts with
enough water to make a thin paste. About loz. of
each ingredient will give enough for an opossum
skin and proportionately more will be required for
larger pelts. When the hide has soaked in the warm
water lift it and spread it out flat, so that the paste
may be applied with a brush to the inside. Double
the skin together, flesh side inwards, and place it in
a cool place for 24 hours, at the end of which time
it should be washed clean and treated in the same
way as before with a mixture of loz. sodium car-
bonate (washing soda), -loz. borax and- 2oz. hard
white soap.

“Lou” : Ilore tire a few of the pet names
given by wieldcrs of the pick and banjo to the
ganger: “The red light,” “the big bloke,” ‘Hhe gun,”
“the nark,” “the spur,” “the shelter,” and “the
nit.” And here’s how they fling him their resigna-
tions: “Write mine out,” “Scratch me off,"’ “Gimme
a slip,” “Get out yer book,” “I’m snatchin’ me
time,” “Give us a leaf,” “Tally me,” “I've called for
the rent,” “Sign my brief.” Heard a ganger one day
as he wrote out the docket quietly ask “Why ?” The
bloke in the same quiet tone replied: “The call of
the uncorked.”

“►S.” : A correspondent of Adelaide Regis-
ter (13/6/’l6) declares that, having inadvertently
crushed two legs of a tarantula in capturing it, he
later watched the beast deliberately bite the two
useless members off. The lower forms of life often
shed their appendages; but deliberate amputation
seems anew thing.
“5.0.5.” : I’ve come to the conclusion
that, my old horse ought to have his life-line ex-
amined. I've owned him for 11 years, and he has
scratched 30,000 miles behind in that period, his
travels round the horizon before being caught and
his trips on ordinary camp messages not included.
How do I know the distance? Well, I’m an in-
specting surveyor, and my calling compels me to keep
an accurate diary. He’s won four blue ribbons and as
many reds in return for his performances at country
shows in the hunting, hack and best-all-round series,
and if he wasn’t a grey he could very nearly fetch
off a buggy-horse prize even now. He was entire
until four, and to a stranger is the worst ever to
catch. I got him for £l2.

“Tweed River”: If any bushman will read
me the riddle of the habitat and habits of the local
black cockatoo when nesting and rearing a family
I will say a prayer for the reprieve of his grand-
mother’s soul. I have questioned likely people from
the Burdekin to the Boga and am still without an
answer.
* * * *
"E.E.S.” : I pass the scientific moniker of
the vegetable; everybody outback is acquainted
with it as the Devil’s puff-box. If nothing else
will stop bleeding get hold of one (plenty of them
in Queensland and N. S. Wales), break it open and
apply the spongy side to the wound. I’ve seen it
turn the tap off when nothing else short of a medical
congress could do the job.
* * * *
“Hay” : When hungry for fruit the bush-
man samples all kinds of wild tack. I have even
tried to swallow a mouthful of the deceitful wild
orange. This one was green, and was about as
satisfactory as a mouthful of concentrated turpen-
tine. The tree, a pretty little thing, flourishes near
the north-west corner of N. S. Wales, and with its
bright, evergreen foliage is decidedly ornamental.
The wild cucumber, found in the north-east parts of
South Aus., is more satisfying. This is a pear-
shaped fruit, the product of a creeping vine of the
clematis kind. When young and tender it is juicy
and eatable, though rather tasteless. The plant is
also known as milk-bush, owing to a milky juice
exuding from the stems when broken. It is probably
a native cotton-bush, for when the pears or cucum-
bers ripen they burst open and expose a silky, white
fibre.

“D.F.”: Hobart has its queer pub-names
still. There’s the Salutation Inn, the Ocean Child,
the Man at the Wheel, the Waggon and Horses, the
Steam Hammer, the Old Commodore, the Doctor
Syntax, and the Hope and Anchor. Some of those
that have passed out are the Cat and Fiddle, the
Hole in the Wall, the Three Trunks, the Load of
Mischief, and the Black Snake.

N.s.w. - LIBERTY MAN.
“Hay”: There is a small annual plant of
the back country which springs up in large quanti-
ties whenever a good rain happens. It starts' as an
insignificant circular weed growing flat on the
ground; then a middle stalk clothed with long
notched leaves runs up to a height of about 18
inches. The spike bears a bunch of little white
flowers, followed by seeds a bit larger than rape
seed. The leaves of this plant are the hottest things
in the shape of green vegetables I have ever
sampled, and the flavor is hardly distinguishable
from the best brand of mustard. Binghi is very
fond of it. He calls it “ooticha,” a word which,
sounding like a sneeze, seems to properly describe
the powerful condiment. After a dry spell, when
the blaekfellow has been living entirely on meat
and a few permanent roots, anything green is in
great request, and as Soon as the ooticha appears
the gi ns bring it into camp in nets. I don’t know
whether the fiery plant would have any value com-
mercially, but I can say for myself that, as a
relish, it has often made an otherwise unappetising
meal very enjoyable.
* * * *
A recent importation into the Speck is
the European currant borer, called for short Aegeria
tipuliformix. The hunt for the San .Tose scale in
Launceston has shown that the insect is going strong-
all over that city. The caterpillar gets into the
pith of the bushes and eats them right down to the
ground-level in a few years. The only, known remedy
seems to be to grub up the infested vegetables and
burn them.
* * * *
“D.F.”: What will probably be Private
Adrian Evans'3 closest call came before he started
for the Front. He was standing on the road near
Wynyard, holding a horse by the bridle, when a
tree crashed over, knocked the horse out of his
hand, and made it the deadest beast in Tasmania.
Evans was thrown down, but got off with a few
bruise.5.
“Llib” : This is one thing that immigrants
who go on the land ought to know—the sensitive
plant. It has seized on hundreds of acres in the
Bloomfield district (Q.), and is making a name for
itself on the Daintree and around Cooktown. I find
some of it now in the Charters Towers district. It
carries a. small thorn, and you cannot coax horses
over a patch of it with a sapling.

THE OLD GENT. : “Why don’t you follow that
young man’s example?”
THE YOUNG P IRATE: “ Gripes, mister, me

afternoon to hook one.”
They’ve learned something new about the
hawk in Maoriland. lie’s a fisherman. For a long
time past he has been suspected of poaching trout,
but no definite charg-e could be brought against him
until recently, in the Canterbury district, one of the
family was seen to rise from a creek clinging to
something in his claws. A hundred feet or so in
space the bird dropped the cargo, which was picked
up on the roadside by two spectators. It was an
albino trout weighing a pound and a half.

From this paper’s old-established Mel-
bourne showman: —
The advent in Melbourne of another Italian opera
co. has set the old heads pondering over bygone
bunches of Italian names. Recollections dating back
to 30 years ago are mostly confused between Martin
Simonsen’s three companies. The first (1886),
which stayed nearly a year in Australia, included
Lazzarini and Ciuti, a tenor and soprano who had
starred in their time at Paris Grand Opera House.
Other principal women were Tagliavia (mezzo),
Alice Rebottaro (light soprano), and Cavalleri (con-
tralto), with Flo Graupner as an Australian extra.
Flo was a mere girl at the time, but she had to
paint wrinkles on her young face and play Martha
in “Faust.” The baritones were Pimazzoni and
Cerne; Buzzi and He Alba supplied the basso re-
quirements, and Santinelli was the other tenor. The
two bassos and Ilazon, the conductor, remained in
Australia when the rest of the co. went back to
Italy.
In 1888 old Simonsen made an unlucky venture
with a band of Spanish mandolin players and a
mixed English and Italian opera co. The foreigners
this time were Dimitresco and Lencioni (tenors),
Pimazzoni, once again as the baritone,
(basso), and Rosina Ajrno and Parmiggiana
(sopranos). A French contralto —Louise Lablache —
was specially imported for Carmen purposes, whilst
the principal women on the English side of the
organisation were Lilian Tree and Flo Graupner.
They appeared together in “The Rose of Castile.”
Simonsen had another try in 1891 with a co. which,
under a stronger financial management, would pro-
bably have been a winner. Or perhaps the bursting
of the land-boom had made it impossible to run
Italian opera at a profit just then. Cuttica was the
prima donna attraction in this case, Emilia Guidotti,
the other soprano, being put out of the running by
sickness. After which she made a happy marriage
in Melbourne. The tenors were Cuttica’s plump and
often mellifluous husband and Vilalta. The con-
traltos were Cavalleri once more and Castiglioni.
Melossi (great as Rigoletto) and Sisco attended to
the baritone duties, and Travaglini and lorio were
profoundly basso. The two Cuttieas—when the hus-
band was in good voice-—with lorio and Travaglini
were a mighty strong combination of voices; more-
over, the winsome little lady had a lot of dramatic
talent and was convincing in every part she took.
All recollections of Tancioni Cuttica are unanimous
in rating her as the most charming operatic artist
ever heard in this country; nevertheless Simonsen
went broke over the season, and the co., after filling
a Williamson engagement, had to give some popular-
price concerts before they left the country.
In 1893 a small Italian co. came along under
W. and M. management to introduce “Cavalleria,”
“Pagliacci” and “L’Ami Fritz” to Australia, with the
Blue Ballet as second half of the bill. Giraud,
Caruson, Frasca. and Roussel were the men, and
Italia Del Tarre the prima donna. Then, after a
lapse of 18 years, came the crowd engaged by George
Allan and Ilazon, who appeared at the Williamson
theatres. This was perhaps the strongest all round,
and certainly the most numerous, of the Italian
combinations. The ladies were Benedetto, De Vere
Sapio, Bassich, Vaccori, Marcomini and Marchi. The
men were Larizza, Dani, Cattadori, Cannonieri,
Bozzoli, Lombardi, Tondini, Salvarani, Savini and
Travaglini, the basso of 20 years earlier. The old
opera-goers who think back from 15 to 30 years
have all these Italian names about their brain, but
find it hard to place them in their respective periods.
The confusion is aggravated by the fact that three of
the names—Cavalleria (contralto), Pimazzoni (bari-
tone) and Travaglini—figured in two companies, but
not twice together.
“Circle”: There was a clanking sound as
if all the cows were coming home, and a
female grenadier covered in furs and with
about half a hundredweight of rattling silver
chatelaine in her fist steamed into a front
stall and dropped anchor. Her escort got
her a programme and a box of chocolates, and
as soon as she had gushed a little and filled
her mouth she started to jabber. Presently
she swung round to signal a woman friend,
and the silver gear slipped unnoticed off her
lap. The elderly and fascinated Billjim who
was occupying the next seat touched her
lightly on her gloved forearm and pointed at
the floor. “ ’Scuse me, missus,” lie said,
“you’ve dropped yer tools.”
“R. 8.”: George Black’s film censorship
scheme (N.S.W.) may not be good in its
constitution, but it’s wanted all right. I saw
this week a film, a rotten thing produced by
a diseased mind from the scenario of a
gorilla. The alleged plot turns on the theft
of a Judge’s children. The girl—at about
the age of 13, apparently—is to be handed
over to a filthy ruffian with a hook instead of
a right hand. The “big” scene is the struggle
of the girl to escape the lust of the ruffian
and of his son, ending with papa et fils killing
each other. No harm to grown-ups, because
the melodrama is so bad as to be farcical,
but children shouldn’t see such insults to
intelligence. The minds of many of these
great motion-picture producers need constant
douches of undiluted permanganate of potash.
Business, Bobbery, etc,
The despised rabbit is the juicy export
nowadays. In May last year 511,095 pairs
of bunny left Sydney for somewhere; in
May, 1916, the figures jumped to 1,661.226
pairs; and for 11 months the exports have
totalled 8,911,189 pairs. The demand for
the canned or frozen rabbit is so lively that
for the moment the cocky who mostly raves
at the curse allows trappers on his ground
for a “concession” and thanks Allah for the
blessing.
* * * *
From a former Australian shipping man
now in his native Holland
Our shipping trade is paralysed. After the Tuban-
tia affair no company cared to send its ships out, and
several incoming Dutch steamers were detained
en route. The Nederland and Rotterdam Lloyd and
several other passenger lines have temporarily sus-
pended their mail services, so that we are practi-
cally cut off from our possessions in the East. Our
two Java mail services decided since the beginning
of this year to abandon the Suez route and send their
steamers via the Cape, entailing an additional voyage
of 12 days. Now even that service is stopped.
Moreover, all Dutch steamers, both inward and out-
ward, and even those trading with our own colonies,
are held up on the high seas by the Entente (if
they have not previously been torpedoed or mined by
the Germans) and conducted to British ports, where
not only their cargo but even their passengers and
mailbags are carefully examined. So, for instance,
I received the other day a letter from my Dutch
brother in Dutch Java posted by the Dutch mail
and marked “opened by (British) Censor.” There
is no redress ; we must just suffer and try to look
pleasant, even though we don't feel so.
The petition of Bahar Nalila Yousef to
his Excellency The Bulletin’s old friend
Brown: —-
■ God Save tub Kino.
To His Excellency Major Brown.
Respected Sir, —Your servant has the greatest
honor to inform you that 1 possess the piece of land
Where the crop, about which you were so kind to
write to the authorities, is found. Here 1 say that
f will offer from my own accord this piece of land
to be used by the military authorities as long as His
Majesty’s troops stay here, and this I do without
taking or looking for any damages or rent.
For this, anil also to prevent any trouble caused either
to us or to your men by foreign persons and men not
of our family, we beseech you to be so kind as to
give us the sole right for keeping the Kantine with
the rest of the members of our family. My son
Kamel is well acquainted with the English language,
and we will charge him with the work, so that there
will be no, misundestang between your men and
the villagers. We hope you will be so kind as to
hand over this request to the high authorities, men-
tioning - Kamel in the letter will get. With great
respect.
Yours very truly, I am, your obedient Servant,
BAHAR N All LA YOUSEF.
P.S. —This piece of land is about one acre, and is
used for the blankets and some few tents.
You remember, Sir, the case of the box which
was stolen by one of another village. Thus our whole
idea by doing this and offering our land is to prevent
any foreign person from coining here either in selling
and buying or for doing anything, in this case we
will be responsible for everything, and we will do
our best to facilitate everything for you.
Humbly I remain,
Your obedient Servant,
Yours very truly,
BAHAR NAIILA.

“Try Fluke” : Some of these days there’ll
be a bailing-up on the high seas that will
send the large, salt tears rolling down the
hard cheeks of the underwriters. Not long
ago the Moana took pretty well half a mil-
lion sterling in gold to California. In the
’Frisco papers of last mail I read that the
Java-Pacific liner Karimoea went out
through the Golden Gate a few weeks ago
with a minted cargo that Customs officials
described as the largest ever taken across the
Pacific. It totalled, British reckoning, nearly
£740,000. Most of it was in 20-dollar gold
pieces for Batavia, sent on account of
Amsterdam banks to maintain their credit
in Java. To a potential pirate like myself
it is painful to see all this good red gold
passing the front door to swell the coffers
of the fat banks and fatter Dutch merchants.
Who is on for a little cutting-out expedition,
in a rakish low steamer —Michael Scott
schooners areCout of date—with a brace of
wicked four-point-sevens and a masked crew
of gentlemen adventurers? I’ve a sweet
little isle of coral and cocoanuts in my mind
where the haul could be stowed and distri-
buted amongst us poor but honest men. And
there’s positively no danger : it could always
be blamed on to the Germans. I\S. —Besides,
think of the movie rights!

“Tiaki”: This is how it’s done. Henare
Haruru wanted a motor-car. He negotiated
with McFlint, the lessee of his share in the
Paikoko block, for a deposit of £75 on a.
prospective sale. Te teposhet secured the
car, and Henare commenced the consumption
of benzine on wholesale lines. Then news
came from the Native Land Board that it
would not sanction the sale, and the agent
pounced down on the car. The lessee, for
his part, is charging Henare a trifle of oyer
1000 per cent, for te teposhet and deducting
the same from te rent. 1 met Henare industri-
ously hiding something under his overcoat. “I
s’ow him te point,” he said. “He got te prurry
car orright, but he no good to him. Book
here. He earn’ wine him up. I kot te
proomin’ key.” But Henare and his wahine
will live on spuds and eel for a year.
* * * *
Hume Brothers, of Adelaide and Mel-
bourne, have an Australian invention for
making water-pipes out of cement, which is
going, they reckon, to revolutionise the
business. They started in Adelaide, and
when a big lot of sewerage pipes were
wanted in Melbourne they shoved in a
tender, thinking they might be given a bit of
the work to go on with. When they found
they had got the contract for the lot
they gasped twice and then started to put
up works in Melbourne in a hurry. Now
they have their first big job for water-
pipes, as distinct from sewer drains. The
llobart Council has given them a contract
for seven miles of line at about half the
£30,000 that steel pipes would have cost.
The Ilumes are going to start works on the
spot, partly because pipes may be wanted
by-and-b.ye for irrigating the midlands.
* * * * •
One of the queer sidelines of Business,
Bobbery, etc., was revealed in Sydney the
other day when an attempt was made to
secure a conviction for false pretences against
two men who had endeavored to sell falsely-
stamped jewellery. The business of one of
the men was to buy up old gold, turn it into
modern jewellery, and stamp it with a num-
ber. The other man was what is known as
“a galloper”; liis business was to run the
stuff around to pawnbrokers. The prosecu-
tion failed on the plea that no false repre-
sentation was made that the number —say,
.15 —indicated the carat. It transpired that,
apart from the Victorian hall-mark, the Aus-
tralian public is entirely unprotected against
falsely-stamped jewellery. The Common-
wealth insists that imported baubles be
correctly marked; but once past the Customs
anything may happen, so far as the Com-
monwealth is concerned. ' The Working
Jewellers’ Union has been barracking for
years for a proper gold-marking Act in N. S.
Wales, and when a man claims (as the chief
defendant in this case did) to have been
conducting his “business” for 20 years there
seems to be reasonable justification for the
disturbance.
* * * *
The case concerned a charge of pilfer-
ing cargo, described as the property of
the P. and O. S. N. Co.; but it had been
discharged from the ship at Port Jackson,
and was on its way to the consignees when
the theft occurred. The point, therefore,
arose, that there was no proof of ownership
in the P. and O. Co.; and, in conformity
with judgment in the case of Bloggs v.
Blithers, his Honor discharged the indict-
ment. Then the Law, in haste, drew up a
charge of theft and unlawful receipt of
articles, the property of some person or per-
sons unknown. As they were not the prop-
erty of the consignees, by whom they had not
yet been received, it seemed the only way
out; but it is understood that Costs is wad-
ing through all the muddled library of Prece-
dent to see how much the business can be
further profitably tangled.
Eric Wright, of Southport (Tas.), sees
money in kelp, and has got some of it out.
With very primitive appliances he has
already gathered and burnt enough of it to
produce between 20 and 30 tons of ash, assay-
ing 32 per cent, of potash. Before the war,
when all Australia’s potash came from Ger-
many, this would have been worth £9 per ton,
but prices have soared since then. As Aus-
tralia used to import an annual 5000 tons,
there ought to be room for a whole lot of
kelp-burning, and there’s no chance of
exhausting the raw material.
THE HAPPY TRAMP.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I see him there across the street;
He sits against the wall,
A raffish, frowsy, friendless beat,
With broken boots upon his feet,
He wears no shirt at all.
He ducks the barber and his ills ;
He’s fretted with no laundry bills.
And though he has not washed to-day
He never will repine ;
He lets the morning drift away,
And scorns the need of making hay
Howe’er the sun may shine;
An outcast thoroughly content,
Who’s never called on for the rent.
With borrowed match he lights the fag
He found upon the pave;
With nothing but a battered swag
And broken victuals in a bag
He has no call to save ;
He sits and grins at all the fates,
A wretch exempt from water rates.
He sniggers at the busy bees
In quest of many crafts;
Why should a free man envy these
Their income tax, and dentist fees,
And little overdrafts ?
One can be happy, he has found,
Who doesn’t owe a single pound.
He feels he lives the life complete,
Of gas-collectors rid,
And owing no account for meat,
And though he has no friends to bleat
For loans of half a quid.
He teaches us, this vagrant stout,
How much a man can do without!
VICTORIA.
SILAS SNELL.
LOVE'S LABOR LOST:
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A letter posted in Mildura in 1913 was delivered
in Melbourne last week.
. I sent a note to my ensnarer,
Saying I’d loved her all my life,
That none to me was dearer, fairer,
So would she be my wife?
Impatient, her reply I waited,
But weeks passed and it never came;
I cursed each rhyming fool who’d prated
Of Love’s enduring flame!
I left sweet honle and sought to borrow
Surcease from pain in sundry ways;
I quickly overcame my sorrow,
And laughter filled my days.
I’d quite forgotten that I’d met her,
For ’twas three years ago I wrote;
And then to-day I got a letter
In answer to my note 1
She was, she said, extremely flattered,
Although my note had missed the ’bus;
’Twas awf’ly sweet to think one mattered.
And then she went on thus:
“But, dear old thing, we cannot marry,
My husband is so strict, you know.
Our Number Two—we’ve named it Harry—
Was born three weeks ago!”
While reading I had grown quite nervous;
Relief, though, quickly banished dread.
God bless the Postal (lack of) Service,
For I am still unwed!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON 0.
THE DIVIDED CRITIC.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I entertain a vast contempt
For things that suit the crowd
In plots by crudest bunglers dreamt.
It pleases me to be exempt
From joy when long and loud
The foolish populace applauds
These shallow old dramatic frauds.
Yet here I make confession fair:
My mind with scoftings wild
May mark the actress tearing hair
And hear her voicing her despair
When driven from her child ;
Yet in my weaker flesh appears
A wretched tendency to tears.
When Jane is cast into the snow,
And callous father cries:
“You have disgraced me old age. Go!”
My splendid brain derides the show,
But water floods my eyes.
This contradiction gives me pain—•
I’m half afraid to go again.
“To my mind” (so I write) “this play
Is most prepost’rous tripe!”
In honesty I ought to say:
“My liver fluttered in its sway,
My spleen was in its gripe.”
Meanwhile am I with doubting racked —
Which of these fools is I in fact?
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
