MELBOURNE:
POLITICAL
POINTS
Massey and Ward are after more revenue
from income tax in Maoriland, and propose
to tackle the job with careful regard for the
feelings of Fat. Thus a man with a tax-
able income of £5OO a year will pay at the
rate of Is. Bd'. in the £l, but if his income
leaps up 100 per cent, the tax will only leap
a lifth of that distance —to 2s. The rest of
the leaping from the £5OO mark goes this
way:—•
Taxable amount. Leap of Income. Leap of Tax.
£ feet. inches.
1500 3 4 4.5
2000 4 6
3000 6 9 3-5
4000 8 12
All of which suggests that Ward ought to
explain why when the citizen on the £5OO
mark is taxed at £4l 13s. 4d., the affluent
individual wallowing in four times this
revenue should be hit up for only £62 10s.
It may be equality of sacrifice, but doesn’t
look like it.
NOT UNDERSTOOD.
H E: “Frightful lot of red tape in the mili-
tary, you know.”
S HE: “Oh, yes. I've noticed it on the offi-
cers' uniforms.”
It at any rate ill becomes shipping people
to gibe at the Commonwealth Government
for their failure to move the wheat harvest
without buying ships of its own. If the
earlier scheme failed it was simply because
Labor Minister, the greed of
patriotic shipowners. If shipping people
outofti,at ’ *■*'«
N. S. Wales Government announces that
the financial year closed last week with “a
surplus of £149,000.” Of course, this refers
to transactions with the left-hand pocket of
the trousers. Holman can’t get his hand in
the other pocket for the jamb of lOU’s.
The Commonwealth’s haphazard system of
recruiting has broken down in another place.
The halting, stumbling Defence Department
now discovers that there is a serious short-
age of metallurgical and technical chemists
owing to their enlistment in the A.1.F., and
the production of war material is thereby
imperilled. Under the scientific plan
ot universal service the danger would
of course not have arisen; the whole
community would have been put to
its best war use. The Government adhered,
however, to the easy, slipshod policy of
muddling through, and, for any difficulties
that may threaten, it and the deplorable lack
ot system for which it stands must he held
wholly responsible.
Have Australian Railway Departments
suddenly gone mad? A few weeks ago
Queensland, being offered linseed oil of
Australian manufacture, turned it down in
tavor of an Indian article. In that case
tnere was the wisp of an excuse that the
foreign stuff was offered at 3d. per gallon
cheaper. But now the Westralian Railways
have accepted an offer of foreign oil at
os. 3d. and ss. 5d., notwithstanding that an
Australian oil was offered at 4s. 4|d. and
4s. Old. Do the Westralian railway people
really think it is so disastrous for Australia
to have population to carry that it will pay
the railways to give people a bonus to live
somewhere else? That, anyhow, is what this
lunacy amounts to.
Hie Hoskins firm, of Litkgow, bought a
coal mine at Dapto the other day; and the
first the public heard about it was through a
whooping paragraph in the Sydney papers
saying that a boom in land had already
started in the neighborhood. In other words,
because the Hoskinses will put their brains
and money and energy into a property, people
who have just been sitting down and wait-
ing for somebody to come along and do some-
thing are going to put up rents for all time-
tor that is wliat this grab at unearned
movement means. When will the Law stop
this robbery of the public? And in this case
the public includes the Hoskinses, because
inevitably, if men have to pay unnecessarily
high rent, they will have to receive unneces-
sarily high wages; wherefore up will go
costs, and by so much will the chance of
successful competition with the foreigner be
diminished.
Even such a crusted old Conservative as
Health-Minister McLeod has been able to
heave a brick at Geelong. To the
local council was l’ecently sent a
letter notifying an outbreak of in-
fectious disease, the envelope being
marked “urgent” and bearing on its
exterior some inkling of its contents.
But it was unstamped; wherefore
Geelong just sent it back and sat
down again in its wallow. It is this
sort of thing which makes it easier
to understand how a big town like
Geelong, while claiming to be a
health resort, has managed to post-
pone until lately the commencement
of an underground sewage system.
Read Murphy, who recently re-
s' gned his job as P.M. to dive into
Victorian politics, isn’t discouraged
by his unsuccessful effort to depose
butcher Angliss from the Leg. Coun-
cil. He means to try again
as soon, as the chance
offers, and to that end has
gone to live in Melbourne.
Some of his platform is
surprisingly good. He be-
lieves that the large, inade-
quately worked estate is the
curse of a nation. Also he
would do without six of
Australia’s seven Gover-
nors and London agents.
Furthermore, he would
make gaol-dwellers self-
supporting, and he ap-
proves of every male Aus-
tralian being a trained
soldier. In fact, almost the
only suspicious circum-
stance about Murphy’s case
is that he calls himself a
Liberal. Why not just call him-
self Murphy?
Blackburn, M.L.A. (A r ic.), who has
started out on the anti-conscription warpath
again, is consistent in one thing, at any rate.
Nine months ago he said in print that the
German conquest of Australia would hardly
worsen the conditions of the country; and
the place of those who think that a German
conquest wouldn’t matter is plainly with
the anti-conscriptionists. But his attitude
ought to make all other anti-conscriptionists
think hard. If they believe that German
conquest would worsen Australian condi-
tions, what are they doing in the same
galley as Blackburn?
The Minister for Defence continues to
excuse the failure of voluntary enlistment
by hotly declaring that 300,000 men were
never promised. The Bulletin doesn’t
know whether the Minister promised 300,000
TWO BATTLERS AND A BEAR.
or not. But it does know that the Minister’s
chief promised “the last man” ; and the last
man isn’t the 300,000 th or even the 350,000 th.
“Trot” remembers the people:—
Apart altogether from the commercial merits of
Hughes’s 15 steamers, no one can convince me (and
I do not believe it possible to convince anyone else)
that the spending- of two millions by a politician
without authority from Parliament is anything but
rotten. It outrages every principle of Parliamen-
tary Government. It goes to the very heart of
popular rights, and cannot be distinguished from the
issue which John Hampden argued over. Parlia-
mentary control of the cashbox in Australia is a jest.
In the case of the Federal Parliament, criticism of
expenditure follows instead of precedes payment.
Authority for expenditure is taken long after the
money has gone. No country on earth could ever
trust its politicians or leaders or kings to go loose
like this. It is no answer to say that Hughes made
a good bargain. That doesn’t matter. The objection
covers the whole field of public expenditure. What-
ever Australia may gain by allowing Hughes to fill
in a cheque for steamers is not counterbalanced by
what it loses every year through the same loose
system. The Parliamentary surrender of the cash-
box to Ministries is one of the wonders of Australian
politics. And how the public is paying for it! And
how the kid is going to sweat!
By all means let us go on talking vaguely
about ways and means of commercially
smothering the German after we’ve kicked
him out of the various Fronts. While we’re
merely fighting and talking, he’s fighting and
working. Bast summer German commercial
travellers were released from the trenches
and sent to neutral Switzerland to hang on
to German trade there and keep it alive.
Also, long after the war had got into its
stride, French surgeons received circulars
from German artificial limb-makers. It calls
for a bit of speed and energy to beat a nation
which blows its enemy’s leg off and promptly
calls round to sell him a ready-made sub-
stitute.
CUPBOARD LOVE.
MR. M OSES: “I doan’dt know, but dere ros
somedings in you dot I like very much."
LITTLE REBECCA: “Who toldt you I shval-
lowed der money?"
Australia is liable to get another black-
eye through its bad habit of depending upon
a foreigner who may be liable to drink and
blind staggers for all it knows. Australia
loafs on the U.S.A. for large quantities of
binder-twine —almost in-
dispensable in harvesting
—and the raw material
for most of it is the sisal
hemp of Yucatan, which
is that part of Mexico
now most anxious to
riddle Wilson with bul-
lets. The Yucatan crop
of sisal is worth about
£3,000,000 a year—
mostly financed by New
Orleans.
If he carries out his
present intention, Yic.
Chief Secretary McLeod
will have done one good
deed deserving mention
on his tombstone. He is
going to enable the gaol-
dweller, or a few of him,
to earn his keep and put
by a bit at the end of his
“stretch.” The reform-
subject is to be employed
on a block of land bought
by the Government near
Castlemaine, where he
will grow fruit and vege-
tables and get paid for
doing it. Only a selec-
tion of well-behaved birds
will be permitted out of
the cage at the commence-
ment, but McLeod hopes
to see the scheme en-
larged as the cab-
bage-growring progresses.
Blinking the fact that
the Minister has con-
sidered it necessary to
buy farm-land while the
Crown declares in its pamphlets that it has
lots ot its own, it is a good scheme as far
as it goes, though McLeod isn’t the sort of
enthusiast to push it to a sensible conclu-
sion.
Acting Prime Minister Pearce prattled the
other ddy about “industrial unrest” in the
best manner of the anguished archbishop
with a mixed congregation:—..
=+,.fi hey , con sWer people who indulged in
strikes and lock-outs as unreasonable and unjust,
there were, however, two sides to everv question,
they could only solve the question by approaching
the matter m a reasonable spirit, from the point of
view of the employer as well as that of the em-
ployee.
Phen doesn’t the Acting-Archbishop consider
people .who indulge in strikes and lockouts
unreasonable ! If he doesn’t, it seems only a
decent thing for him to call off Justice
Higgins, who is administering a law which
regards strikes as so unreasonable that they
are declared illegal. Suppose Pearce learns
to approach the question from the point of
view, not of the employer or the employee,
put °t a third party, the community. That
is the entity that a Government is supposed
to represent.

44 capital from Hobart to Launces-
ton. The Southerners haven’t taken the

they allege that the North has more than

about saving a little and shifting the IOU?

KHYME-BOILER.
ON THE DERELICT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
stated o thnt re thi Ve ! d A om „ Nambucea Heads yesterday
at North k^ ch , Preml «'. which went ashore
at North Head, Nambucca River, had completely
broken up .—Recent winter news item.- 1
Premier Holman, of N.S.W.:
The winter seas are coldly grey
And where the rollers burst in spray
The Premier’s broken up.
O happy ship that died in state,
Beneath the hammer-blows of Fate,
Your omen fills my cup '
i ??
Too weak in spite of my desires
1 To light the patriotic fires,
I shiver, dream and doze;
The rats about the cabin race;
They eat the chart before my face *
And nibble at my toes.
These rats won’t leave the sinking ship
Y hose freight is lost. And so we trip
With any wind that blows.
Yes, we must idly drift about
Until her timbers open out
And, with a roll, she goes.
Oh, would that I had run ashore
Some pitch-black night amid a roar
While bound for port, my friend !
Yes, better end with all sail set,
Than dream and rot in mean regret
Until the bubbly end—-
The choking, bubbly end!
N.S.W.

JUST PUBLISHFD
“GRANDPA’S SELECTION”
chiefly concerns an irascible old gentleman who runs a sort of family farming colony
to such advantage to himself that "his account flows over at the' local bank and
runs into several others.” There are a dozen other equally interesting characters,
who all combine with Grandpa to provide the reader with a great harvest of humor.
“ON OUR SELECTION.”
Steele Rudd’s first great story of Australian bush life. A record seller.
“OUR NEW SELECTION.”
The second success. One of the most popular in the series.
“STOCKING OUR SELECTION.”
“ FROM SELECTION TO CITY,”
“ BACK AT OUR SELECTION.”
“DAD IN POLITICS.”
Dad on his Selection provided a feast of humor; but in Parliament he is the centre
of a whirlwind of laughter.
“SANDY’S SELECTION.”
“ THE BOOK OF DAN.’
“THE POOR PARSON.”
A rattling good tale, describing, the trials, troubles and strange adventures of the
Outback parson.
“ DUNCAN McCLURE.”
A great story of a Scotch squatter—brimful of humorous episodes.
“ON AN AUSTRALIAN FARM.”
A brilliant novel of the Outback. In contrast to his selection stories—most of which
dealt with the struggling selector the author has made a prosperous farming family
the subjects of his sparkling humor.
“THE DASHWOODS.”
The sequel to “On An Australian Farm.”
“ FOR LIFE.”
A great story written round the mysterious Gatton tragedy.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Jullundur’s capture of the G. N. Hurdle
ltace last Saturday was quite in accordance -with the
eternal fitness of things. The son of Challenger was
one of the three youngest horses in the race, and
youth ought to get a fair chance to tell over the
sticks, though the rule is not to put horses to the
jumping game until they have lost a lot of their
spring. Also Jullundur’s good form on the flat, just
prior to the Grand National weights being declared,
was a frank and open exposure of his merits which
the handicapper rewarded by not piling the weight
on him. At lOst. 71b. Jullundur was evidently well
in. He had already won a three-mile hurdle race at
Flemington in record time. But because he had
finished only fourth in a couple of recent races, over
a shorter course, it seemed possible that Jul. had _
started to stale off. Otherwise all followers of the'
guide-books would have preferred him at lOst. 71b.
to Lord Grey at a stone more. Jullundur, who is a
natural rather than an educated jumper, must be one
of the fastest and stoutest racers that ever did well
over the hurdles. The going was heavy on Satur-
day, but he won easily and clipped Jsec. off the
Grand Nat. record, which the 9st. Rathfarnham set
up last year when the ground was firm. Jullundur
has now got two Flemington records to his credit.
He was chased home on Saturday by Van Homreigli,
Lord Grey, Royal Precedent, Beccassine and Boyeo,
the fourth, fifth and sixth being not far away from
the third, who was nearly 10 lengths behind the
winner. Lord Grey began slowly as usual, but the
other five horses were in the leading half-dozen from
start to finish. Nothing fell save Jack Rice, reckoned
the safest jumper in the field. Among the more
fancied of the 17 starters Merrimax, Hypo and Shot
Out ran conspicuously badly.
The hitherto uncertain Meroo made no trouble
about winning the last race on the card at Fleming-
ton on Saturday. Ho went out at (i to I—a1 —a com-
paratively long price against one of Eric Connolly’s
good things, and, with little Eaton up, the good thing
won all the way down the straight. Of four imported
horses that helped to make up the big field Polyga-
mist looked most like a winner in the future.
Lord Grey, with two seconds and a third to his
name, ranks next to Obi for consistent prominence in
G. N. Hurdle racing - . Obi scored two firsts and a
third, but the time was not very good in any in-
stance, whereas it was always fast when Lord Grey
came with a late run. Obi’s sceond win (in 1913)
took a quarter of a minute longer than Jullundur’s.
Bullawarra, in spite of all his weight and his
growing tendency to old age, is reckoned quite a
moral, as morals go, for the G. N. Steeple. If he is
the horse he was when he won the, four-mile Steeple
at Caulfield in 1913, Bullawarra should certainly out-
class the poor field he will meet next Saturday. The
dashing Booligal is under suspicion; Chess, the ex-
hurdler, and Artillery Bill may be better worth
backing on the day ; and Canadium and Highland
Clan are possibles. But none of these is in the same
class as Bullawarra. Nothing can be confidently ex-
pected to beat the old champion after his promising-
display at Williamstown a fortnight ago if he stands
up. A lot of rain before the day is the most likely
thing to beat him. If the going is very heavy the
writer would as soon back Highland Gian as
anything.
The Siege Gun mare Inia has won three
races since James Barden started superintending her
exercise, and her manner of getting away with the
Flying at Canterbury Park on Saturday stamped her
as something above the ordinary. As Inia a few
days earlier had appropriated an event at Rosehill,
beating the flier Boureong, it was surprising to find
McMoses generously offering 9 to 2. Like many
other good ones the mare hails from northern N. S.
Wales. City punters first heard of her when she was
made favorite for the last New England Cup at
Armidale. But the Maorilander Toa Tere landed the
bacon that time at 16 to 1, and Inia also ran.
* * * *
Trainer Billy Kelso has been doing it
twice again. He perpetrated his last joke at Canter-
bury Park on Saturday with Khaf (14 to 1) and
Chid (15 to 1), and if Ikey neglected to meet him
later in a body and give him three cheers then is
there no gratitude in Israel. Apprentice W. Pike
was on top of Khaf. This young member of the
Pike tribe is a brother of Jimmy, who also learned
how to ride winners under Kelso’s tuition. But it
was the trainer’s own son who steered Chid, and it
must have brought to the elder man a surge of mem-
ories. In his day Kelso was good business as a horseman
himself. About the only accident he ever had was
when a domesticated horse slipped from under him
in George-street and broke one of his legs. Kelso
doesn’t ride horses now. He drives with a wheel.
* * * »
The apprentice jock, has become a pest.
On Saturday four of the horde claiming, allowances'
won races on the flat at Canterbury Park, and only one
of the successful nags carried any public confidence
and legal tender. In the Flying 10 of-the 14 runners
were ridden by apprentices. It seems about time the
A.J.C. put up its hand to stop the traffic. Weight-
adjusters handicap on the assumption that each
animal will carry its allotted burden in the event of
starting. When claims are made for allowances of
71b. and 31b. because the riders have not ridden their
share of winners, weights count for nothing, and
any brains that may have been employed in the
effort to compile them are wasted. Even if owners
are keen on having inexperienced boys on their prads
that is no reason why the public should not be pro-
tected by requiring every horse to start from the
right mark.
* * * *
“Dalriada”: When the final acceptances
for Flemington Grand National Hurdle were declared,
on June 30. the list concluded with this unconscious
aphorism: “Unlikely Starter —Purse Filler.” The
result of the race bore out the forecast.
* * * *
Pony racing continues to boom around
Sydney. Three meetings a week have been the
average lately, and last Saturday it was necessary
to run 13 separate races to accommodate all the
entries and get all the bets in. For the next meet-
ing listed the handieapper was called on to allot
weights to 188 diminutive animals masquerading as
“racehorses.” But of course the “sport” is splendid
and affords employment to many undeserving citi-
zens, and the encouragement given to breeding
thoroughbreds is stupendous.
Died a few days ago in Westralia, Reg
Pell, the best-known handieapper and one of the
best-known sportsmen in the State. lie was the
big-hearted son of Professor Pell, the great mathe-
matician who helped to found the A.M.P. Society;
but in his youth he preferred athletics to study, and
was the honorary referee at most of the big battles
which were fought at Broken Hill in the days of the
great boom. In that boom Pell made a comfortable
fortune; but he came to Sydney, and it was soon
taken from him on the turf.
The stake for the M.L. Trotting Cup, to
be run at Christchurch (M.L.) in November, remains
at V 2500. The first past the post collects £l5OO
and a cup, second £5OO, third £3OO, and fourth
£2OO. But pursuit of the cash and crockery this
year is confined to prads that have left two miles
behind in an actual race in 4min. 30see. or better.
This will limit entries considerably.

“Up Stage”: We were touring Taranaki
(M.L.), and had just comfortably settled in the hack
parlor after the show, when the sport entered. After
the third round he professed his admiration for
actors generally, and confided that his pony—Gin
Fizz —-was running at an unregistered meeting the
next day. We thought it was time for bed ! “No,
bli'me, I ain’t puttin’ it up to yer like thet. Come
out —it’ll be jes’ like holdin’ out yer hand.” The
treasury was very thin ; but the sport seemed sincere,
and, anyway, it looked morally certain that we’d have
to “teli the tale” to get out of the town. Follow-
ing afternoon we found the typical bush racecourse.
A hillock fringed with undergrowth rose in the centre
of the course and hid the back, so that when the
horses rounded the turn out of the straight the
punters lost sight of them for about four furlongs.
We got long odds about Gin Fizz; and informa-
tion that he “hadn’t a ghost” floated in from every
side. The sport was, notwithstanding all this,
mysteriously optimistic. “Don’t worry, boys,” he
assured us. “Jim’s the only boy in this race that's
ridden ’ere before.” Turning out of the straight
Gin Fizz was last; but the sport was convulsed with
unholy glee. “Now jes’ wait,” he whispered. “He's
got to fall down to lose!” Anxious years seemed
to pass ; then Gin Fizz flashed into view with a lead
of six lengths, and reached the post with a neck to
spare. We got very enthusiastic over Gin Fizz’s
wonderful sprint along the back. The sport smiled.
“Sprint be blowed!” he said. “Jim was the only
one who knew the short cut!”
* * * *
Though pigeon racing has not much vogue
as a sport it is a desperately calmest business with
those who do make it a hobby. Over 1000 birds
were let loose in Bundanoon last Saturday to fly 100
miles to Sydney. Many birds never get back in
these contests, the first cause of mortality being the
valiant sportsman with a gun who waits for the
scurry homewards. This Hun-like trick is a
penai offence, but the offenders are hard to catch,
being usually dug into a place of safety from attack
by infuriated pigeons or J. Hops.
After Jimmy Hill (N.S.W.) and Bert
Spargo (Vie.) had been wrangling over the feather-
weight championship for 20 rounds at Sydney Sta-
dium on Saturday, the verdict of Arthur Scott was
a draw. It was one of those contests in which any
decision would have been challenged, for the house
was full of heated partisans, and the styles of the
two battlers were in marked contrast. In the
judgment of this writer Hill outpointed his enemy,
but point-scoring in these disturbances is a mys-
terious business. If Scott reckoned that Spargo
by his superiority at infighting, scored as
rapidly as Hill did at longer range, and
that the Victorian’s aggressiveness was entitled to
as much weight as Hill’s elusiyeness, then he was
right. There would be much justification for this
view. But from the point of view of pure skill
with the gloves Hill seemed to have a substantial
lead. His hitting was quicker and more varied ; his
defence more effective. But he hardly deliv-
ered a punch that was not short weight. And, Loid,
how far was it he ran?
It was Spargo who made the fight. He put an
enormous amount of work into it, was nearly always
the aggressor, and showed the greater courage ana
endurance. He never cut it once. Hill did,
several times. Twice at least he could have von
had he been as anxious to go on as he was to hop
away. Still, the Victorian was a bit of a disap-
pointment, and it is hard to imagine lnm seeing
20 rounds through with a good boxer carrying an
emphatic punch, for he leaves himself gaping wide
open for injurious attention on the jaw nearly eveiy
time he uses his left. Hill got him there several times,
but it was only the glove that hit; neither arm nor
shoulder was behind it. It was a fast, strenuous
engagement from first to last —one of the most spec-
tacular seen at the Stadium for many a day. Of
course there will be another encounter, but unless
Spargo can learn to deliver his thump more rapidly
lie stands a good chance of not delivering it at all.

At Dimboola, in the Wimmera (Vic.),
recently, a team of black brothers lined up to play
a local pack football. The abos. were beaten badly.
Amongst the few full-bloods was Kmnear who back
in the ’Bo’s, hit the tape first for the Stawell Gift,
collaring the stake and about £5OO from the^bookies.
Beals Wright, sent to these parts by
Uncle Sam in 1908 with F. B. Alexander to tear the
Davis Cup from Brookes and Wilding, leaped into
the limelight the other day just in time to be seized
by bis famous bad luck, and be yanked out again.
It was at Honolulu, and a lot of Yankee cracks, in-
cluding Griffin, Dawson, Rohlfs and Low, were after
the local championship. Wright got through to the
semi-final, and looked like winning, but when shaving
himself next morning put the razor deep into his
thumb. He only lasted two sets, and then Dawson
took the bauble. Wright was playing as well as
ever he did, and may be heard of doing big things
again in the future —that is, if there is any future.
The Speck’s final cricket spasm didn’t
take place till May, and the averages have only
just appeared. A. C. Newton (S. Hobart) tops the
batting averages with 574 runs; av., 57.4. K. E.
Watt (E. Hobart) was second with 452 runs; av.,
45 2 In bowling the positions are reversed. Watt
heads the list with 56 for 11.92 apiece. Newton (<0
wickets, av. 12.30) is runner-up. Newton s haul ot
70 wickets and his position at the head of the bat-
ting averages constitute a record for the State, the
nearest approach was 09 wickets (highest) and 590
runs (second), by the prolonged C. J. Eady, in
1905-6. -
A motor record which had gathered the
dust of nine years was swept off the slate last
month at New York speedway, when Yankee drive,
R Mulford whizzed over 1819 miles m 24 hour. .
S F Edge (Eng.) held the previous figures (180 l
miles 1350yd5.). The distance averaged per horn
was 75 miles. Mulford, who stuck to the wheel
throughout, had a busy day. Later on he won a
50 miles race in 28min. 45 4-ssec. And over at Cone
Island a car driven by one Aiken rushed ovei
miles in llmin. 15 4-ssec. All of which shows
there was moving. * #
The march of the motor! Three years ago
the Goodyear Co. was turning 5000 tyres a day out
of its factory at Akron (U.S.A.) 0n f JU U\h a
the boss stopped chewing gum for 3sec. to dash a
cable message to Sydney announcing that the output
on that date was 29,976 tyres—a record. Looks
as if the horse is being hustled into the museum
quicker than he # .
The rift between the N.S.W. Howling
Association (Sydney) and the Northern District
Association (Newcastle) has widened into something
that looks like a ditch. the truth at the
bottom of the chasm is that Newcastle and Sydney
don’t mix well. The N.D.A. objects to the parent
which bosses the bowls in the State being made up
of delegates from affiliated clubs instead of from
affiliated bodies. The meeting held to patch up
neaee and entice the errant coalies back to the told
ended with respectable old gentlemen saying most
frightful things, and at present benevolent bowlers
are more bitterly divided than the footballers and
baseballers. By the way, why is it that these strenu-
ous pastimes make everybody so savage t Is there
any real recreation in having a row?
Killed somewhere in France, Lieut.
Murdoch, a son of Jas., M.L.C. “Billy” in his time
was a topnotch footballer in the Speck and represented
Tasmania against mainland teams. #
There appears to have been a vacancy in
the sporting literature of the age, because no one
had compiled a manual and record book devoted to
skating. Well, the vacancy has now been filled by
A. Smith, who has produced an Australian handbook
on roller- and ice-skating 1 . It looks as if the authoi
has gone to infinite trouble over his work. Anyhow,
he has gathered up a vast amount of information
and records and many photographs. Australians are
not the best in the world on ice, on account of the
severity of the winters, but they have done things in
rinks, and if anyone wants to find out all about it
the ljrice is only a shilling.
* *
Tasman Peninsula, the lower half of the
double protrusion from the south-east corner of the
Speck, which early navigators took for a couple
of islands till Baudin’s expedition proved the con-
trary, was turned into a real island the other day.
A hard south-easterly gale brought up a tremendous
sea which broke right through Eaglehawk Neck into
Norfolk Bay. The sea drove an immense number o
perch into the inner bay and local anglers hooked
out 1800 of them in an hour.
“Corio”: “A.B.’s” methods of landing cod
(B 8/6/’l6) are at present in disfavor with the bush
sages.' Give ’em the cross or set lines every time.
The cross line is merely a cord extending from bank
to bank with hooks placed at intervals of two or
three feet. The set-line runs parallel with the bank,
and may extend for a mile or so. Cricket and wood-
grub bait is generally used. But when he goes fish-
ing personally the sage shows the cod a point or
two If shrimp is on the menu well, and good, but
sometimes the animal prefers crayfish or worm His
appetite is very eccentric. At Meniridie (N.S.W.) 1
have landed him with a piece of raw beef, and
successfully used red rag as a signal for breakfast.
Fishing on the backwaters of the Murray I once
used portions of cod itself, and succeeded m getting a
meal. 1 never tried it, but Outback hands tell how
Wallaby Joe once hooked a hundred cod with black
snake as bait. “It wuz boshter, too,” so they say.

THE LAST LAP
or
The Finish of the Argument.
ON THE 6th JULY, 1912,
The General Manager of
The Australian Mutual Provident Society
Mr. RICHARD TEECE, F.1.A., F.F.A., F.A.S., F.S.S., J.P.,
wrote to the Managing Director of
The City Mutual Life Assurance Society Ltd.
Mr. GEORGE CROWLEY, as follows:
“I shall be glad if you will furnish me with specimen bonuses for the first five years
in respect of a policy similar to that in question.”
The policy “similar to that in question” was a policy for £2OOO on the life of
Mr. Arthur Rickard, of Messrs. Arthur Rickard & Co., Realty Specialists, Pitt Street,
Sydney, New South Wales.
The A.M.P. Society issued a policy, No. 9,139,813, in November, 1911, £2OOO on
Mr. Rickard’s life at a premium of £7O per annum.
The City Mutual, within a few days of this date —possibly a week earlier or a
week later—issued a policy, No. 106,583, for £2OOO on Mr. Rickard’s life at a premium
also of £7O per annum.
BOTH POLICIES WERE, THEREFORE, IDENTICAL IN EVERY RESPECT.
The discussion hinged on the much-debated question as to which class of office in
Australia would yield the best results to its policy-holders—the compact Institution
with £1,000,000 or so of easily-invested funds, or the Leviathan with many tens of
millions.
Keeping in view, of course, all the time tliat_the liabilities of the larger office to
its constituents were in the vicinity of £80,000,000 as against the smaller Society’s
£4,000,000 or so.
So that here was a clear-cut opportunity for a straight-out, honest test. It was to
be a fair go.
Needless to say, no exception was to be made in the case of Mr. Rickard’s policy
by either office. It was to be treated exactly the same as each and every other
policy issued that year by each office under the ordinary Whole Life Table —the
true embodiment of Life Assurance, pure and simple. No difference whatever of any
sort was to be made.
That, naturally, was the very first and most primary element of fairness if the test
was to be of the slightest value at all, otherwise the whole thing would have been a farce
between two offices of standing.
Here are the results for the five years of the
AUSTRALIAN MUTUAL PROVIDENT SOCIETY
Here are the results for the five years of the
CITY MUTUAL LIFE:
This is the Last Lap —the Finish
of the Argument.
It is only fair, however, to the A.M.P. Society to say that the figures for 1016 are
affected somewhat by the very heavy claims arising out of the War. Otherwise the
bonuses for 1916, according to Mr. Teece, might have exceeded even £3B, the 1915
figures.
Now that the controversy is ended, just a few —a very few—words in conclusion.
The City Mutual takes its hat off all the time in admiration for the great and
enduring work which the pioneer office of Australia has done during the past 67 years.
It trusts that a friendly spirit will always prevail between the two offices.
There is plenty of room for both in the Commonwealth.
They are both purely Mutual Institutions, the policy-holders—and the policy-holders
alone —manage each ; the whole of the profits of each office go to the policy-holders—and
the policy-holders only—and they are both, each in its own way, capable of doing
abiding service to many millions of Australians.

“Y.Z.”: The assumption that politicians
( would work if they did not talk so much is
now smashed by experience. • When a poli-
tician is holding his tongue he has his hands
full.
From a Westralian Gallery hand : —-
Representatives of the Country party and the
Liberal League recently held a pow-wow behind
closed doors, with the object of giving the Scaddan
Government the knock-out when Parliament meets
on July 13, or thereabout. It was agreed with
absolute unanimity that the Labor Government was
a menace to this bled and bleeding country. But
there the conference stuck hard. What the Hayseed
party really wanted was to attach the Liberal crowd
firmly to its tail. The Hayseeds’ original idea of a
working agreement with the Libs, was that it should
be given immunity from Liberal opposition in 21
electorates out of the total of 50, and that the party
which won the greater number should form a Min-
istry and receive the support of the other section.
A simple calculation caused the Libs, to heave an
iceberg at the proposition. It meant that the
Liberals were to surrender all chance of Ministerial
office for the benefit of their country cousins, who
now hold—with considerable uncertainty—eight seats.
The Liberals’ counter-proposition was that Liberals
and Country party nominees should be free to con-
test any electorate, but that each party should
pledge itself to give its second preference to the
other fellow. As the exercise of the preference vote
is compulsory, this suggestion was sound enough,
but the “farmers’ friends” turned it down hard.
So the only definite result of the conference was a
pious agreement that the party in possession should
be fired out at the earliest opportunity, and this
practically assures that Scaddan will have to hawk
its fearful financial record before the electors within
two or three months.
“City Temple.—The foundation stone will
not be laid this afternoon.” This curt advt.
in the Adelaide dailies of last Saturday has
set that city talking more than ever about
the affairs of Paul Joseph, the young Con-
gregational parson with a Tasmanian legacy
at liis back. He accepted the pastorate of the
old-established Hindmarsh-square Church six
months ago, on condition that it would move
with him into a new “Temple” (with soda-
fountain and other American aids to salva-
tion) to be built on North Terrace. After-
wards he paid £6OOO for the late Chief Jus-
tice Way’s old home, to be used somehow in
connection with the work. Then the
“Temple” began to arise, but just as it got
to the conventional foundation-stone height
work ceased. Also these things happened:
(1) Mr. Joseph resigned his Hindmarsh-
square job; (2) an inquisitive reporter got
a statement from him that he means to work
on broader unsectarian lines, and that the
Temple foundation would be duly laid on the
coming Saturday by his father-in-law, the
veteran Dr. Bevan; (3) it wasn’t. Hence
the guessing.
Just-dead H. E. A. Wells, of Mudgee, was
son of that surveyor Wells who in the ’4o’s
published a very useful directory of N. S.
Wales —now a valuable work. Amongst the
advertisers, by the way, was “Henry Parkes,
ivory and bone turner,” who then kept a
little shop in Hunter-street, Sydney.
Young Robert Gillespie, among those now
admitted killed at Gallipoli last year, was
one of Melbourne Uni.’s rare bachelors of
agricultural science. He was the eldest son
of well-known financial expert James Gil-
lespie, and a grandson of that Robert
of the ilk who for many years was a broad
pillar of the Presbyterian Church in Aus-
tralia.
The daylight-saving idea is being illus-
trated at the head of the rail west from Port
Augusta. The line is now pushed out over
360 miles, and as Adelaide time is used
the clock easilv beats the sun, and the differ-
ence in the length of the evening is very
noticeable. An interesting timekeeper’s argu-
ment will start when the gap of 290 miles is
bridged and East and West shake hands.
On the Kalgoorlie side the railway men use
Perth time, while Adelaide bosses the
watches from Port Augusta.

“P.X.”: According to the Glasgow
correspondent of the Daily News, Prime
Minister Hughes, talking to a big Town
Hall crowd there, “told what Australia had
done in Imperial interests, as well as in
her own, to help on the cause in the matter
of wheat. The Government offered a mini-
mum price for all grain delivered by
farmers at the railway sidings of the great
Southern Continent. The result of that
inducement to put virgin land under wheat
was that the production had been multi-
plied by two and a half times what it was
before.” This gives a very fair idea of the
sort of guff Hughes has been feeding ’em
on in England; also it gives a very fair
indication of the sort of person Hughes is.
The fact, of course, is that one State, N. S.
Wales, guaranteed a minimum for the wheat
harvested from excess ground—very little of
it virgin ground, by the way. And to prove
that the huge' crop was not the result of
that offer it is merely necessary to say that,
according to the Commonwealth Govern-
ments own statist, the increase in yield of
Victoria, where no such offer was in exist-
ence, was from 32 millions in 1913 to 58
millions in 1915, and of S. Australia from
16 millions to 34 millions. As a matter of
fact, so far from the offer in N. S. Wales
inducing the State to put virgin land under
wheat, Knibbs shows that the area under
wheat in N. S. Wales in 1914 was 3,205,397
acres, whereas in 1915, when the offer was
in force, it was only 2,758,024 acres.

ITHOUT imagination the mind of any man or woman
becomes stagnant and uninteresting. Instead of harbouring
sweet fields overrun with the flowers of fancy, there is
but an arid desert of undeveloped thought. This is not right. The
imaginative faculty is necessary to the highest development of intellect
and should be stimulated in every possible way. Good books, good
pictures and, above all, good music should be part and parcel
of one’s every day life.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints,
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the well-known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any-
thing inferior to wash the hair with. (let
some good stallax from your chemist and use
the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Hub _it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
11
Photo: Wrather & Bvys.
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it will look bright and
glossy.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and autumn. Then the hair
needs a little—what do you call
it? —stimulant, and for this I
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum ; dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick, (five your
hair a good brushing every night, and tha
will be all that you need do.

Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coining into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool. This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
Grey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it with
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair with a small sponge for a few nights
and the greyness will gradually disappear.
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has been
used for generations with most satisfactory
results by those who have known the
formula.
To Kill Roots of Superfluous Hair.
The most Effective Formula ever Discovered,
Women annoyed with disfiguring growths
of superfluous hair wish to know not merely
how to temporarily remove the hair, but how
to kill the hair roots permanently. For
this purpose pure powdered pheminol may be
applied directly to the objectionable hair
growth. The recommended treatment is
designed not only to instantly remove the
hair but also to actually kill the roots so that
the growth will not return. About an ounce
of pheminol, obtainable from the chemist,
should be sufficient.
Good News for Fat People.
Something New in Obesity Cures.
A London chemist says: “The latest
method of reducing obesity certainly is far
more pleasant and convenient than all pre-
vious methods. It consists merely in eating
clynol berries. The fat person who wants
to reduce without the usual rigid diet, exer-
cise, sweating baths, etc., now puts a few
of these little brown berries in his or her
pocket and eats three or four each day.”
Clynol berries not only eliminate fat from
the body, but also correct the tendency,
which is usually constitutional, to create
fatty matter. No discomfort whatever is
caused by their action; in fact, except for
the loss of superfluous fat and the feeling
of “fitness” so created, you would not be
aware that these little berries were doing
their work.
Local inquiry shows that clynol berries are
not very well known in this country, but
the demand is increasing daily, and any
chemist can quickly procure them if specially
requested to do so.

When Massey and Ward tear themselves
away from Maoriland shortly and link up
with High Commissioner Tom Mackenzie,
there will be then in London three men who
have had the boss political job in Maoriland.
One of them is a Bart., the other is deco-
rated “Sir.” and Massey is just plain Bill,
with an alarming habit of explosive mirth
at his own jokes. In the days when the
Mackenzie was Minister for Agriculture
plain Bill’s most bitter criticism of him was
that he didn’t know a ram from a gentle-
man Ayrshire. Whereupon he would break
into the loud laugh of derision and Thomas
would volubly hurl back the allegation.
Massey would never consent to follow
Hughes in the purchase of 15 steamers, but
if he sees a nice line of heifers going cheap
he is almost sure to bring them back for the
further cementing of the Empire.
“The Gink”: Somebody should tell the
Commonwealth Public Service that the
recruiting posters are still on the walls.
Two good fellows of my acquaintance
applied at the beginning of April for permis-
sion to enlist. They were duly advised
by their department that when relief was
available they would be allowed to go. It
is now the end of June, and they are still
being held down in their chairs while ex-
cited M’s.P. are abroad semaphoring for
Empire-savers. In another case a young
officer in a hurry to do his duty by his
country did not wait for the official sanc-
tion, but got into camp. The prompt patriot
was informed that there would be no job
waiting for him when he got back. Another
zealous young man asked permission to go
to the Front with the Y.M.C.A. or to enlist
in the ordinary way. lie was told that as
his was the first case of its kind to come
under notice it must be sent to something
called “the Central Administration” for a
ruling—a three months’ job at least. If
some of this foolish obstruction could be
lifted off the national rails, the country
might be saved the cost of the recruiting
trains.
Ivan is coming, yes coming from afar,
Through the snow and marsh.
Sec his bayonet glistening, on he goes to war,
And although his song is harsh,
lie does not spear babies like Willie the Hub,
Look out, Willie 1 lie’s coming, run!
When he gets to Berlin, then he’ll begin,
And he’ll put the Huns in the dust all right.
Now I seem to hear him coming through the night,
And I wake with a fitful start.
With a wild and beating heart.
But he’s coming, so I know it’s all right.

Diamonds Reduced Enormously.
Four years ago we took on a
Sole Agency calling for an enor-
mous output of Diamonds, and,
with a view to making the turn-
over, we offered them at merely

(Lecture No i.)
Anaemia
Our blood is composed of
red and white corpuscles—
the red to nourish the body,
the white to fight disease.
In Anaemia—or bloodless-
ness —the red corpuscles are
more or less deficient. Thus
the blood cannot provide
sufficient nourishment for
the body. Therefore the
face becomes white and
“pasty”—the eyes become
dull and “ heavy ” —and a
feeling of intense weariness
pervades the whole system.
To overcome Anaemia, the
blood supply needs re-
charging with red corpuscles.
And it is here that
h
possesses such wonderful
power. Because, being a
blood-maker, ‘ Wincarnis ’
creates a wealth of new, rich,
red blood, which brings the
roses back to the cheeks —
gives a sparkle to the eyes
—and surcharges the whole
body with new vitality and
new life.
Begin to get well
TO-DAY.

culty in obtaining it, write direct

Cholmondley Fitzmaggbt” from Egypt :
Here in the Combined Training and General Base
Depot, the A.I.F. canteen offers a good assortment
of tinned fruit for sale, and one of the most
popular things with the men is pineapple. But it
is all from the Singapore packing houses, and cannot
compaie with the canned product of Queensland;
both in quality and get-up it is inferior. All the
other tinned fruits are good Australian. Why this
discrimination against one fine fruit and a ' home
industry that needs encouragement?
“Quidam : Re the Macartneys (B.
29/G/’l6). Baronet Sir John died in Rock-
hampton some time ago, and his wife pre-
deceased him by several years. The old
man, who was over 70 when he left, was
one of the pioneers of Queensland, and a
well-known identity of the Port Mac-kay dis-
trict in the early days. He then owned
I orest Hills station, but later on, like most
of his squatting contemporaries, fell on evil
times, and when he passed out practically
owned nothing but his title, a possession
which carried no pecuniary benefit. As he
left behind him seven sons, Queensland is
likely to number at least one hereditary
title among its possessions for some time.
To poor old Sir John, who saw death com-
ing in a particularly painful and unpleasant
fashion for years, the title was only a
reminder that such things without health
and wealth are dreadfully hollow bubbles.
Jos. Campbell, of Cairns, has been busy
showing that in yet another line Australia
can be independent of the foreigner.
Attacking blady grass ( lulling) with vigor
he has found that it yields 60 per cent,
weight of commercial paper-pulp. The cost
delivered to boiling works would be 20s. a
ton; and crude paper-pulp could be turned
out at about £5 Bs. a ton. Cane tops, says
the inventor, can be similarly utilised. So
far there has been no frantic haste on the
part of the local newspapers to investigate.
If the discovery had been made by a small
imported person with a large accent, he
would have had a better chance of being
hailed as a deliverer.
“T. 0.1.”: F. T. Brentnall, of the Brisbane
Telegraph, has a unique record. When
Methodist minister at Wagga he performed
the marriage ceremony between the Ticli-
borne claimant, Tom Castro, alias Orton,
and the lady whose affection he secured.
Mr. Brentnall was one of five Methodist
ministers who arrived in Sydney in 1864,
but after some years’ service a throat affec-
tion drove him to ink.
An old mate’s send-off:—
Died at Sydney on Saturday E. B. Torpy, who left
a mark on Queensland mining and drove the lowest
level at 43. An Adelaide boy, he was a cub re-
porter on the Broken Hill Argus in ’BB, then went
to Jo’burg, and while reporter on the Star distin-
guished himself by punching Barny Barnato —a mean
millionaire who borrowed a liver from the boy-
reporter and refused repayment. Torpy was at Cool-
gardie, having the common Australian disease of fol-
lowing mining camps, and in ’9B he went to Chilla-
goe. There he built three hotels for the copper
boom guaranteed by Alex. Stewart; but when the
railway and smelters were completed they found the
ore had been forgotten. One of Torpy’s hotels took
two and sixpence the first day and another’s receipts
were ninepence. Drinks cost a shilling, hue 9d. was
all the solitary customer had. Always tremendously
energetic and very active as are so many heavy men,
i orpy opened the Chillagoe-Ruddigore, Crooked

Creek, and a lot of shows in the squibby field of
Gurramba. He was a teetotaller; knew a horse and
backed him; was a close, cool and intrepid poker
player. He died as courageously as he lived, taking
the long agony of 18 months’ wasting to the weak-
ness of water from Bright’s disease as cheerfully as
he took his best luck. A good sport, a good miner,
and a good Australian ; and Australia is the poorer
for losing his kindly, courageous personality.
“Cleveland”: My own experience is that
the cochineal insect has no wages board,
recognises no hours, saints’ days or holi-
days, and will clear a patch of the spiny
pear in about three months. All that is
necessary is to place an infected leaf among
the stuff and be handy with a match about
12 weeks later to set light to the debris.
The insect attacks leaves, stems and exposed
roots, and doesn’t let go as long as there
is a drain of moisture to be extracted. It
increases and spreads with remarkable
rapidity, and then dies when there is no
more pear tucker available. Incidentally,
a Commission appointed by the Government
toured comfortably round the world in quest
of information about this insect; yet the
public only hears of the doings of an im-
ported expert out Dulacca way, and not a
whisper of the report of the expensive Com-
mission.
A hideous story was told in Brisbane
Police Court the other day, after a man
had been hauled all the way from a Surat
station on two charges of unnatural assault
committed on boys of 13. According to the
boys’ evidence, they visited the Brisbane
Baths several times in the middle of March
to practise for a swimming carnival. The
accused was employed as an assistant, and
was alleged to have committed the assaults
during these visits. But the crowning horror
was to come. About a fortnight later a
medical examination revealed that both the
boys were suffering from syphilis; and the
State medical officer (Dr. A. S. Roe) gave
evidence that the accused was suffering
from the disease. The accused, who reserved
his defence, has been committed for trial.
A further horror of the case was that all
the witnesses kissed the same dirty old
Book, although it was stated in evidence
that the two principal witnesses were still
under treatment for syphilis.
Customs Minister Tudor explained at Bris-
bane the other day that “high freights would
rule for a long time to come and afford Aus-
tralia an incalculable measure of Protection.’’
Well, freights have been sky-high these last
18 months, and imports have poured into the
country like 15 floods. The result of the
high freights has been to (1) force up the
price of imported goods to the Australian
buyer; (21 to increase the cost of many
things required in Australian industry; (3)
to make the importing disease a more harrow-
ing business than ever, because in addition
to having to pay the foreign manufacturer
the foreign shipowner gets heavy ransom.
The would-be Australian manufacturer wants
above all things an assured Protection; and
it isn’t likely that he will lay out money on
the horribly uncertain Protection that tem-
porary high freights give him.
Gather Ye Memories.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gather ye memories!
The way that slant light caught a free bird’s
wing;
The velvet rosiness of hoya hearts;
The seagull as from blue to blue it darts;
Bird throats that pulse with rapture when
they sing;
Women’s soft lips and the close cling of
arms ;
Sunlight in yellow hair, and brown eyes bent
Over our own in an achieved content;
Rich sunset skies behind black, ragged
palms;
I 1 lame-colored pools on beaches wild and wet
That mirror Day’s red passion ere lie dies
Along the arched intensity, in skies,
Paling to tardy.greyness of regret!
Gather ye memories!
Whispered and radiant words in midnight
hours;
Gleam of white statues in a gallery ;
An open window with a sweep of sea,
And brown bees in the golden cups of
flowers;
Pink Natal grasses blowing on a rise ;
Slim pine trees in a silent, inland place;
In some musk grove cool lingers on your
face,
And the eternal mystery of eyes;
A line of verse that aches with sombre fire,
A phrase that rings with tramp of marching
feet;
The scent of grasses, trodden, warm and
sweet,
And silver moonlit nights of soul-desire!
Gather ye memories!
v,) u will not find their joy too great a load,
These threads of scarlet on a web of gold.
Fold them about your heart. When you are
old
Their color will light up the dull grey road.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, July 1,191 G.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
As usual, armed collectors were out duriug
the week. Friday was Nurse Caveil’s Day
in aid of the War Nurses’ Fund, and over
£5OO was raised.
On Wednesday night, at the Albert Hall,
his Ex., on behalf of the subscribers, handed
over £2OO. a flue invalid chair and a walking-
stick to Lieut. Maurice Little, an Anzac who
gave his sight and his right hand for his
country. Mrs. Little, who met and married
the Lieut, after he had been wounded, was a
medical missionary in the Soudan, and
offered her services to a military hospital
when the war started.
The Toowone Oirls’ Patriotic Club, which
has done splendid work, held an entertain-
ment in the South Brisbane Technical College
on Thursday night, to raise further funds.
The Gov. and his lady sat in the front pews,
and Brig.-General and Mrs. Lee were among
the congregation.
Last Tuesday night the Inky Wayfarers of
Brisbane presented a gold A.J.A. badge to
Jim Webster, who has been at different times
president, treasurer and secretary of the
Brisbane branch. The presentation over, the
press gang, after casting a few shy glances
at each other, got rid of a song or two and
a few recitations. Norman Campbell, actor,
Bulletin bard and story-sketeher, who has
left the Daily Mail and is about to open
ns the terrible villain with George Marlow
at the Theatre Royal, contributed to the
gaiety. By the way, “Norbell” climbs into
khaki next month. His wife, Nella Camp-
bell, herself an experienced footlight lady,
recently appeared at the Royal and played
the part of an ancient and villainous Cock-
ney beldame in “The Ever Open Door.”
The Wistaria Fete, organised by Mrs. R.
O. Hatch and Miss Elfreda Walsh in aid
of the Soldiers’ Comforts Fund, was held
to-day. A concert, stage managed by Leon
Berliner, for the same good cause, happened
this evening. Next Friday the Fund will
get another lift, when Dan Carroll lends
West’s Olympia for the screening of “Britain
Prepared.”
The Wattle Day League holds its annual
corroboree next Friday, and has decided to
devote all funds to the purchase of artificial
limbs for maimed soldiers. Seeing that the
Federal Government has arranged to give
limbs to returned men who have mislaid
their own, some further explanation of the
League’s intentions should be made.
Vida Lahey, one of Brisbane’s cleverest
artists, packed up her brushes and left for
England by the Mongolia. Mildred Lovett,
well known in Sydney art, has taken over
her studio and classes.
The engagement is announced of. Lottie
Bond, daughter of Tom Bond. D. 5.0., to
Lieut. Miles Thompson, of the A.I.F.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.

“Haka”: The old lady wlio produced a pro-
cession of verses as proof of her age (B.
1/6/T6) has a rival. In a court in M.L. the
other day this other lady, when accused of
not putting enough water in it, weepingly
produced a photograph of her five sons at the
Front, and was at once excused by a patriotic
beak.
A Maoriland Billjim who had been in the
field with a gun for over a year and had re-
ceived only two letters from his best girl
was beginning to wonder whether she had
forgotten him, when a sweating mailman
rode into a desert camp in Egypt and handed
the doubting swaddie a bundle of mail con-
taining 111 letters *nut packets, all from the
one faithful heart.
“Trot”: One Australian mother was in the
wrong place the other day and another leaped
clear just in time, and on both occasions a
baby got more than it deserved. Incident
No. 1: A girl making linoleum polish found
the material in flames, tried to throw the
burning mass outside, and deposited it on
the baby instead. The mother rushed to the
rescue, and was fatally burned. No. 2: An
exasperated father, heaving the teapot at
mother, missed her but got home on the
infant. The moral seems to be that fire and
teapots should be left alone. Those young-
sters had better luck than a pair encoun-
tered up in Bananaland a while ago. One of
these nippers managed to get hold of a bottle
of arsenic, and was struggling to remove
the cork when the other happened along, col-
lared the goods and bolted to a quiet place.
He never bolted any more. And the one who
had been left howling wandered off next day
and was found drowned in a waterhole. But
none of these youngsters had the luck which
came my way in the long ago. Dad pro-
mised to give me a hiding when he came
home from work, but before he got back I
had the measles. So he had to give me some
toys instead, and I gave him the measles
just to show there was no ill-feeling.
More about Kimble Bent:—
Kimble Pent, the pakcha-Maori, passed out into
the final adventure at Wairau Hospital, in Marl-
borough (M.L.). He began life as a ’Murrican
citizen with a half-breed Indian mother, in the
State of Maine, served in the Yankee navy, put in
six uneasy years in the British Army, and was triced
up to the triangles in Taranaki and bit on the
bullet to the tune of 25 lashes for insubordination.
When he got over the flogging he made a bee-line
for the bush, lived with the Maoris for pretty well
50 years, and to his last day was shy of the inquisi-
tive pakeha and the police. He was the Hauhaus’
boss armorer and cartridge-maker through the Tara-
naki campaigns, helped build their stockades, joined
in their war-dances with nothing but a loin-mat
on his well-tanned hide, had two or three tattooed
wives, and made about as thoroughgoing a pagan
as any of his brown bush-mates. The only thing he
drew the line at, he told me, was “kai-tangata”—-
cannibalism. He was an eye-witness of two cannibal
feasts in the Taranaki bush in 1868; on each occa-
sion a white soldier’s body was eaten by the Hauhaus.
The old renegade’s technical description of the
cannibal cooks’ modus operandi made my hair curl.
And at the Moturoa fight in ’6B he saw the war-priest
of the bushmen cut out a soldier’s heart and offer
it in burnt sacrifice to Tu, the Maori Mars.
There seems to be a germ of art in the
name of Gill. The Adelaide Gallery Direc-
tor who died the other day was no connec-
tion of the Melbourne Gill who had charge
of the exhibition of Will Dyson’s drawings
a while ago. Nor is there any connection
between either of those Gills and the other
Gill of Melbourne, whose sons —Naylor and
Luther —are artist and art expert re-
spectively. They must all have been forced
into the fine-arts line by their patronymic.
Under the perverse spell of a more dreamily-
poetic name they might have drifted into
the bacon trade.
The first consumptive to land in Australia
and leave his bones here was Forby Suther-
land, who came with Captain Cook, died at
Botany Bay, and was buried at what is now
known as Kurnell. Cook named the place
Point Sutherland. A clump of shrubs is
supposed to be the spot where the grave was
made.
This was the first laugh the Federal
Convention had after it got to work in Adel-
aide. Henry Dobson, of Tasmania, was
talking about the franchise. “And how
many votes do they give a man in Tas-
mania?” asked a ribald cynic. “As many as
he deserves,” said Dobson, unabashed. Ap-
parently they still do. For every 100 per-
sons over 21 in the State there are 1011
electors on the roll. No doubt there are
some whose names stay in the lists after
they have moved to Hongkong, or further.
But there are others who are on the roll in
two places at once—or, occasionally, in
three.
The boot-trade lately showed how easily
it can be done. The employees demanded a
10 per cent, rise, on account of the same
old high cost of living. A conference of the
trade met to consider the matter. That
conference didn’t consider whether the rise
was a fair thing or not; it simply asked
whether the advance could be passed on.
When it found that this could be done it
promptly agreed to the 10 per cent, advance
in wages—-and then went home and marked
up the price of boots. Thus up went the
cost of living some more. In a country
which hadn’t borrowed anything from any
foreigner and hadn’t therefore to pay any
interest, and which, moreover, produced all
it consumed, it wouldn’t matter if wages
were pushed up to £5 a day. But for a
country which has borrowed and which does
import and which has to pay interest and
buy its imports with its exports, there is
trouble brewing if costs of production are
excessive. For this reason: no matter what
its costs are, it has to sell its exports at a
price fixed by what other countries can pro-
duce them for. The more we borrow from
abroad and the more we import, the more
we put ourselves in competition with the
low-wage foreigner; and a tariff as high as
Heaven can’t protect us from the conse-
quences, because we must take our goods into
the foreigner’s market to sell them.

Love came knocking at my door ;

"Hush, heart,” I said, “the door is locked”....
I wonder who it was that knocked?

“That wonderful Little Camera” —
The Vest Pocket Kodak.
This letter came to our attention a short time ago :
“The Vest Pocket Kodak, that wonderful little camera,
bought as a ‘plaything’ for my son, I find myself using
it to the exclusion of my plate camera, inasmuch as the
majority of the negatives stand unbelievable enlarge-
ment.”
All that is necessary to convince one of the practicability of the
Vest Pocket Autographic Kodak is a personal acquaintance with
it. It’s the very essence of efficiency. So small and smooth that it
will readily slip into the vest pocket, so dainty that it is perfectly
at home in a lady’s handbag—the Vest Pocket Kodak may always
be carried where a largef camera would sometimes be an incon-
venience. This is one reason why the gentleman who sent us the
above letter found himself using it to the exclusion of his heavy
plate camera. Many people carry it all the time exactly as they
carry a watch. If you have room for a watch, you have room for
a Vest Pocket Kodak. You don’t know it’s there till you want it
and think of the picture possibilities you have missed because you
didn’t have a camera with you.
The Vest Pocket Autographic Kodak should not form the entire
photographic equipment. This is asking too much of it. There
are plenty of photographic situations that the larger Kodaks can
cope with more satisfactorily than can the Vest Pocket Kodak.
On the other hand, there are many occasions that a larger camera
is out of the question, and it is at such times that the Vest Pocket
Kodak fulfils its destiny. It should be a part of every photo-
graphic equipment—and it is proving itself to be a very important
part.
The Vest Pocket Kodak gives rectangular pictures, If x 2i inches,
but, to quote our correspondent, “the majority of the negatives
stand unbelievable enlargement.” The pictures in their own size
are very satisfactory, but to enlarge them to postcard size is a
perfectly simple operation. The Vest Pocket Enlarging Camera
lias been constructed for this one size enlargement, and performs
the operation most simply. It’s almost as easy as making the
contact print. Of course Vest Pocket negatives are not limited to
postcard size enlargement, and successful results have been
obtained with S x 10 enlargements, and even much larger. Post-
card size, however, has always been the most popular size for
amateurs, and Vest Pocket negatives and the Vest Pocket Kodak
Enlarging Camera prove a very successful combination.
All good Vest Pocket negatives will stand enlargement, but the
microscopic definition so desirable in this kind of work is best
secured with the Vest Pocket Kodak, fitted with the Kodak Anas-
tigmat f. 7.7, a lens ground from the finest glass and submitted
to those same rigid tests that have helped to make Kodak another
name for reliability. Kodak Anastigmat f. 7.7 is free from astig-
matism, and focuses sharply to the edges of each negative. In
short, it is an anastigmat of the highest quality possible, and at
f. 7.7 will do just what any anastigmat will do, regardless of price.
The Vest Pocket Kodak is a fixed focus Kodak —is ready for
business just as soon as you are. It has two instantaneous speeds,
1/25 and 1/50 of a second as well as time and bulb action. It
has a brilliant reversible finder, and uses Kodak Film cartridges
of 8 exposures Price 1/-. Mechanically it is as right as a full-
jewelled watch —photographically, in its own sphere, it could
hardly be improved upon.
The only man who could possibly be dissatisfied with the \ r est
Pocket Kodak would be the man who bought it for a plaything
and really wanted a plaything. He would be disappointed.
Though, of course, it is wonderfully simple to operate.
FOR SOLDIERS. —The Vest Pocket Autographic Kodak is the
Soldier’s Kodak, and meets every requirement of the soldier at the
Front or in the training camp. It is just what he needs, and
therefore makes an ideal gift for him.

Public Notice.
PATENTS. —A useful feature embodied in
Patent Law enables an inventor at comparatively
small cost (with simple inventions £4 45.), even
before he has been to the expense of working out his
invention in practice, to procure “Provisional Protec-
tion,” and thus save the priority of a term to receive
full Patent rights in the Commonwealth of Australia,
and in any or all of those 26 countries having
Patent Laws, and which are parties with the
Commonwealth to the International Convention.
During this term, which is nine months in the
Commonwealth of Australia and 12 months in the
foreign countries, the inventor has ample opportunity
to test the full question of value, to work out his
invention and demonstrate its practicability. If the
principles of his invention are. seen to promise a
success he can proceed to take out full Patents; if
not, he need go to no further expense. During the
period of provisional protection the invention may
be made, used and sold without prejudicing the right
of the inventor to obtain valid Letters Patent. Full
information on Patents furnished free on application
to A. J. CALLTNAN, Registered Patent Attorney (by.
Official Examination, 1906), 281 Collins Street,
I Melbourne—’Phone 5199; or 14 CasUereagh Street,
Sydney—’Phone 8336. Direct and most reliable agents
in all countries granting Patents. — Advt.

When yonr tongue is coated evenly, all,
over, with a whitish-yellow color and your
breath is bad the chances are that you are
bilious. If you also have indigestion, con-
stipation, headache and a general ill feeling,
there is scarcely a doubt about it. Your liver
needs attention.
Harsh cathartics which purge violently
and then produce a reaction only aggravate
the trouble. Gentle assistance is what
nature needs to establish regular habits of
health, and Pinkettes, the little pink laxa-
tive pills, free from harmful or habit-form-
ing drugs, meet the requirements most adnii-:
rably.

The Inky Way.
The rising price of paper and the boom in
freights and other oddments have given the
jumps to the proprietors of the two Hobart
dailies. From July 1 Mercury and Post
will cost lid. per copy instead of Id., and
subscribers will also he hit up, though not
quite as much as 50 per cent. The Labor
Daily Post, which is weaker than the old-
time Mercury, and has been reduced in
size, is likely to be pretty badly hurt in the
subscription list, and it’s a question whether
it will hold long to the agreement. The
Launceston papers are-trying to rub through
at the penny.
* * * *
“Chink”,: The latest Bulletin man to
swap the pen for the bayonet is Norman
Campbell, maker of much decent verse, and
general good fellow of the Inky Way and
Poverty Point. For the past six months or
so he has been using a bright pen on Bris-
bane Daily Mail. Military matters came
in his job, and so impressed was “Norbeli”
by the sight of Billjims rolling up day after
day that lie couldn't resist joining the pro-
cession.

* * . • •
Amongst other good men the Argus has
drawn to it lately is Norman Lilley, who
for some years has freelanced in Sydney.
He is one of the daring spirits who sought
to create the ideal Australian magazine, and
although he did not succeed the skeleton
of his effort will take long to moulder.
Other mounds of Lilley’s on the Inky Way
are the Literary pages which he conducted
for Sydney Mail and Worker. His hobbies'
are books and modesty, with an occasional
trip into verse.
* * * *
Charlie Barrett, of Melbourne Herald,
has joined the Army Medical Corps. His
principal campaigns hitherto have aimed at
inducing a cynical news-editor to give him
five columns of space on page 1 for an illus-
trated article about model homes for blue
wrens, or shorter working hours for magpie
shrikes. Other causes he had at heart were
those of the boy scout and the golfer, which
quaint animals he handled with a tolerant
pen. When he was not giving his attention
to “Inquests 1 col.” or “Recruiting Com-
mittee, par. only,” he was a disciple of
Agassiz, to whom Nature, the old nurse,
said : “Here is a story-book that thy Father
hath written for thee.” He founded Mel-
bourne Bird Observers’ Club, was co-editor
of The Emu, and is an ornithologist of
Australian repute—which is no doubt why
he _ goes after the German eagle. He has
written several books of adventure for boys,
and hundreds of nature-study articles. And
he bears after his name the mystic letters
C.M.Z.S., compared with which D.S.O. are
no mote than N.I.L.
* * * *
, ‘‘Quarter Single”: The most concise sui-
cide of the month was that of W. E. Barnes,
registrar of births and deaths at Wangaratta
(Vic.). He didn’t shoot himself till he had
filled in his own death certificate. It recalls
a rounds item chronicled by Billy Salter,
now of W.A., in his Argus days. A lady in
a ham-and-beef shop had, in the course of
an argument, nearly cut her husband’s nose
off with the chopper. On the way to the
hospital they made friends again, and the
husband, to shield bis wife, gave out that it
was a case of attempted suicide. Billy
brightly paragraphed the nose-cutting, and
headed his report “A Pioneer in Suicide.”
The wounded hero sued the Argus for libel.
Johnston, afterwards Judge, appeared for
the paper, but it wasn’t he that won the
case. The exact point at which victory
turned to the Argus was when Billy, re-
splendent in a frock coat and impeccable
etceteras, entered the box and placed a
faultless silk hat on the ledge on its fore-
castle. “What is your name?” he was
asked. Drawing himself together like a
Dreadnought about to be launched, Billy
fixed the foreman of the jury with his mild
and magnificent eye, and declaimed “William
—Ewart —'Gladstone —Salter.” The rest
was merely decent obsequies and a verdict
for th! defendant newspaper.

STANDARDISED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
To this complaint, O harp, awake!
In our fair country, all o’ergrown
With bards of every shape and make,
I’ve no emotions of my own ;
No man of us can have a love —
No matter be she stout or slim,
A keen-billed hawk or meek-eyed dove—
That is original to him.
There is no girl that I can please,
But'all her points some minstrel bellows,'
And makes it very plain that she’s
Exactly like some other fellow’s;
Her little looks, her- turns of phrase,
Her pretty pout, her blush of shame,
Have all inspired a writer’s praise,
Embodied in some other dame.
I never had a subtle kiss,
And told myself in ecstasy:
“No other man was kissed like this—■
It is God-given, just for me!”
I never had endearment sweet
To cherish in my heart’s recess
But some rude scribe, with rhythm neat
Detailed my joy, my sweet caress.
The fond familiarities,
The words that I in whispers dole—
That no one hears, that no one sees,
All copyrighted in the soul—
Unshamed in public and for gold
A poet blurts. We are devised
All in the same unchanging mold,
And all our parts are standardised!
VICTORIA.

nurtured and grown, and it alone should
receive the attention if results are to be
expected. It would do no earthly good to
treat the stem of a plant with a vietv to mak-

soil in which the plant grows must be
attended to. Therefore, the scalp, in which
the hair grows, must receive the attention if
you are to expect it to grow and become more
beautiful. Loss of hair is caused by the scalp
drying up or losing its supply of moisture or
nutriment; when baldness occurs, the scalp
has simply lost all its nourishment, leaving
nothing for thejhair to feed upon. (A plant,
or even a tree, would die under similar condi-
tions.) The natural thing to do in either
case is to feed or replenish the soil or scalp,
as the case may be, and your crop will grow
and multiply as Nature intended it should.
