Where Sapling Tops Are Red.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I KNOW a street in a seaside town,
Where ceaseless feet go up and down,
And restless pulses beat;
Upon its pavement hopes are strewn —-
Was it last night or yesternoon
I walked that seaside street?
I met Lies clad in lace and gold,
Lies young and sweet, and sour and old,
Lies fleet of foot and slow; ,
There walked the robber, plunderer, thief,
There —with mouth red as a red rose-leaf —
The wanton, eyes aglow.
There minced my lady Powder Puff,
Tricked out in gold and silken stuff
And plume and gem and flower,
The-broken heart, the heart to break —•
Poor Human Nature out to make
The best of its brief hour.
And then said I: “Full sick am I
Of lying lips, and hearts that lie,
And Self in deed and word!”
Then all at once amid the rout
A Way was made —gates opened out —•
This summoned, and I heard!
Was it last night or yesternoon
I left that town, where hopes lie strewn
As thick as leaves wind-shed?
I know not, care not —an ye please.
Why should I care couched here at ease
Where sapling tops are red?
llow other to the roar, the rush—
These silent ways, this golden hush,
These tall trees green and grey !
Now, where’s my lady Powder Puff?
And where her scents and silken stuff,
And what’s her value —say?
I think here was green gaiety, •
And gossiping ’twixt tree and tree,
Ere I disturbed the fioon;
Full suddenly the birds grew dumb,
A startled stillness seemed to come—•
A sleep, a spell, a swoon !
For musing in the golden air,
As I lay thus, I grew aware
Of worlds beyond my ken ;
Of something in the conscious wood —•
A soul, a sense, a mind, a mood
Not understood of men.
The old wood-folk are dead and gone-
The nymph, the satyr and the faun,
The sylvan company;
And yet around me dwells the grace
That moulded shining form and face
And peopled poetry.
If underneath these arching skies
A sudden burst of mirth should rise
And echo wide around,
I would not count it out of touch
With things about, nor overmuch
Make marvel at the sound.
Nor would I wonder if there sped
Down yonder ways of green and red
A sudden passion-gust—•
Nymphs flying, lips apart and mute;
Goat-footed satyrs in pursuit
With reaching hands out-thrust.
I go not to the seaside town
Y here ceaseless feet move up and down,
And many hopes lie dead ;
For better far are gracious trees,
A musing hour, a couch of ease,
Where sapling-tops are red.
N.S.W.
RODERIC QUINN.
The Luck of Helsingfors.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BKUNO HELSINGFORS, the big,
blue-eyed, yellow-haired, Viking-
moustached Norwegian, first mate of
the Star of Papua, slept naked in the steam-
ing air of the tropics, which had given wings
to the copra bugs. Youth was in every
rippling muscle of him ; strength and clean-
liness in the lvhiteness of his skin. On his
great arms and wide breasts the rough
artists of the forecastle in his early days of
the sep. had made tattooings in the blue of
gunpowder and the dull red of lead. So
decorated he lay there breathing easily in
the heated cabin; sleeping and smiling in
his sleep—a splendidly handsome thing;
one to thank God for.
The fan, running on its third speed, made
waves in the thicker air. As the ship, rolled
to starboard in the stale swell, admitted the
red sun to stare for a moment through the
port at the closed eyes of the sleeper, he
turned over on his side with his face to the
cabin wall. A thin, dark hand, almost like
a bird’s claw in its absence of flesh, and
covered with black hairs clammy with sweat,
pulled back the cabin door-curtain on its
rings; the mess-room steward entered, and
laid down a cup of coffee and a plate of
biscuits on the cedar top of the low bureau.
The steward—a little, thin, worried Londoner,
yellow of skin and pouched of neck —rattled
the curtain rings on their brass bar, and
Bruno Helsingfors awoke.
The Carolines lay astern; away, some-
where in the invisibility ahead, lay the
Straits of San Bernardino, and the six hun-
dred islands of the Philippines. San Ber-
nardino is seen mostly as an open lane under
a clear sky sown with islands —islands
everywhere, as if Creation had spilled a
quart of them in mere spendthrift careless-
ness.
A hundred and fifty miles from the Straits
they ran into a deep haze, settling on the
sea like curtains, and capriciously torn and
closed again by wind that came in puffs and
varied in direction; yet it gave them no
concern, with a good ship and a mile or so
of water under it. But, naturally, the
people in the smoking-room, glad of the
haze because it provided conversation, talked
of wrecks and sea disasters —to their own
enjoyment and the discomfort of the thin,
dark messroom steward on duty as drink-
waiter that watch.
They talked of wrecks and founderings; of
cranky ships and changing lists; of sea-
cocks opened, and disasters in a calm ocean;
of coffin ships, and heavy insurances that
give the seaman no right to protection ot
life outside the three-mile limit; of the Elbe,
and the collier, steering wild, that sank her
in collision —two ships on a wide sea
attracted to each other as if magnetised;
of the overloaded Taramung sunk by follow-
ing seas; of the Koombana and Yongala
overwhelmed by cyclones ; of typhoons in the
China Sea; blundering junks by the great
Ladrones; colliers hitting Bramble Cay and
strewing their coal like apples out of an
overturned basket; the Catterthun and Seal
Rocks, the Wairarapa and the Great
Barrier; the sunken galleon of Throne
Shoal; the Elingamite at the Islands of the
Three Kings.
Chief-officer Bruno Helsingfors smoked his
fat cheroots in silence until he heard a wild
enthusiast talking of the sea’s romance; and
then he spat out half an inch of cigar leaf
and talked.
“Yah! I vill tell you der romances! Nor-
vegians buy der rotten ships of all der vorldt.
You hear of miserable ship foundering in
calm —it is offenly der Norvegians. I haf
ship on a barque at Cardiff boundt by
chains fore and afdt so she shall nodt fall
do bieces vile ve loadt der coals. In der
Nort Sea she go down —ve in der boats are;
and ve vatch der ship sink slow, so slow,
like ve vas in der teatre vatching a bainted
ship go down. Then ve are alone in der
Nort Sea —fourteen men in von boat. Ve
haf von saldt ham and von bugget vater for
tree days before ve are pick-ed up....1t is
so terrible time that ever since I carry vis
me this.’’
He took from his breast-pocket a small tin
case and held it up before them.
“What is that. Chief?”
“It is a capsule of cyanite of potass, so
that if ever I am wreck-ed some more I
suffer not too much.”
As he returned the case to his pocket
eight bells struck, and Chief-officer Bruno
Helsingfors threw his cheroot away and
went out to duty. When he had left the
smoking-room the dark, thin steward
shivered. The sea and its dangers held him
for his necessities, and he could find no
philosophy to firing to him the careless cheer-
fulness of use.
The haze lifted with the dawn and came
down again to the sea late in the afternoon,
and the wind puffs seemed to return with
it; probably because the tearing of the mists
by the variable winds made the air
movement visible. As the haze deepened
with the dusk, the dark, thin steward,
always in a cold sweat in anything approach-
ing a high temperature, moved about the
deck and smoking-room afraid as a man
under sentence of death, cursed with imagi-
nation and a memory indelible. Meantime
the cheery laugh of Chief-officer Bruno
Helsingfors was heard as he came off duty,
as if duty were a picnic.
As the Star of Papua approached San
Bernardino Strait that night the _ haze
deepened to the thickness of many veils of
grey chiffon. Only the occasional tearing of
the veil by a wind-puff was outside to tell
the ship the way; for the rest, she had the
chart and her observation of the red beacon;
and in such safety she threaded the twisting
channel as the fog settled down on her. still
intangible, but of the harsh tint of crape.
Bruno Helsingfors came off the bridge at
midnight; an hour later he awoke the
moment the engines stopped. The thin,
dark steward awoke trembling and switched
on the light to soften the blackness of the
fog which had sent its ghost to the interior
of the ship. The steward drew on his
clothes, sweating cold in this, sudden
inversion of the ship’s habit; the chief officer
tumbled into his pyjamas, intent only on
his clothing, and refusing to marvel as to
causes until he could find them out.
He found the deck spotting the fog with
light and men rushing fore and aft to hasty
orders.
“Journals running hot,” said the captain
as Helsingfors stood at the foot of the
bridge companion—“a damned careless lot
we’ve had to stop the engines. Get a
sea ancho/ out to check the drift, and we’ll
chance the engines whenever we’ve got to
get steerage way.”
Chief-officer Helsingfors hurried the coolie
crews in the making of a sea-anchor from
the deck-cargo of Australian hardwood
hound to Manila for harbor works. He put
half a dozen slings around the long raft, and
with a cargo winch lifted it, slung it over-
board, and down to the water. The sea
gripped it, and it floated aft to the limit of
its cable, showing the set of the current to
be astern.
“Afdt or astern dond’t matter,” said
Helsingfors to the Old Man. “Everyvere
there is rocks andt islandts, andt all der vorld
is made of fog.”
A gusty wind blew from dead ahead; the
captain spoke to the engine-room; the pro-
peller revolved for ten minutes, and stopped
again to a strong smell of burning oil and
heated metal. In that ten minutes Chief-
officer Helsingfors dressed himself fully, and
as he came to the bridge again he felt for the
cyanide-case in his breast pocket, and so
was reassured.
The passengers had been quieted with lies,
and sent to bed ; the ship drifted slowly for
half an hour, and then was brought up again,
to a position that might be safety in the
darkness, by a few minutes’ steam; but the
imaginative steward shivered in apprehen-
sion, unable to think, and holding down with
all his soul the almost overmastering desire
to scream. The tide ran strongly eastward,
seeming to suck them back through the
Straits, through which they were fain to win
to open sea, only that the way was sown with
rocks, and chance and indecision seemed the
only plan of safety. Then, an hour before
daylight came to lift the mist, and when cool
bearings seemed to be near, the Star of
Papua grounded, tore herself clear, struck
and listed heavily to starboard.
Pell-mell, passengers and crew flung them-
selves in darkness on the boats; the water
reached the fires, the smoke came out of her
funnels thick as wool, hut showing only as a
floceulent smear under the one star that
pierced the fog above the ship. Ere they
could get the boats clear the ship slipped off
the rock into deep water, taking with her
almost all her company in the suction.

aches, the limbs drag, the eyelids seem like lead ; there are
dropsical swellings, rheumatic pains, dizzy spells, urinary
troubles; the sufferer is moody, despondent and irritable.
This is how the fatal Bright’s disease begins.

Bruno Helsingfors, kicking his shoes off,
dived far out from the starboard side. Cries
and yells of terror came to him out of the
warm water. He did not see the ship’s last
plunge, but felt it in the displacement of
the water, and struck further out to star-
board of her. The screaming ceased ; the
big Norwegian swam a little, floated for 20
minutes, and swam again ; and then his feet
touched rock, and he struggled into shallower
water and found himself at daylight on a
flat rock as big as a dining-table, in four
teet ot water, and with a tide almost run
out.
The haze thinned, then lifted, and he saw
swimming feebly near him, and supported by
a partly-submerged stand for fire buckets, a
thin dark head, a livid yellow face, a
little black moustache drooping and wrung
out to a wisp by the sea. Helsingfors went
into the water to his neck, and drew to the
shallows on the rock the steward who had
been more afraid than any, and yet had won
through.
These two men stood mid-thigh in the
water, and looked around them as the haze
lilted for a circle of 500 yards or so. And
then Helsingfors shouted welcome to the
eight men—two white men and six Chinese
whom the chance of the current had
brought to this little ark of stone that was
four feet under water. The eight men swam
to the rock and the ten huddled together,
all differences of race and caste forgotten in
the tragedy of the ship.
“Der Old Man gone, andt all der passen-
chers,” said Helsingfors, “unless they vasgedt
to some udder islandt, and ve can’t flndt oudt
till der sun come.”
But the sun did not come. The dawn'
wind tore the haze, and showed them, a mile
or more away, heavy shapes which might be
islands or clouds, but nothing to show of
life, there or between. Then the drab
morning saw tlie wind rolling up the haze,
and the day opened in rain, flattening the
sea still more, and blotting out the islands
that might be clouds.
Dull with hunger and sodden with the sea,
they jostled each other on the rock for
hours that seemed like days. Helsingfors
maintained the place he had won at start-
ing, in the most central and highest posi-
tion on the rock. Next him the imagina-
tive steward shivered with apprehension at
every change in the water, and the warm
rain fell ecaselessly on them all, pitting the
ocean, and steaming in its passage through
the heated air. Helsingfors, feeling warmer
about the waist, looked down, and saw that,
the tide had risen to his belt.
A Chinaman on the outer edge of the rock
began to jabber excitedly in his own tongue;
his fellow coolies joined him, pointing ex-
citedly to the quarter where advanced the
tide. A dozen fins, like wet, black, flattened
horns, furrowed the sea; the fins swept
around the stony refuge and waited as if they
sentinelled it —sharks coming from greater
depths with the rising of the tide, and cor-
ralling the men. Helsingfors drew a long
breath and steeled himself; the steward
whimpered, and his legs went from under
him. Helsingfors put a great arm around
the steward, and held him up.
The sharks stalked them as if they en-
joyed the sport. A black fin sank; a ripple
came to the outer edge of the rock, a belly-
flash of white showed below, and with a yell
quickly smothered by the water a Chinaman
disappeared.
Then followed a brute struggle for life; a
struggle of worms to delay the inevitable
giant. Helsingfors, still supporting the
weakling, held his position in the centre. His
belt was hidden by the water now. The
sharks, feeling the extra depth, grew bolder,
and slaughtered the wrecked men one by
one, as if they were calves in a shambles.
Then even Helsingfors grew sick of the
rushing assassin shapes, of the reddened
water, and the shrieking men drawn under
one by one. He still held the fainting
steward ; but he closed his eyes. While so
self-blindfolded lie heard the terrible sounds
and anguish nearer him, and opened his eyes
again to find the steward and himself alone
upon the rock —and the tide apparently
stationary. The steward’s face was green
with horror; he seemed dying of fear.
The sharks held off for a little; the rain
ceased, and the sun split the moist air.
Around him Helsingfors saw the misty
shapes of real islands—the presence of firm
ground beckoning the shipwrecked, and the
sharks mocking the fading hope. The
steward, held in the crook of the big Nor-
wegian’s arm, moaned the bitterness of it
all; and the big heart of Helsingfors softened
for the other man’s need.
He drew from his pocket the little case of
cyanide,"'‘shook the steward into conscious-
ness, and, with a full knowledge of the self-
sacrifice, offered him the capsule.
“You poor liddle chap,” said Helsingfors.
“Take der cyanite. I am strong—l can
trown.”
But the other, awakened by the imminence
of death, put the cyanide from him with a
cry of fear, and drew away from Helsingfors
as if ho feared enforced death.
“No, no, Chief! I want to live! I can’t
die—-I can’t die !’’
“Poor little, foolish shap. You cannot
lif....And this is easy.... Come, there is
enough for von.”
But the steward, screaming, forced the
Norwegian’s hands away, crying, without
reason, as a baby might, that he wanted to-
live, and could not die.
Helsingfors, weary of it all. and with the
long horror of that drift in the North Sea
sharp in his memory, made a gesture of dis-
gust of this cowardice; looked at the sea,
empty but for the unapproachable islands
and the cruising sharks; tore off the lid of
the case in his strong white teeth, and swal-
lowed the capsule. lie crumpled at the
knees as if a sledge hammer had struck him
over the heart; bis head went, back into the
water; lie floated off the rock, and drifted
seawards. The sharks followed him. swim-
ming around his body, but not touching itr
puzzled, maybe, by the blue of his uniform
and the black socks that hid his feet.
And then, around the cape of the
nearest island that had lost .all its
disguising haze, came a little Filipino
steamer, the Binondo, bound for Pasig River,
and rescued the steward, to prove that the
theory of the fittest surviving is little more
than a poor sort of joke.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Woman with the Hoe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
p| HE was standing in a potato patch,
the woman with the hoe. Her face
had tanned unevenly, and graduated
from pale cinnamon to old roan, with
patches of nigger-liead. Her arms were
embossed with freckles.
“Damn this ’ere war,” she said. “Lemme
get a holt of Mister Andrew Hartigan,
M.P., I’ll teach him.” Then with infinite
disgust: “Him ’n’ his war!’
She swiped away the sweat ot her brow
with a forearm, and glanced reproachfully
at the sun.
"Four more hours iv it,” she said. When
we was all at ’ome it was ’ard enough, but
me ’n’ mum didn’t kill ourselfs. Then Bill
went ’n’ ’listed, ’n’ scooted off t’ their
bloomin’ war. That left me with all the
milkin’. Dan said the place was_ gittin’
slower ’n’ more lonesome, ’n’ next we knew
he was a soldier, ’n’ off t’ enjoy hisself
fightin’ too. That left me with the pigs on
me ’ands, ’n’ the garden t’ look to. Now
Tom’s gone; ’n’ if I don’t harvest these
spuds they’ll rot, ’n’ there’s been a bloke
here after dad, ’cause dad knows horses.
Well, let ’em have him, ’n’ it’ll be up t’ me t’
do the ploughin’ ’n’ ’arretin’, ’n’ get the seed
in atop iv everythin’ else. S’pose they think
three men’s work don’t make any difference
to a girl while she’s about it. But wait till
I meet Hartigan.”
The woman with the hoe turned up a few
tubers and sighed despairingly.
“That ain’t all I got agin Hartigan ’n’
his war,” she continued. “Steve Jonson
come over near every mornin’ t’ see me
milk; said he never seen the like iv it, ’n’
if things ’eld out he’d be marryin’ me. But
1 lartigan, M.P., come up ’ere, torkin’ about
his war. wantin’ ’ands for it, ’n’ off goes me
noble Steve, ’n’ gets hisself shot. Then
Charley Ryan comes hangin’ round, gettin’
interested in me milkin’, till one day he
says he’d like t’ be trackin’ with me, but for
the present lie’s goin’ t’ the war, he is. He
went, ’n’ I ain’t ’eard iv him since. Har-
tigan ’n’ his war!” she said with vindictive
derision. “Hartigan, M.P. ! Pie orter be
shot. No sooner gets inter Parliament than
lie’s gotter have a war.”
This was three weeks ago. Yesterday I
learned that Mr. Hartigan, M.P., the ener-
getic representative of Bunang (including
Flatrock, Crane’s Crossing and Squirrel
Hill) in the Legislative Assembly, had been
assaulted by a farmer’s daughter with a
hoe. Mr. Hartigan does not intend taking
action, I am told, as the girl was obviously
under a delusion. It was a funny thing, she
said, they had never had any wars at
Squirrel Ilill till Hartigan was sent to
Parliament.
EDDYSON.
Utopia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HERE'S a land where nothing matters
I Sleeping in a silver sea,
JL Where the breeze of morning scatters
Golden fruit from every tree.
Where the pumpkins young and willing
Climb inside the kitchen door,
And the waves are always.spilling
Choicest fish along the shore.
There the population browses
On the best the world can give,
And the men who own the houses
Give their tenants leave to live
In the greatest of contentment.
Without cash, or price, or fee;
None could tell us there what rent meant,
For the bricks and land are free.
Every dimpled damsel knows her
Trousseau can be got for- naught,
Never comes a wrathful grocer
, With a bill for things you bought,
Furniture is given gratis,
No one’s credit e’er can stop,
Everybody’s summer hat is
Simply taken from the shop.
No one worries o’er insurance.
No one speaks of cheap or dear,
Tom and Bill with great endurance
Front a world of priceless beer,
None for gold misspent need sorrow,
None for ready coin need pray,
Everyone can freely borrow
Cash he never need repay.
There methinks must be enjoyment
More than in this march of mind,
For the wealth of unemployment
Makes the world and nature kind.
There no sham and no pretension
Stirs spleen or wakes the gall;
No one ever needs to mention
Any blessed thing at all.
There the artist paints his pictures
As the warm cow chews the cud,
And the critic’s'fiercest strictures
Fall like gentle flakes of mud ;
There the poet keeps on stringing
Rhymes from dawn till evening dim,
And the people hear his singing
Without throwing things at him.
Young joy never goes a-missing,
Even naldheads have their day,
Reddest lips are there for kissing,
Brightest eyes make happy play;
Every morn is crisp with laughter,
Every evening glad with song,
No one fears the morning after
For you simply can’t go wrong.
Y'et amid enjoyment utter
And a world remote from care
One black flag is seen to flutter
Regularly on the air.
Joy the dregs of doom is scorning
In that bright and sunny land,
For on every Tuesday morning
Someone hangs the German land!
N.S.W.
